Dividing Lafz into Mufrad, Murakkab Naqis, and Murakkab Tamm
Author divides lafz into mufrad, murakkab naqis, and murakkab tamm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic expression as mufrad, murakkab tamm, or murakkab naqis based on the criteria of intentional parts and completeness.

Core Idea
The classification of words determines logical meaning: simple, compound complete, or incomplete.

Words are the building blocks of reasoning. Before analyzing arguments, a logician must know whether an expression is simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab), and if compound, whether it forms a complete statement. This lesson walks you through the three-way classification with subtle examples like proper names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as mufrad or murakkab
Check if any part of the expression is intended to indicate a meaning independently. If not, it is mufrad. For example, 'Isa' and 'insan' have parts that carry no intended meaning, so they are mufrad.
Proper names like 'Abd al-Malik' are also mufrad when used as names, not descriptions.
2
If murakkab, distinguish incomplete from complete
A compound is murakkab naqis if it does not form a complete idea that can stand alone, like 'fi al-dar' (in the house) or 'al-insan' (the man) without a predicate. It is murakkab tamm if it forms a full sentence, like 'Zayd yamshi' or 'al-natiq hayawan'.
Complete compounds can be from two nouns or a noun and a verb.
3
Apply the 'silence test'
If it is acceptable to be silent upon hearing the expression (i.e., it feels complete), it is murakkab tamm. If it leaves you waiting for more, it is murakkab naqis.
This test relies on natural language intuition.
4
Handle the special case of descriptive vs. proper names
If 'Abd al-Malik' is used as a proper name, it is mufrad because the parts are not intended to mean 'slave of the king'. But if it describes someone who is actually a slave, it becomes a descriptive compound (murakkab).
Neglecting this leads to errors in theoretical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الرابعة للفظ : قسمته من حيث إفراده وتركيبه : اعلم أن اللفظ ينقسم إلى مفرد ومركب (۱) ، والمركب ينقسم إلى مركب ناقص وإلى تام، فهي ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : هو المفرد وهو الذي لا يراد بالجزء منه دلالة على شيء أصلاً هو جزؤه، كقولك : عيسى إنسان فإن جزئي عيسى وهما عي وسا وجزئي إنسان وهما إن وسان ما يراد بشيء منهما للدلالة على شيء أصلاً. فإن قلت : فما قولك في عبد الملك؟ فاعلم أنه أيضاً مفرد إذا جعلته اسماً علماً كقولك زيد، وعند ذلك لا تريد بعبد دلالة على معنى ولا بالملك دلالة على معنى . فكل منهما من حيث هو جزؤه لا يدلّ على شيء، فيكونان كأجزاء اسم زيد وهما اسمان في الصورة جعلا اسماً واحداً كبعلبك ومعديكرب، فإن اتفق أن يكون المسمّى به عبداً للملك تحقيقاً فيكون هذا الإسم مطلقاً عليه من وجهين: أحدهما في تعريف صفته في عبودية الملك فيكون قولك عبد الملك وصفاً له فيكون مركباً لا مفرداً؛ فافهم هذه الدقائق فإن مثار الأغاليط في النظريات تنشأ من إهمالها . والمركب التام : هو الذي كلّ لفظ منه يدلّ على معنى، والمجموع يدل دلالة تامة بحيث يصح السكوت عليه فيكون من اسمين ويكون من اسم وفعل. والمنطقي يُسمّي الفعل كلمة والمركب الناقص بخلافه. فقولك : زيد يمشي والناطق حيوان مركب تام، وقولك : في الدار أو الإنسان؛ مركب ناقص لأنه مركب من اسم وأداة لا من اسمين ولا من اسم وفعل فإن مجرد قولك: «زيد في أو زيد لا لا يدلّ على المعنى الذي يراد الدلالة عليه في المحاورة ما لم يقل زيد في الدار أو زيد لا يظلم، فإنه بذلك الاقتران والتتميم يدل دلالة تامة بحيث يصح السكوت عليه .
The fourth division of the word: its division in terms of its singularity and composition: know that the word divides into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) (1), and the compound divides into incomplete compound (murakkab naqis) and complete (tamm), thus they are three divisions. The first: the simple, which is that whose part is not intended to indicate anything at all as its part, like your saying: 'Isa is a man' – for the parts of 'Isa, which are 'ay and sa, and the parts of 'insan, which are 'in and san, nothing from them is intended to indicate anything at all. If you say: what about your saying 'Abd al-Malik'? Know that it is also simple if you make it a proper name like 'Zayd', and then you do not intend by 'abd' an indication of a meaning nor by 'al-malik' an indication of a meaning. So each of them, in that it is a part of it, does not indicate anything, so they are like the parts of the name 'Zayd', though they are two nouns in form made into one name, like 'Ba'labak' and 'Ma'dikarib'. If it happens that the one named is actually a slave of the king (abd al-malik), then this name is applied to him in two ways: one in defining his description of servitude to the king, so your saying 'Abd al-Malik' is a description of him, and then it is compound, not simple. So understand these subtleties, for the source of errors in theoretical matters arises from neglecting them. The complete compound: it is that every word of it indicates a meaning, and the whole indicates a complete signification such that it is permissible to be silent upon it. It can be from two nouns or from a noun and a verb. The logician calls the verb a 'kalima', and the incomplete compound is unlike that. So your saying: 'Zayd walks' and 'The speaking animal' are complete compounds. And your saying: 'In the house' or 'The man' are incomplete compounds because they are composed of a noun and a particle, not from two nouns or from a noun and a verb. For merely your saying 'Zayd in' or 'Zayd not' does not indicate the meaning intended to be indicated in discourse until he says 'Zayd is in the house' or 'Zayd does not oppress', for by that conjunction and completion it indicates a complete signification such that it is permissible to be silent upon it.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a classification of words (alfadz) into simple and compound, with further subdivision of compound into incomplete and complete. The author begins by stating that the word is divided into mufrad and murakkab. Mufrad is defined as a unit whose parts are not intended to indicate anything independently. Examples: 'Isa' and 'insan' – their parts like 'ay' and 'sa' carry no meaning. Proper names like 'Abd al-Malik' are also mufrad if used as a proper name, despite being composed of two apparent words. However, if 'Abd al-Malik' describes someone who is actually a slave of the king, it becomes a compound (descriptive phrase). The author warns that neglecting these subtleties leads to errors. The complete compound (murakkab tamm) is a complete sentence where each word signifies something and the whole can stand alone, like 'Zayd walks' or 'al-natiq hayawan'. The incomplete compound (murakkab naqis) is like 'fi al-dar' or 'al-insan' – it needs additional completion to be a full statement. The logician uses 'kalima' for the verb. The passage emphasizes the importance of distinguishing these types for accurate reasoning.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب التام
المركب الناقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 'Fi al-dar'.

D) 'al-Insan'.

Key Takeaway
The classification of words into simple, compound complete, and compound incomplete is foundational for logical analysis and prevents errors in theoretical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Quranic expressions as mufrad, murakkab tamm, or murakkab naqis: 'Bismillahi' (In the name of Allah), 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is Greatest), 'fi al-jannati' (in the garden). Explain your reasoning using the criteria from the passage.
?

The Declarative Statement: Foundation of Logical Propositions
Author introduces the third art, defines types of compound meanings, and distinguishes declarative sentences for syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the declarative statement (khabar) from other types of compound expressions and explain why only declarative statements can serve as premises in demonstrations.

Core Idea
Only declarative statements admit truth and falsity; only they can form syllogistic premises.

In this lesson, we focus on the third art of logic: compound meanings. The author lists several types of compound expressions but singles out the declarative statement (khabar) as essential for logical reasoning. Why? Because only declarative statements can be true or false in themselves, making them the building blocks of demonstrations (barahin) and syllogisms. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly identifying propositions (qadaya) that can be used in rigorous argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of compound expressions
Read the list: interrogation, request, wish, expectation, wonder, and declarative statement. Note that each type has a different communicative function.
The author defines each in the footnotes—interrogation uses tools like 'hal' and 'man', wish expresses desire for the impossible, etc.
2
Extract the defining characteristic of the declarative statement
Find where the author defines khabar: 'that which is said to be truthful or false essentially'. This is the key differentia: truth-aptness.
The definition excludes accidental truth or falsehood, focusing on the statement's own nature.
3
Contrast with non-declarative types
Apply the definition to an example: an interrogative sentence like 'Is Zayd in the house?' cannot be called true or false in itself—it seeks information. The author also shows that a vocative like 'O Zayd' may imply a hidden declarative, but the utterance itself is not truth-apt.
Thus, the truth-aptness condition separates khabar from all other compound expressions.
4
Understand the scope of declarative statements
The author notes that even statements that are necessarily true (e.g., divine revelations) or necessarily false (e.g., claims of false prophets) are declarative because their truth value is inherent to the statement itself when considered without external factors.
This ensures that all possible premises for syllogisms are included under khabar, regardless of actual truth value.
5
Connect to the goal of logic
The author states that demonstrations are syllogisms composed of premises, each premise being a single declarative statement called a proposition (qadiyya). Therefore, the entire edifice of logical proof rests on the khabar.
Without this distinction, we could not know which linguistic units can function in reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث : في تركيب المعاني المفردة، أو القضايا

اعلم أن المعاني إذا ركبت حصل منها أصناف كالاستفهام (۱) والالتماس والتمنّي ) والترجي (٤) والتعجب والخبر . وغرضنا من جملة ذلك الصنف الأخير وهو الخبر، لأن مطلبنا البراهين المرشدة إلى العلوم وهي نوع من القياس المركب من المقدمات التي كلّ مقدمة منها خبر واحد يُسمّى قضية. والخبر هو الذي يقال لقائله : إنه صادق أو كاذب فيه بالذات لا بالعَرَض، وبه يحصل الاحتراز عن سائر الأقسام إذ المستفهم عمّا يعلمه قد يقال له لا تكذب، فإنه يعرض به إلى التباس الأمر عليه . وكذلك من يقول : يا زيد ويريد غيره لأنه يعتقد أن زيداً في الدار، فإذا قيل له لا تكذب لم يكن ذلك تكذيباً في النداء بل في خبر اندرج تحت النداء ضمناً، فإذاً نَظرُنا في هذا الفنّ في القضية وبيانها، بذكر أحكامها : وأقسامها .

(۱) الاستفهام: هو طلب فهم شيء لم يتقدم لك علم به بأداة من إحدى أدواته (هل، من، متی، ...).
(۲) الالتماس : الطَّلَبُ ؛ يقال : التمس الشيء: طلبه.
(۳) التمني : هو طلب حصول شيء محبوب لا يُرجى حصوله إما لكونه مستحيلاً، وإما لكونه بعيد التحقق والحصول.
(٤) الترجي : هو طلب الممكن المرغوب فيه، أو توقع الأمر المحبوب، وغير المحبوب، وأهم أدواته : لَعَلَّ، عَسَى .
(٥) التَّعجبُ : هو صيغة للتعبير عن الدهشة والانفعالات إزاء أمرٍ ما بواحد من شكليه : ما أكرم فلان، أو أكرم به .
(٦) الخبر : هو ما احتمل الصدق والكذب لذاته، والقول لذاته لتدخل فيه الأخبار الواجبة الصدق كأخبار الله سبحانه وتعالى، وأخبار رسله ؛ والواجبة الكذب كأخبار المتنبئين في دعوى النبوة، والبديهيات المقطوع بصدقها أو كذبها فكل هذه إذا نظر إليها لذاتها من دون اعتبارات أخرى احتملت أحد الأمرين؛ أما إذا نُظر فيها إلى خصوصية في المُخبر أو في الخبر، تكون متعينة لأحدهما .
The third art: On the composition of simple meanings, or propositions.

Know that when meanings are composed, several kinds arise from them, such as interrogation (1), request, wish, expectation, wonder, and declarative statement. Our purpose among all of these is the last kind, namely the declarative statement, because our goal is demonstrations that guide to the sciences, and they are a type of syllogism composed of premises, each premise being a single declarative statement called a proposition. The declarative statement is that of which the speaker is said to be truthful or false in it essentially, not accidentally. By this, avoidance of the other divisions is achieved, for the one who questions about what he knows may be told 'do not lie,' because it leads to confusion for him. Similarly, one who says 'O Zayd' but means someone else because he believes that Zayd is in the house, when it is said to him 'do not lie,' that is not a falsehood in the call but rather in a declarative statement implicitly contained within the call. Therefore, our consideration in this art is the proposition and its elucidation, by mentioning its rulings and its divisions.

(1) Interrogation: it is requesting the understanding of something that you have no prior knowledge of, by means of one of its tools (هل, من, متى, ...).
(2) Request: it is asking; it is said: 'talaba al-shay'a: he sought it.'
(3) Wish: it is requesting the occurrence of a beloved thing that is not hoped for, either because it is impossible or because it is far from being realized and obtained.
(4) Expectation: it is requesting the possible desired thing, or anticipating a beloved or disliked matter; its most important tools are لعل and عسى.
(5) Wonder: it is a form for expressing astonishment and reactions to something by one of its two forms: 'ما أكرم فلان' or 'أكرم به'.
(6) Declarative statement: it is that which admits truth and falsity in itself, and the statement in itself, to include statements that are necessarily true, like the statements of Allah the Exalted and His messengers, and those necessarily false, like the statements of false prophets in claiming prophethood, and self-evident statements that are certainly true or false; all of these, when considered in themselves without other considerations, admit one of the two; but when considered with respect to a specificity in the informant or the information, they become determined to one of them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage introduces the third art of logic, dealing with compound expressions, specifically propositions (qadaya). The author first enumerates the types of compound utterances: interrogation, request, wish, expectation, wonder, and declarative statement. Of these, the declarative statement (khabar) is singled out because it is the foundation of demonstrations (barahin) and syllogisms. A declarative statement is defined as that which can be essentially described as true or false. This distinguishes it from other types: for example, an interrogative sentence like 'Is Zayd in the house?' cannot be called true or false in itself; rather, one who questions may be told not to lie because of an implied underlying claim. Similarly, a vocative like 'O Zayd' when Zayd is not present contains an implicit declarative statement about Zayd's presence. The author then provides detailed definitions in the footnotes for each type, with special attention to the declarative statement, noting that even statements that are necessarily true or false (like divine revelation or false prophecies) are considered declarative in themselves because their truth value is attached to their content, not to an external factor. This discussion is crucial for understanding the building blocks of logical arguments.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar): declarative statement that essentially admits truth and falsity
القضية (al-qadiyya): a proposition; a declarative statement used as a premise in a syllogism
الاستفهام (al-istifham): interrogation, requesting understanding using tools like هل, من, متى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because they alone can be premises in demonstrations and syllogisms
C) Because they are easier to analyze
D) Because they are the only type of compound meaning

Key Takeaway
The declarative statement is uniquely defined by its essential truth-aptness, making it the only suitable building block for logical demonstrations and syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Identify which of the following Arabic sentences is a declarative statement (khabar) and justify your answer using the definition from the lesson: 'هل تعلمون أن الصلاة فريضة؟' (Do you know that prayer is an obligation?) vs. 'الصلاة فريضة' (Prayer is an obligation).
?

Structure of a Proposition: Subject and Predicate
Author explains the subject-predicate structure of a categorical proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawḍūʿ) and predicate (maḥmūl) in any Arabic categorical proposition from an Islamic text.

Core Idea
Every proposition splits into subject and predicate — the foundation of all logical judgment.

Before you can evaluate arguments, you must dissect the building blocks: propositions. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize the two essential parts of a categorical proposition — subject and predicate — using examples from the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the declarative sentence
Find the sentence that makes a claim about something. It will have a subject and a predicate connected by a copula (often implied).
In Arabic, the copula is often omitted, e.g., 'العالم حادث' (The world [is] originated).
2
Identify the subject (mawḍūʿ)
The subject is the thing being talked about. Ask: 'What or who is this sentence about?' The answer is the subject.
In 'Zayd is standing' (زيد قائم), the subject is Zayd.
3
Identify the predicate (maḥmūl)
The predicate is what is affirmed or denied of the subject. Ask: 'What is said about the subject?'
In 'Zayd is standing', the predicate is 'standing'.
4
Check for negation
If the proposition includes a negation particle (like 'laysa' or 'mā'), the predicate is negated. The structure remains: subject + negation + predicate.
E.g., 'العالم ليس بحادث' (The world is not originated) — subject: 'العالم', predicate: 'حادث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الأولى : إن القضيّة باعتبار ذاتها تنقسم إلى جزأين مفردين: أحدهما خبر، والآخر مُخبَر عنه ، كقولك : زيد ،قائم، فإن زيداً مُخبَر عنه والقائم خبر، وكقولك : العالم ،حادث، فالعالم مُخبَر عنه والحادث خبر. وقد جرت عادة المنطقيين بتسمية الخبر محمولاً والمُخبَر عنه موضوعاً (۱) ، فلننزل على اصطلاحهم فلا مشاحة في الألفاظ . ثم إذا قلنا : الشكل محمول على المثلث، فإن كلّ مثلث شكل فلسنا نعني به أن حقيقة المثلث حقيقة الشكل، ولكن معناه أن الشيء الذي يقال له مثلث فهو بعينه يقال له شكل، سواء كان حقيقة ذلك الشيء كونه مثلثاً أو كونه شكلاً أو كونه أمراً ثالثاً فإنّا إذا أشرنا إلى إنسان وقلنا : هذا الأبيض طويل، فحقيقة المشار إليه كونه إنساناً لا هذا الموضوع وهو الأبيض ولا هذا المحمول وهو الطويل. وإذا قلنا هذا الإنسان أبيض، فالموضوع هو الحقيقة. فإذن لسنا نعني بالمحمول إلا القدر الذي ذكرناه من غير اشتراط، فلنفهم حقيقته فهذا أقلّ ما تنقسم إليه القضية الحملية والقضايا باعتبار وجوه تركيبها ثلاثة أصناف : الأول: الحملي، وهو الذي حكم فيه بأنه معنى محمول على معنى أو ليس بمحمول عليه كقولنا : العالم حادث - العالم ليس بحادث؛ فالعالم موضوع والحادث محمول يسلب مرة ويثبت أخرى. وقولنا ليس هو حرف سلب، إذا زيد على مجرد ذكر ذات الموضوع والمحمول صار المحمول مسلوباً عن الموضوع. الصنف الثاني : ما يُسمّى شرطياً متصلاً كقولنا: إن كان العالم حادثاً فله ،محدث، سُمّي شرطياً لأنه شرط وجود المقدم لوجود التالي بكلمة الشرط، وهو إن وإذا وما يقوم مقامهما فقولنا : إن كان العالم حادثاً يُسمّى مقدماً، وقولنا : فله . محدث، يُسمّى تالياً، وهو الذي قرن به حرف الجزاء الموازي للشرط. والتالي يجري مجرى المحمول ولكن يفارقه من وجه، وهو أن المحمول ربما يرجع في الحقيقة إلى نفس الموضوع، ولا يكون شيئاً مفارقاً له ولا متصلاً به على سبيل اللزوم والتبعية، كقولنا : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان محمول وليس مفارقاً، ولا ملازماً تابعاً. وأما قولنا فله محدث، فهو شيء آخر لزم اتصاله وإقرانه بوصف الحدوث، لا أنه يرجع إلى نفس العالم . والشرطية المتصلة إذا حللتها رجعت بعد
The first division: The proposition, considered in itself, divides into two simple parts: one is the predicate (khabar) and the other is the subject (mukhbar 'anhu). For example, 'Zayd is standing': Zayd is the subject and 'standing' is the predicate. And 'The world is originated': The world is the subject and 'originated' is the predicate. The logicians customarily call the predicate 'mahmul' and the subject 'mawdu'. So let us follow their convention — there is no disputing about words. Then if we say 'Shape is predicated of triangle', since every triangle is a shape, we do not mean that the reality of triangle is the reality of shape, but its meaning is that the thing called triangle is also called shape, whether the reality of that thing is being triangular or being a shape or being a third thing. For if we point to a human and say 'This white is tall', the reality of the thing pointed to is being human, not this subject (white) nor this predicate (tall). And if we say 'This human is white', the subject is the reality. So we do not mean by predicate except the capacity we mentioned without condition. So understand its reality. This is the least (division). The proposition, according to the modes of its composition, is of three kinds: First: categorical, in which it is judged that a meaning is predicated of a meaning or not predicated, as we say 'The world is originated' — the world is subject, originated is predicate, sometimes negated and sometimes affirmed. And our saying 'is not' is a particle of negation; when added to the mere mention of the subject and predicate, the predicate becomes negated of the subject. The second kind: what is called conditional conjunctive, like our saying 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator'. It is called conditional because the existence of the antecedent is a condition for the existence of the consequent by the particle of condition, such as 'in', 'idha', and what stands in their place. Our saying 'If the world is originated' is called antecedent (muqaddam), and our saying 'then it has a creator' is called consequent (tali), which is connected to the particle of the answer corresponding to the condition. The consequent is similar to the predicate but differs in one respect: the predicate might in reality refer back to the subject itself and is not separate from it nor connected to it in the manner of entailment and dependence, as in 'Human is an animal' — animal is a predicate but not separate nor a dependent concomitant. But our saying 'then it has a creator' is something else that is necessarily connected and associated with the description of origination, not referring back to the world itself. And the conditional conjunctive, if you analyze it, returns after...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions into three types based on their composition. The author first explains the internal structure of a proposition: every proposition consists of a subject (mukhbar 'anhu, also called mawdu') and a predicate (khabar, also called mahmul). He gives examples like 'Zayd is standing' and 'The world is originated'. He clarifies that predication does not imply identity of realities; for instance, 'shape is predicated of triangle' does not mean that the essence of triangle is shape, but that the same thing called triangle is also called shape. Similarly, when we say 'this white is tall', the underlying reality is the human, not the whiteness or tallness. Then the author introduces the three kinds of propositions: 1) Categorical (hamli), where a predicate is affirmed or denied of a subject, e.g., 'The world is originated' or 'The world is not originated'. 2) Conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), which has an antecedent and a consequent linked by a conditional particle like 'in' (if) or 'idha' (when). The antecedent is the condition, and the consequent is entailed by it. The author distinguishes the consequent from a predicate: the predicate of a categorical proposition often returns to the subject itself (like 'animal' is a constitutive part of 'human'), whereas the consequent in a conditional is something separate that is necessarily connected to the antecedent (like 'having a creator' follows from 'the world is originated'). The passage ends abruptly while discussing the conditional conjunctive; it likely continues to explain the third kind (conditional disjunctive) on the next page.

Types of Propositions: Categorical and Conjunctive Conditional
Author classifies propositions into three kinds and details the conditional conjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional conjunctive proposition and identify their parts (subject/predicate vs. antecedent/consequent).

Core Idea
Categoricals predicate a term of a subject; conditionals link two statements with a condition.

Not all propositions are of the simple 'A is B' form. Some are conditional: 'If A then B'. This lesson shows you how to spot the difference and correctly label the parts of each type, using examples from Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Check for a conditional particle
Look for words like 'in' (if), 'idhā' (when), or 'law' (if) at the beginning. If present, the proposition is likely conditional.
The passage uses 'إن' (in) as the particle in 'إن كان العالم حادثاً فله محدث'.
2
If no conditional particle, it is categorical
If the proposition simply has a subject and predicate with no conditional link, it is categorical (ḥamliyya).
E.g., 'العالم حادث' — no 'if', so categorical.
3
For categorical: identify subject and predicate
The subject is what the sentence is about; the predicate is what is affirmed/denied. Use the skills from Lesson 1.
4
For conditional conjunctive: locate antecedent and consequent
The part after 'if' is the antecedent (muqaddam); the part after 'then' is the consequent (tālin).
In 'إن كان العالم حادثاً فله محدث', antecedent = 'العالم حادث', consequent = 'له محدث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الأولى : إن القضيّة باعتبار ذاتها تنقسم إلى جزأين مفردين: أحدهما خبر، والآخر مُخبَر عنه ، كقولك : زيد ،قائم، فإن زيداً مُخبَر عنه والقائم خبر، وكقولك : العالم ،حادث، فالعالم مُخبَر عنه والحادث خبر. وقد جرت عادة المنطقيين بتسمية الخبر محمولاً والمُخبَر عنه موضوعاً (۱) ، فلننزل على اصطلاحهم فلا مشاحة في الألفاظ . ثم إذا قلنا : الشكل محمول على المثلث، فإن كلّ مثلث شكل فلسنا نعني به أن حقيقة المثلث حقيقة الشكل، ولكن معناه أن الشيء الذي يقال له مثلث فهو بعينه يقال له شكل، سواء كان حقيقة ذلك الشيء كونه مثلثاً أو كونه شكلاً أو كونه أمراً ثالثاً فإنّا إذا أشرنا إلى إنسان وقلنا : هذا الأبيض طويل، فحقيقة المشار إليه كونه إنساناً لا هذا الموضوع وهو الأبيض ولا هذا المحمول وهو الطويل. وإذا قلنا هذا الإنسان أبيض، فالموضوع هو الحقيقة. فإذن لسنا نعني بالمحمول إلا القدر الذي ذكرناه من غير اشتراط، فلنفهم حقيقته فهذا أقلّ ما تنقسم إليه القضية الحملية والقضايا باعتبار وجوه تركيبها ثلاثة أصناف : الأول: الحملي، وهو الذي حكم فيه بأنه معنى محمول على معنى أو ليس بمحمول عليه كقولنا : العالم حادث - العالم ليس بحادث؛ فالعالم موضوع والحادث محمول يسلب مرة ويثبت أخرى. وقولنا ليس هو حرف سلب، إذا زيد على مجرد ذكر ذات الموضوع والمحمول صار المحمول مسلوباً عن الموضوع. الصنف الثاني : ما يُسمّى شرطياً متصلاً كقولنا: إن كان العالم حادثاً فله ،محدث، سُمّي شرطياً لأنه شرط وجود المقدم لوجود التالي بكلمة الشرط، وهو إن وإذا وما يقوم مقامهما فقولنا : إن كان العالم حادثاً يُسمّى مقدماً، وقولنا : فله . محدث، يُسمّى تالياً، وهو الذي قرن به حرف الجزاء الموازي للشرط. والتالي يجري مجرى المحمول ولكن يفارقه من وجه، وهو أن المحمول ربما يرجع في الحقيقة إلى نفس الموضوع، ولا يكون شيئاً مفارقاً له ولا متصلاً به على سبيل اللزوم والتبعية، كقولنا : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان محمول وليس مفارقاً، ولا ملازماً تابعاً. وأما قولنا فله محدث، فهو شيء آخر لزم اتصاله وإقرانه بوصف الحدوث، لا أنه يرجع إلى نفس العالم . والشرطية المتصلة إذا حللتها رجعت بعد
The first division: The proposition, considered in itself, divides into two simple parts: one is the predicate (khabar) and the other is the subject (mukhbar 'anhu). For example, 'Zayd is standing': Zayd is the subject and 'standing' is the predicate. And 'The world is originated': The world is the subject and 'originated' is the predicate. The logicians customarily call the predicate 'mahmul' and the subject 'mawdu'. So let us follow their convention — there is no disputing about words. Then if we say 'Shape is predicated of triangle', since every triangle is a shape, we do not mean that the reality of triangle is the reality of shape, but its meaning is that the thing called triangle is also called shape, whether the reality of that thing is being triangular or being a shape or being a third thing. For if we point to a human and say 'This white is tall', the reality of the thing pointed to is being human, not this subject (white) nor this predicate (tall). And if we say 'This human is white', the subject is the reality. So we do not mean by predicate except the capacity we mentioned without condition. So understand its reality. This is the least (division). The proposition, according to the modes of its composition, is of three kinds: First: categorical, in which it is judged that a meaning is predicated of a meaning or not predicated, as we say 'The world is originated' — the world is subject, originated is predicate, sometimes negated and sometimes affirmed. And our saying 'is not' is a particle of negation; when added to the mere mention of the subject and predicate, the predicate becomes negated of the subject. The second kind: what is called conditional conjunctive, like our saying 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator'. It is called conditional because the existence of the antecedent is a condition for the existence of the consequent by the particle of condition, such as 'in', 'idha', and what stands in their place. Our saying 'If the world is originated' is called antecedent (muqaddam), and our saying 'then it has a creator' is called consequent (tali), which is connected to the particle of the answer corresponding to the condition. The consequent is similar to the predicate but differs in one respect: the predicate might in reality refer back to the subject itself and is not separate from it nor connected to it in the manner of entailment and dependence, as in 'Human is an animal' — animal is a predicate but not separate nor a dependent concomitant. But our saying 'then it has a creator' is something else that is necessarily connected and associated with the description of origination, not referring back to the world itself. The Categorical Proposition: Subject and Predicate
Author defines the categorical proposition, its subject and predicate, and clarifies predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in any categorical proposition and explain what predication means.

Core Idea
Predication does not require identity of essence; it is a linguistic relation of description.

Every categorical proposition has two essential parts: the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul). But what does it mean to predicate one term of another? In this passage, the author defines these parts using examples from kalam and clarifies that predication is not about identity of realities—a crucial point to avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In the examples, 'Zaid' and 'world' are subjects; 'standing' and 'originated' are predicates. Subject is what we speak about, predicate is what we affirm or deny of it.
2
Recognize terminological differences
Logicians say mawdu' and mahmul; rhetoricians say musnad and musnad ilayhi; grammarians say mubtada' and khabar. The logical relation is the same despite different terms.
No quarrel over terms, as the author notes.
3
Understand predication as description, not identity
When we say 'shape is predicated of triangle', we do not mean triangle's reality is shape's reality. The thing called triangle is also called shape, regardless of essence.
The author illustrates with 'this white thing is tall'—the pointed-to is a man, not whiteness.
4
Apply the clarification to avoid misinterpretation
In 'this man is white', the subject is the human reality. Predication does not imply the subject's essence is the predicate's essence.
This prevents confusing predication with definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الأولى : إن القضيّة باعتبار ذاتها تنقسم إلى جزأين مفردين: أحدهما خبر، والآخر مُخبَر عنه ، كقولك : زيد قائم، فإن زيداً مُخبَر عنه والقائم خبر، وكقولك : العالم حادث، فالعالم مُخبَر عنه والحادث خبر. وقد جرت عادة المنطقيين بتسمية الخبر محمولاً والمُخبَر عنه موضوعاً (۱) يسمي البلاغيون الخبر (المحمول): مسنداً، والمخبر عنه (الموضوع): مسنداً إليه. ويسمي النحويون الموضوع مبتدأ، والمحمول خبراً. فلننزل على اصطلاحهم فلا مشاحة في الألفاظ . ثم إذا قلنا : الشكل محمول على المثلث، فإن كلّ مثلث شكل فلسنا نعني به أن حقيقة المثلث حقيقة الشكل، ولكن معناه أن الشيء الذي يقال له مثلث فهو بعينه يقال له شكل، سواء كان حقيقة ذلك الشيء كونه مثلثاً أو كونه شكلاً أو كونه أمراً ثالثاً فإنّا إذا أشرنا إلى إنسان وقلنا : هذا الأبيض طويل، فحقيقة المشار إليه كونه إنساناً لا هذا الموضوع وهو الأبيض ولا هذا المحمول وهو الطويل. وإذا قلنا هذا الإنسان أبيض، فالموضوع هو الحقيقة. فإذن لسنا نعني بالمحمول إلا القدر الذي ذكرناه من غير اشتراط، فلنفهم حقيقته فهذا أقلّ ما تنقسم إليه القضية الحملية
The first division: Indeed the proposition, in terms of its essence, divides into two simple parts: one of them is a predicate (khabar) and the other is the subject (mukhbar 'anhu), as in your saying: Zaid is standing, for Zaid is the subject and 'standing' is the predicate; and as in your saying: The world is originated, so the world is the subject and 'originated' is the predicate. And the logicians have customarily named the predicate (khabar) as 'mahmul' and the subject (mukhbar 'anhu) as 'mawdu'' (1) The rhetoricians name the predicate (mahmul) as 'musnad', and the subject (mawdu') as 'musnad ilayhi'. And the grammarians name the subject as 'mubtada'' and the predicate as 'khabar'. So let us follow their convention, for there is no quarreling over terms. Then if we say: Shape is predicated of triangle, for every triangle is a shape, we do not mean by it that the reality of triangle is the reality of shape, but its meaning is that the thing which is called triangle is itself called shape, whether the reality of that thing is its being a triangle or its being a shape or its being a third thing; for if we point to a man and say: This white thing is tall, the reality of the pointed-to is its being a man, not this subject which is 'white', nor this predicate which is 'tall'. And if we say: This man is white, then the subject is the reality. Thus we do not mean by 'predicate' except the capacity we mentioned without condition. So understand its reality. This is the least to which the categorical proposition divides.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) and its two essential parts: the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul). Author begins by noting that every proposition, considered in itself, consists of a subject (mukhbar 'anhu or mawdu') and a predicate (khabar or mahmul). Examples from Islamic theology are given: 'Zaid is standing' and 'The world is originated (hadith)'—a classic kalam example. The logicians' terminology is adopted, with a footnote noting that rhetoricians and grammarians use different terms (musnad/musnad ilayhi and mubtada'/khabar respectively). Author emphasizes that there is no dispute over terms; it is merely conventional. Then a crucial clarification: when we say 'Shape is predicated of triangle', we do not mean the reality (haqiqa) of triangle is the reality of shape. Rather, predication means the thing called triangle is also called shape, regardless of the essence. Author illustrates by pointing to a man and saying 'This white thing is tall'—the reality of the pointed-to is man, not 'white' or 'tall'. If we say 'This man is white', the subject is the human reality. Therefore, predication does not entail identity of essence; it only affirms that the subject is described by the predicate. This guards against a common misunderstanding: confusing is predicated of with is identical to.

Conditional Conjunctive Proposition: Structure and Reduction
Defines the conditional conjunctive proposition and its reduction to categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in any conditional conjunctive proposition and explain how it reduces to two categorical propositions.

Core Idea
The conditional conjunctive is a compound proposition that hides two categoricals within it.

This lesson introduces the second type of proposition in Islamic logic: the conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila). You will learn its parts—the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali)—and how they differ from a simple subject-predicate structure. Most importantly, you will see how this proposition decomposes into two categorical propositions, making it more complex than it first appears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional particle
Look for words like 'in' (if) or 'idha' (when) that introduce the antecedent. The presence of such a particle is what makes the proposition conditional.
In the Arabic text, the particles 'in' and 'idha' are mentioned as examples.
2
Separate antecedent and consequent
The clause after the conditional particle is the antecedent; the clause after the consequent particle (jaza') is the consequent. In 'If the world is originated, then it has an originator', the antecedent is 'the world is originated' and the consequent is 'it has an originator'.
3
Distinguish consequent from categorical predicate
Note that the consequent does not refer back to the subject of the antecedent—it is an external implication. Compare with a categorical predicate like 'animal' in 'Man is an animal', which is part of the essence.
The text stresses this difference to avoid confusion.
4
Reduce the conditional to categoricals
Remove the conditional and consequent particles. The two clauses become two independent categorical propositions: 'The world is originated' and 'The world has an originator'. Each then decomposes into a subject and a predicate.
This two-step reduction shows the conditional is more composite than a categorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثاني : ما يُسمّى شرطياً متصلاً كقولنا: إن كان العالم حادثاً فله محدث، سُمّي شرطياً لأنه شرط وجود المقدم لوجود التالي بكلمة الشرط، وهو إن وإذا وما يقوم مقامهما فقولنا : إن كان العالم حادثاً يُسمّى مقدماً، وقولنا : فله محدث، يُسمّى تالياً، وهو الذي قرن به حرف الجزاء الموازي للشرط. والتالي يجري مجرى المحمول ولكن يفارقه من وجه، وهو أن المحمول ربما يرجع في الحقيقة إلى نفس الموضوع، ولا يكون شيئاً مفارقاً له ولا متصلاً به على سبيل اللزوم والتبعية، كقولنا : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان محمول وليس مفارقاً، ولا ملازماً تابعاً. وأما قولنا فله محدث، فهو شيء آخر لزم اتصاله وإقرانه بوصف الحدوث، لا أنه يرجع إلى نفس العالم . والشرطية المتصلة إذا حللتها رجعت بعد حذف حرفي الجزاء والشرط منها إلى حمليتين، ثم ترجع كل حملية إلى محمول مفرد وموضوع مفرد فالشرطية أكثر تركيباً لا محالة إذ لا تنحل في أول الأمر إلى البسائط، بل تنحل إلى الحمليات أولاً ثم إلى البسائط ثانياً .
The second type: what is called conditional conjunctive, as in our saying: If the world is originated, then it has an originator. It is called conditional because the existence of the antecedent is a condition for the existence of the consequent by the conditional particle, which is 'in' (if), 'idha' (when), and what stands in their place. So our saying: 'If the world is originated' is called the antecedent (muqaddam), and our saying: 'then it has an originator' is called the consequent (tali), and it is linked with the particle of consequent (jaz') that parallels the condition. And the consequent runs in the manner of the predicate [in categorical propositions], but it differs from it in one respect: that the predicate sometimes in reality refers back to the subject itself, and is not something separate from it nor connected to it in the mode of necessary implication and dependency, as in our saying: Man is an animal; 'animal' is a predicate and is not separate nor necessarily implying and dependent. As for our saying: 'then it has an originator', it is something else whose connection and coupling with the attribute of origination is necessary, not that it refers back to the world itself. And the conditional conjunctive proposition, if you analyze it, after removing the two particles of condition and consequent, returns to two categorical propositions, then each categorical proposition returns to a single predicate and a single subject. So the conditional proposition is necessarily more composite, since it does not, in the first instance, decompose into simples, but it first decomposes into categoricals, then secondarily into simples.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional conjunctive proposition (shartiyya muttasila) and explains its structure. Author begins by giving an example from kalam: 'If the world is originated, then it has an originator' (a standard argument for God's existence). The proposition is called 'conditional' because the antecedent (muqaddam) is a condition for the consequent (tali). The conditional particle (e.g., 'in' or 'idha') links them, and the consequent is introduced by the particle 'fa' (jaz'). Author then compares the consequent to a predicate in categorical propositions, noting a key difference: a categorical predicate may be inherent to the subject (e.g., 'man is animal' where 'animal' is part of the essence of man), whereas the consequent is an external necessary implication—it does not refer back to the antecedent's essence but is a separate thing that necessarily accompanies the antecedent. In the example, the world's being originated necessarily implies it has an originator, but 'has an originator' is not part of the world's essence. Finally, author explains the reduction (tahlil) of a conditional conjunctive to its simpler components: by removing the conditional and consequent particles, the proposition becomes two categoricals. Each categorical then reduces to a simple subject and predicate. Thus the conditional is more composite than a categorical, requiring a two-step reduction to the simplest elements. This is a foundational analysis for understanding propositional complexity in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة (shartiyya muttasila): A conditional conjunctive proposition consisting of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali) linked by a conditional particle such that the antecedent is a condition for the consequent. [Note: Stable term; used consistently by all major logicians.]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent clause in a conditional proposition, introduced by a conditional particle like 'in' or 'idha'. [Note: In logic, always the 'if' part; in usul al-fiqh, it has a different meaning (a premise).]
تال (tali): The consequent clause, which follows from the antecedent and is linked by the particle 'fa'. [Note: Stable in logic.]
شرط (shart): The conditional particle (e.g., 'in', 'idha') that makes the antecedent a condition for the consequent. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, shart means a legal condition (e.g., ablution for prayer).]
جزاء (jaza'): The particle of consequent (usually 'fa') that introduces the consequent and parallels the conditional particle. [Note: Also used in grammar for the result clause of a conditional sentence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is simpler because it has only one part.
B) It is more composite because it first reduces to two categoricals before reaching simples.
C) It is the same complexity as a categorical.
D) It does not involve predication at all.

Key Takeaway
A conditional conjunctive proposition is more complex than a categorical because it contains two implicit categoricals within its structure.

Three Subtypes of Conditional Disjunctive Propositions
Author defines the conditional disjunctive proposition, its types by number of parts, and its three subtypes based on conjunction and exclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three subtypes of conditional disjunctive propositions and determine which subtype is used in a given theological or legal example.

Core Idea
A disjunctive proposition either prevents conjunction, exclusion, or both.

In this passage, the author introduces the conditional disjunctive proposition (al-shartiyya al-munfasila) and distinguishes three subtypes based on whether they prevent conjunction (jam') and/or exclusion (khuluw). These distinctions are not mere academic curiosities; they are essential tools for probing and dividing possibilities in usul al-fiqh and kalam, known as sabr wa taqsim. Understanding these subtypes will sharpen your ability to analyze dichotomies in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the definition of the conditional disjunctive proposition: two categorical propositions combined such that one is necessarily separate from the other. Note the contrast with the conditional conjunctive.
The author first defines the general concept.
2
Understand the two-part vs multiple-part distinction
Notice that disjunctives may be confined to two parts (e.g., world is either originated or eternal) or have three or more parts (e.g., number is equal, less, or greater). Some are not confined (e.g., black or white, because there are other colors).
This sets the stage for the three subtypes.
3
Learn the first subtype: man' al-jam' wa al-khuluw
This prevents both conjunction and exclusion. Exactly one alternative must be true. The example 'The world is either originated or eternal' illustrates this: both cannot be true, and both cannot be false.
This is the strongest form of disjunction.
4
Learn the second subtype: man' al-jam' duna al-khuluw
This prevents conjunction but allows exclusion. Both cannot be true, but both can be false. The example 'This is either an animal or a tree' shows this: it can be neither (e.g., a donkey).
This is a weaker disjunction.
5
Learn the third subtype: man' al-khuluw duna al-jam'
This prevents exclusion but allows conjunction. Both cannot be false, but both can be true. The example 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he does not drown' is explained: the second part is a necessary implication (lazim) of being on land, but it is more general and can also apply to being in the sea, allowing both to be true.
This is the most subtle subtype; note the substitution of a part with its lazim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : ما يُسمّى شرطياً منفصلاً كقولنا : العالم إما حادث وإما قديم فهما قضيتان حمليتان جمعتا وجعلت إحداهما لازمة الانفصال للأخرى، وكانت فيما قبل الشرطي المتصل) لازمة الاتصال ولأجله سُمّي منفصلاً. والمتكلمون يُسمّون هذا سبراً وتقسيماً . ثم هذا المنفصل قد يكون محصوراً في جزأين كما ذكرنا، وقد يكون في ثلاثة أو أكثر كقولنا هذا العدد إما مثل هذا العدد أو أقل أو أكثر، فهو مع كونه ذا ثلاثة محصور. وربما تكثر الأجزاء بحيث لا يكون داخلاً في الحصر كقولنا : هذا إما أسود أو أبيض، وفلان إما بمكة أو ببغداد. ثم ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : ما يمنع الجمع والخلق جميعاً كقولنا : العالم إما حادث أو قديم، فإنه يمنع اجتماع القدم والحدوث والخلو من أحدهما، أي لا يجوز كلاهما ويجب أحدهما لا محالة . والثاني : ما يمنع الجمع دون الخلوّ كما إذا قال قائل : هذا حيوان وشجر، فتقول : هو إما حيوان وإما شجر أي لا يجتمعان جميعاً، وإن جاز أن يخلو عنهما بأن يكون حماراً مثلاً . والثالث : ما يمنع الخلق ولا يمنع الجمع كما إذا أخذت بدل أحد الجزأين لازمه لا نفسه بأن قُلت مثلاً: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما ألا يغرق، فإن هذا يمنع الخلق ولا يمنع الجمع، إذ يجوز أن يكون في البحر ولا يغرق، ولا يجوز أن أحد القسمين؛ وسببه أنك أخذت نفي الغرق الذي هو لازم كونه في البرّ وهو أعم منه، فإن الذي في البحر أيضاً قد لا يغرق وكان أصل التقسيم يقتضي أن يقال : إما أن يكون في البحر وإما أن يكون في البرّ، فكان يمتنع به الجمع والخلوّ جميعاً، ولكن عدم الغرق لازم لكونه في البرّ ثم ليس مساوياً بل هو أعمّ فلم يبعد أن يتناول كونه في البحر فيؤدي إلى الاجتماع. فهذه أمور متشابهة لا بدّ من تحقيق الفرق بينها . فلا معنى لنظر العقل إلا درك انقسام الأمور المتشابهة في الظاهر، ودرك اجتماع الأمور المفترقة في الظاهر، فإن الأشياء تختلف في أمور وتشترك في أمور ، وإنما شأن العقل أن يميّز بين ما يشترك فيه وما يفترق فيه، وذلك بهذه التقسيمات التي نحن في سياقها، فهذا وجه قسمة القضايا باعتبار
The third type: what is called conditional disjunctive, as in our saying: The world is either originated or eternal. They are two categorical propositions that are combined and one of them is made necessarily separate from the other, while previously, in the conditional conjunctive, it was necessarily connected. For this reason it is called 'disjunctive'. The theologians call this 'sabr and taqsim' (probing and dividing). Then this disjunctive may be confined to two parts as we mentioned, or it may be in three or more, as in our saying: This number is either equal to this number or less or greater; it is, while being three, confined. And sometimes the parts may become numerous so that it is not included in confinement, as in our saying: This is either black or white, and so-and-so is either in Mecca or Baghdad. Then it divides into three divisions: The first: that which prevents both conjunction and exclusion together, as in our saying: The world is either originated or eternal; for it prevents the combination of preeternity and origination and the absence of both, i.e., both are not permissible and one of them is necessarily necessary. The second: that which prevents conjunction but not exclusion, as when someone says: This is an animal and a tree? Then you say: It is either an animal or a tree, i.e., they do not both come together, although it is possible to be free of both, for example by being a donkey. The third: that which prevents exclusion but does not prevent conjunction, as when you take, in place of one of the two parts, its necessary implication (lazim) not itself, as for example you say: Either Zaid is in the sea or he does not drown. For this prevents exclusion but does not prevent conjunction, since it is possible for him to be in the sea and not drown, and it is not possible that neither of the two parts [is true]? And its cause is that you took the negation of drowning which is a necessary implication of his being on land, and it is more general than it, for the one in the sea may also not drown. And the original division would require that it be said: Either he is in the sea or he is on land, so both conjunction and exclusion would be prevented. But non-drowning is a necessary implication of being on land, then it is not equal but more general, so it is not far-fetched that it may include his being in the sea, leading to [both] coming together. So these are similar matters; it is necessary to achieve the distinction between them. For the meaning of the intellect's consideration is only the perception of the division of things that are similar in appearance, and the perception of the combination of things that are separate in appearance. For things differ in matters and agree in matters, and the function of the intellect is to distinguish between what they share and what they differ in. And that is by these divisions which we are engaged in. So this is an aspect of the division of propositions by consideration
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional disjunctive proposition (shartiyya munfasila) and its variants. Author first gives the classic kalam example: 'The world is either originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim)'—a fundamental dichotomy in Islamic theology. The disjunctive contrasts with the conjunctive: in the former, one part is necessarily separate from the other, whereas in the latter, one part is necessarily connected. Author notes that theologians (mutakallimun) call this method 'sabr wa taqsim' (probing and dividing), a key tool for identifying the 'illa (ratio legis) in usul al-fiqh. Then disjunctives are categorized by number of parts: confined (hasr) to two or more (e.g., a number being equal, less, or greater), or not confined (e.g., 'This is either black or white'—there are other colors). The main focus is the three subtypes based on whether they prevent conjunction (jam') and/or exclusion (khuluw): 1) Mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw: prevents both conjunction and exclusion—exactly one alternative must be true (e.g., world is either originated or eternal). 2) Mani' al-jam' duna al-khuluw: prevents conjunction but allows exclusion—both cannot be true, but both can be false (e.g., 'This is either animal or tree' allows it to be neither, like a donkey). 3) Mani' al-khuluw duna al-jam': prevents exclusion but allows conjunction—both cannot be false, but both can be true. This is the trickiest subtype, illustrated by 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he does not drown.' Author explains that this arises when one part is replaced by its necessary implication (lazim) that is more general. The proper division should be 'in the sea or on land' (mutually exclusive and exhaustive), but 'not drowning' is a necessary implication of 'on land' and is more general (sea-dwellers may also not drown). Thus it is possible for Zaid to be in the sea and not drown (both true) but impossible for both to be false if he is on land he doesn't drown? Actually, the passage is a bit confusing: it says it prevents exclusion (one must be true) but allows conjunction. The author then concludes with a meta-commentary on the intellect's function: to distinguish between things that appear similar and to combine things that appear separate. This taxonomy of disjunctives is an exercise of the intellect in discerning subtle logical differences. The lesson is crucial for understanding classical logic and its application in Islamic legal theory and theology.

The three subtypes of conditional disjunctive propositions—preventing both conjunction and exclusion, preventing conjunction only, and preventing exclusion only—form the basic logical tools for exhaustive analysis in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Classification of disjunctive conditionals into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive conditionals (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluw, man' al-jam' duna al-khuluw, man' al-khuluw duna al-jam') and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals are defined by what they exclude: joint truth, joint falsehood, or both.

Conditional propositions are more complex than categoricals because they involve a relation between two categoricals. Disjunctive conditionals assert that at least one of two (or more) parts holds, but the exact logical force depends on whether both can be true or both false. This lesson unpacks the three types of disjunction, a key tool for exhaustive analysis in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
A disjunctive conditional connects two categorical propositions with 'either...or'. For example, 'The world is either originated or eternal.' Recognize the two parts (e.g., 'the world is originated' and 'the world is eternal').
The parts are called 'juz' (جزء).
2
Check for joint truth (jam')
Ask: Can both parts be true simultaneously? If the answer is no, the disjunction prevents combination (mani' al-jam').
For 'originated or eternal', both cannot be true because the same thing cannot be both.
3
Check for joint falsehood (khuluw)
Ask: Can both parts be false at the same time? If the answer is no, the disjunction prevents absence (mani' al-khuluw).
For 'originated or eternal', both cannot be false; the world must be one or the other.
4
Classify into one of three types
Combine the answers: (1) If both jam' and khuluw are prevented → first type (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw). (2) If only jam' is prevented → second type. (3) If only khuluw is prevented → third type.
The third type often arises when one part is replaced by its necessary implication (lazim), as in 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not drown'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لازمه لا نفسه بأن قُلت مثلاً: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما ألا يغرق، فإن هذا يمنع الخلق ولا يمنع الجمع، إذ يجوز أن يكون في البحر ولا يغرق، ولا يجوز أن أحد القسمين؛ وسببه أنك أخذت نفي الغرق الذي هو لازم كونه في البرّ وهو أعم منه، فإن الذي في البحر أيضاً قد لا يغرق وكان أصل التقسيم يقتضي أن يقال : إما أن يكون في البحر وإما أن يكون في البرّ، فكان يمتنع به الجمع والخلوّ جميعاً، ولكن عدم الغرق لازم لكونه في البرّ ثم ليس مساوياً بل هو أعمّ فلم يبعد أن يتناول كونه في البحر فيؤدي إلى الاجتماع. فهذه أمور متشابهة لا بدّ من تحقيق الفرق بينها . فلا معنى لنظر العقل إلا درك انقسام الأمور المتشابهة في القضايا باعتبار أجزائها في الحلّ والتركيب إلى أصنافها من الحمل والاتصال والانفصال.
The third art: on the composition of simple meanings, i.e. propositions. The removal of the two letters of condition and consequence from them into two categoricals, then each categorical is reduced to a single predicate and a single subject, so the conditional is necessarily more composite since it is not resolved in the first instance into simples, but rather is resolved into categoricals first then into simples secondarily. The third type: what is called a disjunctive conditional, like our statement: the world is either originated or eternal, so they are two categorical propositions combined and one of them made necessarily disjoined from the other, whereas in the conjunctive conditional it was necessarily conjunctive, and for that reason it is called disjunctive. The theologians call this 'exploration and division'. Then this disjunctive may be confined to two parts as we mentioned, or it may be in three or more like our statement: this number is either equal to that number or less or greater, so it is confined despite having three. And sometimes the parts become numerous such that it is not included in enumeration, like our statement: this is either black or white, and so-and-so is either in Mecca or Baghdad. Then it is divided into three divisions: First: what prevents both combination and absence together, like our statement: the world is either originated or eternal, for it prevents the combination of eternity and origination and the absence of both, i.e. both are not allowed and one of them is necessarily required. Second: what prevents combination without absence, as when someone says: this is an animal and a tree, so you say: it is either an animal or a tree, i.e. they do not combine together, though it is possible to be free of both, e.g. being a donkey. Third: what prevents absence and does not prevent combination, as when you take instead of one of the two parts its necessary implication not itself, e.g. you say: either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown, for this prevents absence but does not prevent combination, because it is permissible that he is in the sea and does not drown, and it is not permissible that [he is free from] one of the two parts; the reason is that you took the negation of drowning which is the necessary implication of his being on land, and it is more general than that, for he who is in the sea may also not drown, and the original division required that it be said: either he is in the sea or he is on land, so it would prevent both combination and absence, but the non-drowning is a necessary implication of his being on land, then it is not equal but more general, so it is not far that it includes his being in the sea, leading to combination. These are similar matters which must be investigated to distinguish between them. There is no meaning for the intellect except the perception of the division of things that are similar in appearance, and the perception of the combination of things that are separate in appearance, for things differ in some matters and share in others, and the function of the intellect is to distinguish between what they share and what they differ in, and that is through these divisions which we are in the process of, so this is an aspect of the division of propositions with respect to their parts in analysis and composition into their types of categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the third art of logic, focusing on conditional propositions. The author explains that conditionals are more composite than categoricals because they reduce to categoricals. He then defines the disjunctive conditional and gives examples. The main classification is into three types: (1) man' al-jam' wa al-khuluw (exclusive disjunction), where both cannot be true and both cannot be false; (2) man' al-jam' duna al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction), where both cannot be true but both can be false; (3) man' al-khuluw duna al-jam' (exclusive only of absence), where at least one must be true but both can be true. The third type arises when the necessary implication replaces one part. The author concludes with a reflection on the role of the intellect in discerning similarities and differences.

Affirmative and Negative Propositions: Categorical, Conjunctive, Disjunctive
Author defines affirmative and negative for categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is affirmative or negative in categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive forms.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either affirmative or negative; negation of a conditional negates the relation, not just the parts.

This lesson introduces the fundamental division of propositions into affirmative (positive) and negative (negative). The author explains how this applies to each of the three types of propositions: categorical, conjunctive conditional, and disjunctive conditional. Understanding this division is crucial for correctly analyzing logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
The author states that every proposition (قضية) is divided into affirmative and negative, and he covers three types: categorical (حملي), conjunctive conditional (متصل), and disjunctive conditional (منفصل). Locate the examples for each type in the Arabic text.
Categorical: 'man is an animal'. Conjunctive: 'if the world is originated, then it has a creator'. Disjunctive: 'this number is either equal to that number or different from it'.
2
Define affirmative for each type
For categoricals, affirmatives assert the predicate of the subject (e.g., 'man is an animal'). For conjunctive conditionals, affirmatives assert the implication (لزوم) between antecedent and consequent. For disjunctive conditionals, affirmatives assert the disjunction (انفصال) and its prevention of absence (منع الخلاء).
The author emphasizes that categorical affirmation is absolute, without temporal or modal qualification.
3
Define negative for each type
Negation negates the relation: categorical negation denies the predicate (e.g., 'man is not an animal'). Conjunctive negation denies the implication (نفي اللزوم). Disjunctive negation denies the disjunction (نفي الانفصال), which the author says negates the 'prevention of absence' and indicates possibility.
The negation of a disjunctive proposition does not simply assert that both parts are false; it negates the exclusivity relation.
4
Understand the purpose of the division
The author states that the purpose of the division of disjunctive propositions is 'prevention of absence' (منع الخلاء). The negation of a disjunctive proposition negates this prevention, implying that the two parts are not mutually exclusive in that way.
This distinction is important for correctly interpreting disjunctive arguments in Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثانية للقضية باعتبار نسبة محمولها إلى موضوعها بنفي أو إثبات : اعلم أن كل قضيّة من هذه الأصناف الثلاثة تنقسم إلى سالبة وموجبة، ونعني بهما النافية والمثبتة، فالإيجاب الحملي مثل قولنا : الإنسان حيوان، ومعناه أن الشيء الذي نفرضه في الذهن إنساناً، سواء كان موجوداً أو لم يكن موجوداً، يجب أن نفرضه حيواناً ونحكم عليه بأنه حيوان من غير زيادة وقت وحال، بل على ما يعمّ الموقت ومقابله والمقيد ومقابله بل قولنا : إنه حيوان في كلّ حال أو حيوان في بعض الأحوال كلامان متصلان بزيادتين على مطلق قولنا : إنه حيوان، هذا ما اللفظ صريح فيه وإن كان لا يبعد أن يسبق إلى الفهم العموم بحكم العادة، لا سيما إذا انضمت إليه قرينة حال الموضوع. وأما السلب الحملي فهو مثل قولنا: الإنسان ليس بحيوان. وأما الإيجاب المتصل فهو مثل قولنا : إن كان العالم حادثاً فله محدث، والسلب ما يسلب هذا اللزوم، والاتصال كقولنا: ليس إن كان العالم محدثاً فله محدث والإيجاب المنفصل مثل قولنا : هذا العدد إما مُساوِ لذلك العدد أو مفاوت له، والسلب ما يسلب هذا الانفصال وهو قولنا : ليس هذا العدد إما مساوياً لذلك العدد أو مفاوتاً له ومقصود هذا التقسيم منع الخلق، فالسلب له هو الذي يسلب منع الخلق ويشير إلى إمكانه .
The second division of the proposition with respect to the relation of its predicate to its subject by negation or affirmation: Know that every proposition of these three types is divided into negative and affirmative, and we mean by them the negating and the affirming. So categorical affirmation is like our statement: man is an animal, and its meaning is that the thing which we suppose in the mind as a man, whether it exists or does not exist, must be supposed as an animal and we judge it to be an animal without any addition of time or state, but rather in a way that includes the temporal and its opposite, the qualified and its opposite. Rather our statement: he is an animal in every state or an animal in some states are two conjunctive statements with additions to the absolute statement: he is an animal. This is what the wording makes explicit, although it is not unlikely that generality comes to mind by habit, especially when accompanied by a contextual indication of the subject. As for categorical negation, it is like our statement: man is not an animal. As for conjunctive affirmation, it is like our statement: if the world is originated, then it has a creator; and negation is what negates this implication, i.e., the conjunction, like our statement: it is not the case that if the world is originated then it has a creator. And disjunctive affirmation is like our statement: this number is either equal to that number or different from it; and negation is what negates this disjunction, i.e., our statement: it is not the case that this number is either equal to that number or different from it. And the purpose of this division is the prevention of absence, so the negation of it is what negates the prevention of absence and indicates its possibility.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains the second division of propositions: affirmative and negative. The author clarifies that every proposition (categorical, conjunctive, disjunctive) can be either affirmative or negative. For categoricals, affirmation means judging the predicate to be true of the subject without temporal or modal qualification; negation is the opposite. For conjunctive conditionals, affirmation is asserting the implication (luzum), negation is denying that implication. For disjunctive conditionals, affirmation is asserting the disjunction (infisal) with its preventing of absence, negation is denying that disjunction. The author emphasizes that the negation of a disjunctive proposition negates the 'prevention of absence' (man' al-khuluw) and indicates possibility.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation apply to all proposition types: categorical (subject-predicate), conjunctive (implication), and disjunctive (exclusive relation); negation of a disjunctive negates the prevention of absence.

Distinguishing Negative from Privative Propositions
Author addresses objection about 'not seeing' proposition and distinguishes between negative and privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition as negative or privative (معدولة) based on its logical meaning and explain the consequence for syllogistic validity.

Core Idea
A proposition's logical quality is determined by its meaning, not its verbal form.

This passage from the Mi'yar al-'Ilm addresses a common confusion: the phrase 'غير بصير' appears negative but expresses a positive quality (blindness). Understanding this distinction is critical because valid syllogisms require at least one affirmative premise. Let's analyze the author's argument and learn how to classify such propositions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The author presents a hypothetical objector asking whether 'Zayd is not seeing' is negative or affirmative. If affirmative, what distinguishes it from 'Zayd is not seeing' (true negation)? If negative, what distinguishes it from 'Zayd is blind' (affirmative)?
The objector points out that the phrase has no meaning except affirmation, as shown by Persian translation.
2
Understand the Author's Warning
The author calls this a 'slippery place' and stresses the importance of the distinction because failing to distinguish leads to errors in demonstrations, particularly in syllogisms where two negative premises cannot yield a conclusion.
This sets up the practical importance of the lesson.
3
Recognize the Category: Privative (معدولة)
The author classifies 'زيد غير بصير' as a positive proposition (موجبة) despite its negative form. It is a privative proposition (قضية غير محصلة or معدولة) – it affirms a quality (blindness) using a negated predicate.
Compare with true negative: 'زيد ليس بصيرًا' would be a simple negation.
4
Apply the Rule to Syllogisms
In a categorical syllogism, at least one premise must be affirmative. If a privative proposition is mistakenly treated as negative, a syllogism with two such premises would be invalid. Correct classification ensures valid reasoning.
The author explicitly links this to syllogistic rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : قولنا : زيد غير بصير سالبة أو موجبة ؛ فإن كانت موجبة فما الفرق بينه وبين قولنا زيد ليس بصيراً؟ وإن كانت سالبة فما الفرق بينه وبين قولنا : زيد أعمى وهي موجبة؟ ولا معنى لقولنا : غير بصير إِلَّا الإيجاب، ولذلك لا يتبيّن في الفارسية فرق بين قولنا «زيد كوراست» وبين قولنا : «زيد نابیناست وكذا قولنا : زيد «نادانست إذ المفهوم منه أنه جاهل والصيغة صيغة النفي قلنا : هذا موضع مزلّة قدم والاعتناء ببيانه واجب، فإن مَنْ لا يُميز بين السالب والموجب كثر غلطه في البراهين فإنا سنبين أن القياس لا ينتظم من مقدمتين سالبتين بل لا بد أن يكون إحداهما موجبة حتى ينتج . ومن القضايا ما صيغتها صيغة السلب ومعناها معنى الإيجاب فلا بد من تحقيقها ؛ فنقول : قولنا زيد غير بصير قضية موجبة كترجمته بالفارسية، وكأن
If someone says: Our statement 'Zayd is not seeing' is it negative or affirmative? If it is affirmative, what is the difference between it and our statement 'Zayd is not seeing'? And if it is negative, what is the difference between it and our statement 'Zayd is blind', which is affirmative? There is no meaning for 'not seeing' except affirmation, and therefore in Persian there is no difference between our statement 'Zayd is blind' and our statement 'Zayd is not seeing', and similarly our statement 'Zayd is ignorant', for what is understood is that he is ignorant, and the form is negative. We say: This is a slippery place and it is obligatory to take care in explaining it, for whoever does not distinguish between the negative and affirmative makes many mistakes in demonstrations, for we will show that a syllogism cannot be composed of two negative premises, but one of them must be affirmative in order to produce a conclusion. Among propositions, some have the form of negation but the meaning of affirmation, so it is necessary to investigate them. So we say: Our statement 'Zayd is not seeing' is an affirmative proposition, like its translation in Persian, and as if...

Commentary
This passage addresses a common confusion: the phrase 'غير بصير' (not seeing) appears negated but actually expresses a positive quality (blindness). The author explains that such propositions are affirmative in meaning, even though their verbal form is negative. This distinction is crucial for logical demonstrations because a valid syllogism requires at least one affirmative premise. The author emphasizes that failing to distinguish between true negation (سالب) and privative/negative form with affirmative meaning (معدولة) leads to errors. The example from Persian illustrates that both 'Zayd is blind' and 'Zayd is not seeing' translate identically, showing the affirmative intent.
