Classifying Propositions by Quantity
Author defines and classifies propositions based on subject generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify any proposition as personal, indefinite, universal quantified, or particular quantified, and explain the logical force of indefinite propositions in a syllogism.

Core Idea
A proposition's quantity reveals whether its judgment covers all, some, or one individual of the subject.

Propositions are not all alike in how much of the subject they cover. Some talk about a single individual, others about a general category, and among those, some make it clear whether they mean all or only some. The author of this passage lays out these distinctions and warns about the danger of using an indefinite proposition—one without an explicit quantifier—when you need a universal conclusion. Let's break down the classification and see why the indefinite is treated like a particular in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify personal propositions
A personal proposition has a singular subject, like 'Zayd is a writer'. The subject is an individual name. Recognize it by the subject being a proper noun or a singular definite description.
Personal propositions are the simplest; they apply to exactly one individual.
2
Distinguish indefinite from quantified propositions
Indefinite propositions (muhmala) have a universal term as subject but no quantifier, e.g., 'Man is in loss'. Quantified propositions (mahṣūra) include a quantifier like 'every', 'some', 'no one', making the quantity explicit.
The Arabic definite article 'al-' does not automatically make it universal; the author argues that if it is not established as a universal quantifier in the language, the proposition remains indefinite.
3
Classify quantified propositions as universal or particular
Quantified propositions are either universal (e.g., 'Every man is an animal') or particular (e.g., 'Some animal is a man'). The quantifier determines which. Negatives also have quantifiers: 'No one of people is a stone' (universal negative), 'Some people are not a writer' (particular negative).
The author notes that 'not some' and 'not every' have the same import in particular negatives.
4
Apply the indefinite in a syllogism example
Examine the jurist's argument: 'The measured is usurious, and gypsum is measured, so gypsum is usurious.' The term 'the measured' is indefinite (no quantifier). If intended as universal ('all measured'), the premise may be false; if intended as particular ('some measured'), the conclusion is only that some measured is usurious, not necessarily gypsum. The syllogism fails if a universal conclusion is sought.
The indefinite has the logical force of a particular proposition because it necessarily includes at least one instance, but does not guarantee universality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الثالثة للقضية باعتبار عموم موضوعها أو خصوصه: اعلم أن موضوع القضايا إما شخصي فتكون شخصية كقولنا : زيد كاتب زيد ليس بكاتب، وإما كلّي فتكون كلّية والكلية إما مهملة كقولنا : الإنسان في خُسْر (۱)، الإنسان ليس في خُسْر . وسميناها مهملة لأنه لم يتبين فيها وجود المحمول لكلية الموضوع أو لبعضه، وإما محصورة وهي التي بيّن فيها أن الحكم لكله كقولنا : كلّ إنسان حيوان، أو ذكر أنه لبعضه كقولنا : بعض الحيوان إنسان فإذن القضية بهذا الاعتبار أربعة شخصية ،ومهملة ومحصورة كلّية ومحصورة جزئية. والقضية تقسم إلى هذه الأقسام، سالبة كانت أو موجبة شرطية كانت أو حملية، متصلة كانت الشرطية أو منفصلة، واللفظ الحاصر يُسمّى سوراً، كقولنا في الموجبة الكلية : كلّ إنسان حيوان، وقولنا في الموجبة الجزئية بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكقولنا في السالبة الكلية : لا واحد من الناس بحجر وكقولنا في السالبة الجزئية : ليس بعض الناس كاتباً، أو ليس كلّ إنسان كاتباً، فإن فحواهما واحد. فإن قلت : فالألف واللام إذا كانتا للاستغراق فقول القائل: الإنسان في خُسْرٍ كلية، فكيف سميناها مهملة؟ فاعلم أنه إن ثبت ذلك في لغة العرب وجب طلب المهمل من لغة أخرى، وإن لم يثبت فهو مهمل إذ يحتمل الكلّ ويحتمل الجزء، وتكون قوة المهمل قوة الجزئي لأنه بالضرورة يشتمل عليه. وأما العموم فمشكوك فيه وليس من ضرورة ما يصدق جزئياً ألا يصدق كليا، فليحذر عن المهملات في الأقيسة إذا كان المطلوب منها نتيجة كلية، كما يقول الفقيه مثلاً : المكيل ربوي والجص مكيل فكان ربوياً، فيقال : قولك المكيل مهمل ، فإن أردت الكلّ فممنوع وإن أردت به الجزء فينتج أن بعض المكيل ربوي، فإذا قلت : بعض المكيل ربوي والجص مكيل فكان ربوياً، لم يلزمه النتيجة إذ يحتمل أن يكون من البعض الآخر الذي ليس بربوي. فإن قلت : فكيف يكون الحصر والإهمال في الشرطيات؟ فافهم أنك مهما قلت: كلّما كان الشيء حادثاً فله محدث، أو قلت دائماً : إما أن يكون الشيء حادثاً أو قديماً، فقد حصرت الحصر الكلّي الموجب. وإذا قلت ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء موجوداً فهو في جهة وليس البتة إذا كان البيع صحيحاً فهو لازم، فقد سلبت الاتصال وحصرت وسائر نظائر هذا يمكنك قياسها عليه.
The third division of the proposition in consideration of the generality of its subject or its particularity: Know that the subject of propositions is either individual, so it is individual like our saying: Zayd is a writer, Zayd is not a writer; or universal, so it is universal. The universal is either indefinite like our saying: 'Man is in loss', 'Man is not in loss'. And we called it indefinite because it is not clarified in it the existence of the predicate for all of the subject or for some of it. Or quantified, which is that in which it is clarified that the judgment is for all of it like our saying: 'Every man is an animal', or it is mentioned that it is for some of it like our saying: 'Some animal is a man'. So the proposition according to this consideration is four: individual, indefinite, universal quantified, and particular quantified. The proposition divides into these divisions, whether negative or affirmative, conditional or categorical, the conditional being conjunctive or disjunctive. And the word of quantification is called a quantifier (sūr), like our saying in the universal affirmative: 'Every man is an animal', and our saying in the particular affirmative: 'Some animal is a man', and like our saying in the universal negative: 'No one of people is a stone', and our saying in the particular negative: 'Some people are not a writer', or 'Not every man is a writer', for their import is one. If you say: The alif and lām when they are for generality, so the saying of the speaker: 'Man is in loss' is universal, so how did we call it indefinite? Know that if that is established in the language of the Arabs, then it is necessary to seek the indefinite from another language; and if it is not established, then it is indefinite, since it admits the whole and admits the part, and the force of the indefinite is the force of the particular because it necessarily includes it. As for generality, it is doubtful, and it is not necessary that what is true as particular is not true as universal. So beware of indefinites in syllogisms if the desired result from them is a universal conclusion, as the jurist says for example: 'The measured (mikyal) is usurious, and gypsum is measured, so it is usurious.' Then it is said: Your saying 'the measured' is indefinite; if you intended the whole, it is denied, and if you intended by it the part, then it produces that some measured is usurious. So if you say: 'Some measured is usurious and gypsum is measured, so it is usurious', the conclusion does not follow from it, for it is possible that it is from the other part that is not usurious. If you say: So how is quantification and indefinite in conditionals? Understand that whenever you say: 'Whenever a thing is originated, it has an originator', or you say always: 'Either a thing is originated or eternal', you have quantified the universal affirmative quantification. And if you say: 'Not at all if a thing exists then it is in a direction', and 'Not at all if the sale is valid then it is binding', you have negated the connection and quantified. And the rest of the like of this you can measure upon it.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of propositions according to the generality or particularity of their subject. The author divides propositions into three main types: personal (when the subject is an individual like Zayd), universal (when the subject is a universal term), and the universal is further divided into indefinite (muhmala) and quantified (mahṣūra). Quantified propositions are either universal (e.g., 'Every man is an animal') or particular (e.g., 'Some animal is a man'). So there are four types: personal, indefinite, universal quantified, particular quantified. The author notes that this division applies to negative, affirmative, conditional, and categorical propositions alike. The word that indicates quantification is called a quantifier (sūr), and examples are given for each type: 'every' for universal affirmative, 'some' for particular affirmative, 'no one' for universal negative, and 'not some' or 'not every' for particular negative (both having the same meaning). Then the author addresses a potential objection: if the definite article 'al-' in Arabic indicates generality (istighrāq), then 'Man is in loss' would be universal, not indefinite. The author's response is that if this is established in Arabic, then one must look for indefinite in another language; but if not established, it remains indefinite because it could refer to all or some. The indefinite has the force of the particular (juz'ī) because it necessarily includes at least one instance. The author warns against using indefinite premises in syllogisms when a universal conclusion is desired, giving a fiqh example: a jurist might argue that 'The measured (mikyal) is usurious, and gypsum is measured, so it is usurious.' The objection is that 'the measured' is indefinite; if it means all measured things, the premise is false; if it means some, the conclusion is only that some measured is usurious, not necessarily gypsum. So the syllogism fails. The author then explains how quantification works in conditional propositions: e.g., 'Whenever a thing is originated, it has an originator' is universal affirmative; 'Either a thing is originated or eternal' is also quantified; negating the connection also constitutes quantification.

محصورة
سور
شخصية

Key Takeaway
Indefinite propositions (muhmala) have the logical force of particulars; they cannot be used to derive universal conclusions in a syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Modalities of Predication
Classification of propositions by modal material and the absolute/restricted distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition according to its modality (necessary, impossible, possible) and identify whether it is absolute or restricted.

Core Idea
Every proposition has a 'material' – the nature of the predicate's relation to the subject: necessary, impossible, or possible.

Before this lesson, you learned to divide propositions by quantity and quality. Now we add a deeper layer: the mode (jiha) of the relation between predicate and subject. The author calls this the 'material' (mādda) of predication. Understanding modality is essential for evaluating theological and legal propositions, such as whether a divine attribute is necessary or possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate relation
For a given proposition, ask: Is the predicate necessarily true of the subject, necessarily false, or neither? Example: 'Man is animal' – necessary truth.
2
Label the material
If necessary truth, material is wujūb; if necessary false, material is imtinā'; if neither, material is imkān.
3
Check for explicit modality
Does the proposition state 'necessarily', 'possibly', etc.? If yes, it is restricted (muqayyada); if no, it is absolute (muṭlaqa).
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Take a theological proposition like 'Allah is knowing' – necessary; a legal proposition like 'Prayer is obligatory' – necessary in a different sense; 'Man is a writer' – possible.

القسمة الرابعة للقضية باعتبار جهة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بالوجوب أو الجواز أو الامتناع : اعلم أن المحمول في القضية لا يخلو إما أن تكون نسبته إلى الموضوع نسبة الضروري الوجود في نفس الأمر ، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، فإن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان ونسبته إليه نسبة الضروري الوجود، وإما أن تكون نسبته إليه نسبة الضروري العدم كقولنا : الإنسان حجر فإن الحجرية محمولة، ونسبتها إلى الإنسان نسبة الضروري العدم، وإما ألا يكون ضرورياً لا وجوده ولا عدمه كقولنا : الإنسان كاتب الإنسان ليس بكاتب ولنسم هذه النسبة مادة الحمل، فالمادة ثلاثة : الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع والقضية بهذا الاعتبار إما مطلقة وإما مقيدة، والمقيدة ما نص فيها بأن المحمول للموضوع ضروري أو ممكن أو موجود على الدوام لا بالضرورة ) . والمطلق ما لم يتعرض فيه إلى شيء من ذلك، فإن هذه الأمور زائدة على ما يقتضيه مجرّد الحمل والقضيّة الضرورية تنقسم إلى ما لا شرط فيه كقولنا : الله فإنه لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك، وإلى ما شرط فيه وجود الموضوع كقولنا : الإنسان حي، فإنه ما دام موجوداً فهو كذلك، فوجود الموضوع مشروط فيه ولا يفارق هذا المشروط الضروري الأول في جهة الضرورة، وإنما يفارق في دوام الموضوع لذاته أزلاً وأبداً ووجوب وجوده لنفس حقيقته، ولنسم هذا بالضروري المطلق. فأما الضروري المشروط فثلاثة : الأول : ما يشترط فيه دوام وجود الموضوع ومثاله ما تقدّم . الثاني : ما شرط فيه دوام كون الموضوع موصوفاً بعنوانه كقولنا : كلّ مُتحرك متغيّر، فإنه مُتغيّر ما دام مُتحرّكاً لا ما دام ذات المُتحرّك موجوداً فحسب. والفرق بينه وبين قولنا : الإنسان حي أن الشرط في الحي ذات الإنسان، والشرط ههنا ذات المُتحرّك فقط، بل ذات المُتحرّك له ذات وجوهر من كونه فرساً أو سماء أو ما شئت أن تسميه ويلحقه أنه مُتحرّك، وذاك الذات هو غير المُتحرّك، وليس الإنسان كذلك . الثالث : ما يشترط فيه وقت مخصوص إما مُعيّن أو غير مُعيّن، فإن قولنا : القمر بالضرورة مُنخسف مُقيّد بوقت مُعيّن، وهو وقت وقوعه في ظلّ الأرض محجوباً بذلك عن ضوء الشمس، وقولنا الإنسان بالضرورة متنفس فمعناه أنه في بعض الأوقات، وذلك الوقت غير مُتعيّن .
The fourth division of the proposition in consideration of the mode of the relation of the predicate to the subject by necessity, possibility, or impossibility: Know that the predicate in the proposition is either such that its relation to the subject is a relation of necessary existence in reality, like your saying: 'Man is an animal', for animal is predicated of man and its relation to him is a relation of necessary existence; or its relation to him is a relation of necessary non-existence like our saying: 'Man is a stone', for stonehood is predicated, and its relation to man is a relation of necessary non-existence; or it is not necessary either in existence or in non-existence like our saying: 'Man is a writer', 'Man is not a writer'. And let us call this relation the material (mādda) of predication. So the material is three: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And the proposition according to this consideration is either absolute (muṭlaqa) or restricted (muqayyada). The restricted is that in which it is stated that the predicate for the subject is necessary or possible or existent perpetually not necessarily. And the absolute is that in which none of that is mentioned, for these matters are additional to what is required by mere predication. And the necessary proposition divides into that which has no condition in it, like our saying: 'God is', for He has not ceased and will not cease to be thus; and into that in which the existence of the subject is conditioned, like our saying: 'Man is living', for as long as he exists he is thus. So the existence of the subject is conditioned in it, and this conditioned does not differ from the first unconditional necessary in respect of necessity, but differs in the perpetuity of the subject in itself eternally and everlastingly and the necessity of its existence for its own essence. And let us call this the absolute necessary. As for the conditioned necessary, it is three: First: that in which the perpetuity of the existence of the subject is conditioned, and its example is what has preceded. Second: that in which the perpetuity of the subject being described by its description is conditioned, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is changing', for it is changing as long as it is moving, not as long as the essence of the moving thing exists merely. And the difference between it and our saying 'Man is living' is that the condition in 'living' is the essence of man, while the condition here is only the essence of the moving thing. Rather, the essence of the moving thing has an essence and substance from its being a horse or a sky or whatever you wish to name, and there attaches to it that it is moving, and that essence is other than the moving thing. And man is not like that. Third: that in which a specific time is conditioned, either determined or undetermined. For our saying: 'The moon is necessarily eclipsed' is restricted to a determined time, which is the time of its falling in the earth's shadow, thereby being veiled from the sun's light. And our saying: 'Man is necessarily breathing' means that he is in some times, and that time is undetermined.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions according to modality. The author explains that the predicate's relation to the subject can be one of three types: necessary existence (e.g., 'Man is an animal'), necessary non-existence (e.g., 'Man is a stone'), or neither necessary (e.g., 'Man is a writer' could be true or false). This relation is called the 'material' (mādda) of predication, and it has three divisions: necessity (wujūb), possibility (imkān), and impossibility (imtinā'). Propositions are then either absolute (muṭlaqa) — those that do not specify modality — or restricted (muqayyada) — those that explicitly state necessity, possibility, or perpetualness. The author then focuses on necessary propositions, dividing them into unconditional and conditional. Unconditional necessary propositions have no condition, like attributing existence to God, who is necessarily existent always. Conditional necessary propositions require a condition: (1) the condition of the subject's existence (e.g., 'Man is living' only as long as he exists), (2) the condition of the subject's being described by a certain attribute (e.g., 'Every moving thing is changing' only while it is moving — the difference is that here the condition is the attribute 'moving', not the essence of the subject), and (3) the condition of a specific time, either determinate (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed' at a particular time) or indeterminate (e.g., 'Man breathes' at some indefinite times). The author carefully distinguishes these types to show the different ways necessity can be qualified.

مادة الحمل
وجوب
امتناع
إمكان
مطلقة
مقيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the material (mādda) of the proposition 'Man is a writer'?
A) Necessary existence (wujūb)
B) Necessary non-existence (imtinā')
C) Possibility (imkān)
D) It is absolute, so no material

Key Takeaway
The material of a proposition is determined by whether the predicate relation is necessary, impossible, or possible, independent of whether the proposition explicitly states the modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (al-‘ālam ḥādith). Determine its material according to the passage. Is it necessary existence, necessary non-existence, or possible? Then, is it absolute or restricted? Justify your answer based on the text.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Subdivisions of Necessary Propositions
Division of necessary propositions into unconditional and conditional, with three subtypes of conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between unconditional necessary and three types of conditional necessary propositions, using examples from Islamic theology and natural philosophy.

Core Idea
Not all necessary propositions are eternal; many are conditionally necessary, depending on the subject's existence, attributes, or time.

In Lesson 1, you learned that some propositions have necessary material. But the author now shows that necessity itself has varieties. Some necessary truths hold without any condition (like God's existence), while others require a condition to be true. This distinction is crucial for understanding statements about creatures, like 'Man is living' or 'The moon is eclipsed'.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the necessity is unconditional
Does the proposition state a predicate that is always true of the subject, regardless of any condition? Example: 'God is existent' – unconditional.
2
If conditional, determine the type of condition
Type (a): condition of the subject's existence (e.g., 'Man is living' – true only while man exists). Type (b): condition of the subject's attribute (e.g., 'Every moving thing is changing' – true only while it moves). Type (c): condition of a specific time (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed' – true at a specific time).
3
Differentiate between condition types
Note the difference between (a) and (b): In (a), the condition is the subject's essence; in (b), the condition is an accidental attribute separate from the essence.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
For instance, 'The Prophet is infallible' – conditional on his being a prophet? Or 'The Qur'an is revealed' – conditional on time? Use appropriate examples from kalam or fiqh.

The fourth division of the proposition in consideration of the mode of the relation of the predicate to the subject by necessity, possibility, or impossibility: Know that the predicate in the proposition is either such that its relation to the subject is a relation of necessary existence in reality, like your saying: 'Man is an animal', for animal is predicated of man and its relation to him is a relation of necessary existence; or its relation to him is a relation of necessary non-existence like our saying: 'Man is a stone', for stonehood is predicated, and its relation to man is a relation of necessary non-existence; or it is not necessary either in existence or in non-existence like our saying: 'Man is a writer', 'Man is not a writer'. And let us call this relation the material (mādda) of predication. So the material is three: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And the proposition according to this consideration is either absolute (muṭlaqa) or restricted (muqayyada). The restricted is that in which it is stated that the predicate for the subject is necessary or possible or existent perpetually not necessarily. And the absolute is that in which none of that is mentioned, for these matters are additional to what is required by mere predication. And the necessary proposition divides into that which has no condition in it, like our saying: 'God is', for He has not ceased and will not cease to be thus; and into that in which the existence of the subject is conditioned, like our saying: 'Man is living', for as long as he exists he is thus. So the existence of the subject is conditioned in it, and this conditioned does not differ from the first unconditional necessary in respect of necessity, but differs in the perpetuity of the subject in itself eternally and everlastingly and the necessity of its existence for its own essence. And let us call this the absolute necessary. As for the conditioned necessary, it is three: First: that in which the perpetuity of the existence of the subject is conditioned, and its example is what has preceded. Second: that in which the perpetuity of the subject being described by its description is conditioned, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is changing', for it is changing as long as it is moving, not as long as the essence of the moving thing exists merely. And our saying: 'Man is necessarily breathing' means that he is in some times, and that time is undetermined.

Commentary

The author explains that the predicate's relation to the subject can be one of three types: necessary existence (e.g., 'Man is an animal'), necessary non-existence (e.g., 'Man is a stone'), or neither necessary (e.g., 'Man is a writer' could be true or false). Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the conditional necessary proposition 'Every moving thing is changing' from 'Man is living'?
A) In 'Man is living', the condition is the subject's essence; in 'Every moving thing is changing', the condition is the attribute 'moving'.
B) 'Man is living' is unconditional; 'Every moving thing is changing' is conditional.
C) The first is about an essential property, the second about an accidental property.
D) Both are the same type of conditional necessary.

Key Takeaway

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Three Materials of Predication
Author defines the three modal materials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal materials (necessary existence, necessary non-existence, possibility) in any proposition from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every predicate relates to its subject necessarily, impossibly, or possibly.

In logic, propositions are not just about what is said, but how the predicate attaches to the subject. This lesson introduces the three fundamental 'materials' (maddah) of predication that determine whether a claim is necessary, impossible, or merely possible. Understanding these materials is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate and subject
Take a proposition like 'Man is animal'. The subject is 'man', predicate 'animal'. Notice the relation.
2
Determine if the predicate is necessarily true of the subject
Ask: Could the subject exist without this predicate? If not, it is necessary existence (wujub). Example: 'Man is animal'.
3
Determine if the predicate is necessarily false of the subject
Ask: Can the subject ever have this predicate? If impossible, it is necessary non-existence (imtina'). Example: 'Man is stone'.
4
Determine if neither is necessary
If the predicate can be true or false without contradiction, it is possible (imkan). Example: 'Man is a writer'.
5
Name the material
The material is the modal status: wujub, imtina', or imkan. This classification is the foundation of modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الرابعة للقضية باعتبار جهة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بالوجوب أو الجواز أو الامتناع : اعلم أن المحمول في القضية لا يخلو إما أن تكون نسبته إلى الموضوع نسبة الضروري الوجود في نفس الأمر ، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، فإن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان ونسبته إليه نسبة الضروري الوجود، وإما أن تكون نسبته إليه نسبة الضروري العدم كقولنا : الإنسان حجر فإن الحجرية محمولة، ونسبتها إلى الإنسان نسبة الضروري العدم، وإما ألا يكون ضرورياً لا وجوده ولا عدمه كقولنا : الإنسان كاتب الإنسان ليس بكاتب ولنسم هذه النسبة مادة الحمل، فالمادة ثلاثة : الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع والقضية بهذا الاعتبار إما مطلقة وإما مقيدة، والمقيدة ما نص فيها بأن المحمول للموضوع ضروري أو ممكن أو موجود على الدوام لا بالضرورة ) . والمطلق ما لم يتعرض فيه إلى شيء من ذلك، فإن هذه الأمور زائدة على ما يقتضيه مجرّد الحمل والقضيّة الضرورية تنقسم إلى ما لا شرط فيه كقولنا : الله فإنه لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك، وإلى ما شرط فيه وجود الموضوع كقولنا : الإنسان حي، فإنه ما دام موجوداً فهو كذلك، فوجود الموضوع مشروط فيه ولا يفارق هذا المشروط الضروري الأول في جهة الضرورة، وإنما يفارق في دوام الموضوع لذاته أزلاً وأبداً ووجوب وجوده لنفس حقيقته، ولنسم هذا بالضروري المطلق. فأما الضروري المشروط فثلاثة : الأول : ما يشترط فيه دوام وجود الموضوع ومثاله ما تقدّم . الثاني : ما شرط فيه دوام كون الموضوع موصوفاً بعنوانه كقولنا : كلّ مُتحرك متغيّر، فإنه مُتغيّر ما دام مُتحرّكاً لا ما دام ذات المُتحرّك موجوداً فحسب. والفرق بين قولنا : الإنسان حي أن الشرط في الحي ذات الإنسان، والشرط ههنا ذات المُتحرّك فقط، بل ذات المُتحرّك له ذات وجوهر من كونه فرساً أو سماء أو ما شئت أن تسميه ويلحقه أنه مُتحرّك، وذاك الذات هو غير المُتحرّك، وليس الإنسان كذلك . الثالث : ما يشترط فيه وقت مخصوص إما مُعيّن أو غير مُعيّن، فإن قولنا : القمر بالضرورة مُنخسف مُقيّد بوقت مُعيّن، وهو وقت وقوعه في ظلّ الأرض محجوباً بذلك عن ضوء الشمس، وقولنا الإنسان بالضرورة متنفس فمعناه أنه في بعض الأوقات، وذلك الوقت غير مُتعيّن . (۱) فرض أرسطو على القضية إدخال ألفاظ واجب ،وممكن ،وممتنع وتجنّب الغزالي فعل ذلك . فإن قال قائل : وهل يتصوّر دائم غير ضروري؟ قلنا : نعم. أما في الأشخاص فظاهر كالزنجي، فإنك قد تقول : إنه أسود البشرة ما دام موجود البشرة وليس السواد لبشرته ضرورياً ولكنه قد اتفق وجوده لها على الدوام، ولنسم هذه القضية وجودية. وأما في الكليات فكقولنا : كلّ كوكب إما شارق أو غارب، فإنه في كل ساعة كذلك وليس ذلك ضرورياً في وجود ذاته إذ ليس كالحيوان للإنسان فافهم.
The fourth division of the proposition with respect to the mode of the relation of the predicate to the subject: by obligation, possibility, or impossibility. Know that the predicate in a proposition is either such that its relation to the subject is a relation of necessary existence in reality, like your saying: 'Man is animal', for animal is predicated of man and its relation to him is a relation of necessary existence; or such that its relation to him is a relation of necessary non-existence, like our saying: 'Man is stone', for stone-ness is predicated and its relation to man is a relation of necessary non-existence; or it is not necessary, neither its existence nor its non-existence, like our saying: 'Man is a writer', 'Man is not a writer'. And let us name this relation the 'material of predication'. So the material is three: obligation, possibility, and impossibility. And the proposition, in this respect, is either absolute or conditional. The conditional is that in which it is explicitly stated that the predicate for the subject is necessary or possible or perpetually existing without necessity. The absolute is that in which none of this is touched upon, for these matters are additional to what is required by mere predication. And the necessary proposition is divided into that which has no condition, like our saying: 'God', for He has not ceased and will not cease to be so; and into that in which the existence of the subject is conditioned, like our saying: 'Man is living', for as long as he exists, he is so. So the existence of the subject is conditioned in it, and it does not differ from the first conditioned necessary in the aspect of necessity, but only differs in the permanence of the subject in itself eternally and forever and the necessity of its existence for its own essence. And let us name this the 'absolute necessary'. As for the conditioned necessary, it is of three types: First: that in which the permanence of the subject's existence is conditioned, and its example is what preceded. Second: that in which the permanence of the subject being described by its title is conditioned, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is changing', for it is changing as long as it is moving, not as long as the essence of the moving thing exists merely. And the difference between our saying: 'Man is living' is that the condition for 'living' is the essence of man, and the condition here is only the essence of the moving thing—rather, the essence of the moving thing has an essence and substance from its being a horse or sky or whatever you wish to name it, and attached to it is that it is moving, and that essence is other than the moving thing, and man is not like that. Third: that in which a specific time is conditioned, either determinate or indeterminate. For example, our saying: 'The moon is necessarily eclipsed' is restricted to a determinate time, which is the time of its falling into the earth's shadow, being veiled thereby from the sun's light; and our saying: 'Man necessarily breathes' means that it is in some times, and that time is indeterminate. (1) Aristotle imposed on the proposition the introduction of the terms 'necessary', 'possible', and 'impossible', and al-Ghazali avoided doing that. If someone says: 'Is a perpetual non-necessary conceivable?' We say: Yes. As for individuals, it is manifest, like the dark-skinned person: you might say that he is black-skinned as long as skin exists, but blackness is not necessary for his skin, but it happens to exist in it perpetually. Let us name this proposition 'existential'. As for universals, like our saying: 'Every star is either rising or setting', for it is so at every hour, and that is not necessary in the existence of its essence, since it is not like animal for man. So understand.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions according to the mode or 'material' (maddah) of predication. The author begins by explaining that the predicate's relation to the subject is either necessarily existent (like 'man is animal'), necessarily non-existent (like 'man is stone'), or neither necessary nor impossible (like 'man is writer' – he may or may not write). These three possibilities are called the material of the proposition: obligation (wujub), possibility (imkan), and impossibility (imtina'). Propositions are then divided into absolute (mutlaq) – which do not specify any mode – and conditional (muqayyad) – which explicitly state that the predicate is necessary, possible, or perpetual without necessity. The author then focuses on necessary propositions, dividing them into unconditional (like 'God is eternal') and conditional. The unconditional necessary is called 'absolute necessary' (daruri mutlaq). The conditioned necessary has three types: (1) condition on the permanence of the subject's existence (e.g., 'man is living' – as long as he exists); (2) condition on the subject being described by a certain title (e.g., 'every moving thing is changing' – as long as it is moving, not merely as long as its essence exists); (3) condition on a specific time, either determinate (e.g., lunar eclipse at a particular time) or indeterminate (e.g., man breathes at some unspecified time). A footnote mentions that Aristotle imposed the use of modal terms, while al-Ghazali avoided them. Finally, the author answers a potential objection: can a property be perpetual but not necessary? Yes, e.g., a black-skinned person's skin color is perpetual as long as skin exists but not necessary; such a proposition is called 'existential' (wujudiyya). The lesson demonstrates the precise classification of modal propositions in logic.

Key Takeaway
The material of a proposition is the modal status of its predication: necessary existence, necessary non-existence, or possibility.

Propositions
beginner
4 min
Absolute vs Conditional Propositions
Author divides propositions into absolute and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (mutlaq) and conditional (muqayyad) propositions and identify the modal qualifier in a conditional proposition.

Core Idea
A proposition is absolute if it states predication without modal comment; conditional if it explicitly declares necessity, possibility, or perpetual existence.

Once the material is clear, propositions can be expressed either without any modal qualification (absolute) or with an explicit statement about the modality (conditional). This lesson explains that distinction and why it matters for precision in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proposition and note if any modal word appears
Look for words like 'necessary', 'possible', 'always', 'perpetual', or their Arabic equivalents (ضروري, ممكن, دائم). If absent, it is absolute; if present, it is conditional.
2
Check if the modal qualifier is part of the meaning or an addition
The author says modal qualifiers are 'additional to what is required by mere predication'. So absolute propositions just assert the connection; conditional ones add a modal detail.
3
Identify the type of conditional
Is it specifying necessity, possibility, or perpetual existence without necessity? The passage mentions all three as possibilities for conditional propositions.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take 'Zakat is obligatory'. Absolute: 'Zakat is obligatory' says nothing about modality. Conditional: 'Zakat is necessarily obligatory' adds the modal 'necessarily'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الرابعة للقضية باعتبار جهة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بالوجوب أو الجواز أو الامتناع : اعلم أن المحمول في القضية لا يخلو إما أن تكون نسبته إلى الموضوع نسبة الضروري الوجود في نفس الأمر ، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، فإن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان ونسبته إليه نسبة الضروري الوجود، وإما أن تكون نسبته إليه نسبة الضروري العدم كقولنا : الإنسان حجر فإن الحجرية محمولة، ونسبتها إلى الإنسان نسبة الضروري العدم، وإما ألا يكون ضرورياً لا وجوده ولا عدمه كقولنا : الإنسان كاتب الإنسان ليس بكاتب ولنسم هذه النسبة مادة الحمل، فالمادة ثلاثة : الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع والقضية بهذا الاعتبار إما مطلقة وإما مقيدة، والمقيدة ما نص فيها بأن المحمول للموضوع ضروري أو ممكن أو موجود على الدوام لا بالضرورة ) . والمطلق ما لم يتعرض فيه إلى شيء من ذلك، فإن هذه الأمور زائدة على ما يقتضيه مجرّد الحمل والقضيّة الضرورية تنقسم إلى ما لا شرط فيه كقولنا : الله فإنه لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك، وإلى ما شرط فيه وجود الموضوع كقولنا : الإنسان حي، فإنه ما دام موجوداً فهو كذلك، فوجود الموضوع مشروط فيه ولا يفارق هذا المشروط الضروري الأول في جهة الضرورة، وإنما يفارق في دوام الموضوع لذاته أزلاً وأبداً ووجوب وجوده لنفس حقيقته، ولنسم هذا بالضروري المطلق. فأما الضروري المشروط فثلاثة : الأول : ما يشترط فيه دوام وجود الموضوع ومثاله ما تقدّم . الثاني : ما شرط فيه دوام كون الموضوع موصوفاً بعنوانه كقولنا : كلّ مُتحرك متغيّر، فإنه مُتغيّر ما دام مُتحرّكاً لا ما دام ذات المُتحرّك موجوداً فحسب. والفرق بين قولنا : الإنسان حي أن الشرط في الحي ذات الإنسان، والشرط ههنا ذات المُتحرّك فقط، بل ذات المُتحرّك له ذات وجوهر من كونه فرساً أو سماء أو ما شئت أن تسميه ويلحقه أنه مُتحرّك، وذاك الذات هو غير المُتحرّك، وليس الإنسان كذلك . الثالث : ما يشترط فيه وقت مخصوص إما مُعيّن أو غير مُعيّن، فإن قولنا : القمر بالضرورة مُنخسف مُقيّد بوقت مُعيّن، وهو وقت وقوعه في ظلّ الأرض محجوباً بذلك عن ضوء الشمس، وقولنا الإنسان بالضرورة متنفس فمعناه أنه في بعض الأوقات، وذلك الوقت غير مُتعيّن . (۱) فرض أرسطو على القضية إدخال ألفاظ واجب ،وممكن ،وممتنع وتجنّب الغزالي فعل ذلك . فإن قال قائل : وهل يتصوّر دائم غير ضروري؟ قلنا : نعم. أما في الأشخاص فظاهر كالزنجي، فإنك قد تقول : إنه أسود البشرة ما دام موجود البشرة وليس السواد لبشرته ضرورياً ولكنه قد اتفق وجوده لها على الدوام، ولنسم هذه القضية وجودية. وأما في الكليات فكقولنا : كلّ كوكب إما شارق أو غارب، فإنه في كل ساعة كذلك وليس ذلك ضرورياً في وجود ذاته إذ ليس كالحيوان للإنسان فافهم.

Propositions
intermediate

Types of Necessary Propositions
Author divides necessary propositions into unconditional and three conditioned types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between unconditional necessary, conditioned necessary (three types), and identify the condition in each.

Core Idea
Necessary truths are not all alike; some are unconditional, others depend on subject existence, title, or time.

Necessary propositions (daruriyyat) are divided based on whether they hold without any condition or with conditions. This lesson unpacks the three conditioning factors: subject existence, subject being described by a certain title, and time. Understanding these nuances is crucial for precise theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessary proposition
A necessary proposition is one where the predicate is necessarily true for the subject. Look for examples like 'God is eternal' or 'Man is living'.
2
Check if the necessity holds unconditionally
If the predicate is true of the subject regardless of any condition, it is unconditional necessary. Example: 'God is eternal' – no condition needed.
3
If conditional, determine the type of condition
The first condition is the permanence of the subject's existence (e.g., 'Man is living' – as long as man exists). The second is the permanence of the subject being described by a certain title (e.g., 'Every moving thing is changing' – as long as it is moving). The third is a specific time, determinate or indeterminate (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed' at a specific time; 'Man breathes' at some time).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'God is knowing'. Is it unconditional? Yes, because God is always knowing. Now consider 'The Prophet is human' – it is necessary but conditioned on his existence? Actually 'The Prophet is human' is necessary unconditionally? The author might say it depends. But a better example: 'The fasting person is abstaining from food' – necessary as long as he is fasting.

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions according to the mode or 'material' (maddah) of predication. The author begins by explaining that the predicate's relation to the subject is either necessarily existent (like 'man is animal'), necessarily non-existent (like 'man is stone'), or neither necessary nor impossible (like 'man is writer' – he may or may not write). These three possibilities are called the material of the proposition: obligation (wujub), possibility (imkan), and impossibility (imtina'). Propositions are then divided into absolute (mutlaq) – which do not specify any mode – and conditional (muqayyad) – which explicitly state that the predicate is necessary, possible, or perpetual without necessity. The author then focuses on necessary propositions, dividing them into unconditional (like 'God is eternal') and conditional. The unconditional necessary is called 'absolute necessary' (daruri mutlaq). The conditioned necessary has three types: (1) condition on the permanence of the subject's existence (e.g., 'man is living' – as long as he exists); (2) condition on the subject being described by a certain title (e.g., 'every moving thing is changing' – as long as it is moving, not merely as long as its essence exists); (3) condition on a specific time, either determinate (e.g., lunar eclipse at a particular time) or indeterminate (e.g., man breathes at some unspecified time). A footnote mentions that Aristotle imposed the use of modal terms, while al-Ghazali avoided them. Finally, the author answers a potential objection: can a property be perpetual but not necessary? Yes, e.g., a black-skinned person's skin color is perpetual as long as skin exists but not necessary; such a proposition is called 'existential' (wujudiyya). The lesson demonstrates the precise classification of modal propositions in logic.

Perpetual Non-Necessary (Wujudiyya)
Author explains and exemplifies perpetual non-necessary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a perpetual non-necessary (wujudiyya) proposition and distinguish it from necessary propositions.

Core Idea
Some properties are always found with the subject but not by necessity – they are merely perpetual coincidences.

The author answers an important question: Can a predicate be perpetually true of a subject without being necessary? Yes, and such propositions are called 'wujudiyya' (existential). This lesson uses concrete examples from the natural world and Islamic sciences to clarify this concept.

Guided Path
1
Locate a proposition that claims constant or perpetual truth
Look for statements like 'Zanjī is black-skinned' or 'Every star is either rising or setting'. These are said to be always true.
2
Check if the predicate is necessary for the subject
Ask: Could the subject exist without this predicate? For a black person, could skin be white? In principle, yes – blackness is not part of the definition of a human. So it is not necessary, even if always present.
3
If always true but not necessary, it is wujudiyya
The author calls this 'existential' – it is a matter of fact that the property happens to be always present, but it is not required by the essence.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'Every prophet is truthful'. Is this necessary? If truthfulness is part of the definition of prophethood, it is necessary. But if one argues it is simply always the case that prophets are truthful but not by definition, it could be wujudiyya. Usually it is considered necessary in kalam.

القسمة الرابعة للقضية باعتبار جهة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بالوجوب أو الجواز أو الامتناع : اعلم أن المحمول في القضية لا يخلو إما أن تكون نسبته إلى الموضوع نسبة الضروري الوجود في نفس الأمر ، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان، فإن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان ونسبته إليه نسبة الضروري الوجود، وإما أن تكون نسبته إليه نسبة الضروري العدم كقولنا : الإنسان حجر فإن الحجرية محمولة، ونسبتها إلى الإنسان نسبة الضروري العدم، وإما ألا يكون ضرورياً لا وجوده ولا عدمه كقولنا : الإنسان كاتب الإنسان ليس بكاتب ولنسم هذه النسبة مادة الحمل، فالمادة ثلاثة : الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع والقضية بهذا الاعتبار إما مطلقة وإما مقيدة، والمقيدة ما نص فيها بأن المحمول للموضوع ضروري أو ممكن أو موجود على الدوام لا بالضرورة ) . والمطلق ما لم يتعرض فيه إلى شيء من ذلك، فإن هذه الأمور زائدة على ما يقتضيه مجرّد الحمل والقضيّة الضرورية تنقسم إلى ما لا شرط فيه كقولنا : الله فإنه لم يزل ولا يزال كذلك، وإلى ما شرط فيه وجود الموضوع كقولنا : الإنسان حي، فإنه ما دام موجوداً فهو كذلك، فوجود الموضوع مشروط فيه ولا يفارق هذا المشروط الضروري الأول في جهة الضرورة، وإنما يفارق في دوام الموضوع لذاته أزلاً وأبداً ووجوب وجوده لنفس حقيقته، ولنسم هذا بالضروري المطلق. فأما الضروري المشروط فثلاثة : الأول : ما يشترط فيه دوام وجود الموضوع ومثاله ما تقدّم . الثاني : ما شرط فيه دوام كون الموضوع موصوفاً بعنوانه كقولنا : كلّ مُتحرك متغيّر، فإنه مُتغيّر ما دام مُتحرّكاً لا ما دام ذات المُتحرّك موجوداً فحسب. والفرق بين قولنا : الإنسان حي أن الشرط في الحي ذات الإنسان، والشرط ههنا ذات المُتحرّك فقط، بل ذات المُتحرّك له ذات وجوهر من كونه فرساً أو سماء أو ما شئت أن تسميه ويلحقه أنه مُتحرّك، وذاك الذات هو غير المُتحرّك، وليس الإنسان كذلك . الثالث : ما يشترط فيه وقت مخصوص إما مُعيّن أو غير مُعيّن، فإن قولنا : القمر بالضرورة مُنخسف مُقيّد بوقت مُعيّن، وهو وقت وقوعه في ظلّ الأرض محجوباً بذلك عن ضوء الشمس، وقولنا الإنسان بالضرورة متنفس فمعناه أنه في بعض الأوقات، وذلك الوقت غير مُتعيّن . (۱) فرض أرسطو على القضية إدخال ألفاظ واجب ،وممكن ،وممتنع وتجنّب الغزالي فعل ذلك . فإن قال قائل : وهل يتصوّر دائم غير ضروري؟ قلنا : نعم. أما في الأشخاص فظاهر كالزنجي، فإنك قد تقول : إنه أسود البشرة ما دام موجود البشرة وليس السواد لبشرته ضرورياً ولكنه قد اتفق وجوده لها على الدوام، ولنسم هذه القضية وجودية. وأما في الكليات فكقولنا : كلّ كوكب إما شارق أو غارب، فإنه في كل ساعة كذلك وليس ذلك ضرورياً في وجود ذاته إذ ليس كالحيوان للإنسان فافهم.
The fourth division of the proposition with respect to the mode of the relation of the predicate to the subject: by obligation, possibility, or impossibility. Know that the predicate in a proposition is either such that its relation to the subject is a relation of necessary existence in reality, like your saying: 'Man is animal', for animal is predicated of man and its relation to him is a relation of necessary existence; or such that its relation to him is a relation of necessary non-existence, like our saying: 'Man is stone', for stone-ness is predicated and its relation to man is a relation of necessary non-existence; or it is not necessary, neither its existence nor its non-existence, like our saying: 'Man is a writer', 'Man is not a writer'. And let us name this relation the 'material of predication'. So the material is three: obligation, possibility, and impossibility. And the proposition, in this respect, is either absolute or conditional. The conditional is that in which it is explicitly stated that the predicate for the subject is necessary or possible or perpetually existing without necessity. The absolute is that in which none of this is touched upon, for these matters are additional to what is required by mere predication. And the necessary proposition is divided into that which has no condition, like our saying: 'God', for He has not ceased and will not cease to be so; and into that in which the existence of the subject is conditioned, like our saying: 'Man is living', for as long as he exists, he is so. So the existence of the subject is conditioned in it, and it does not differ from the first conditioned necessary in the aspect of necessity, but only differs in the permanence of the subject in itself eternally and forever and the necessity of its existence for its own essence. And let us name this the 'absolute necessary'. As for the conditioned necessary, it is of three types: First: that in which the permanence of the subject's existence is conditioned, and its example is what preceded. Second: that in which the permanence of the subject being described by its title is conditioned, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is changing', for it is changing as long as it is moving, not as long as the essence of the moving thing exists merely. And the difference between our saying: 'Man is living' is that the condition for 'living' is the essence of man, and the condition here is only the essence of the moving thing—rather, the essence of the moving thing has an essence and substance from its being a horse or sky or whatever you wish to name it, and attached to it is that it is moving, and that essence is other than the moving thing, and man is not like that. Third: that in which a specific time is conditioned, either determinate or indeterminate. For example, our saying: 'The moon is necessarily eclipsed' is restricted to a determinate time, which is the time of its falling into the earth's shadow, being veiled thereby from the sun's light; and our saying: 'Man necessarily breathes' means that it is in some times, and that time is indeterminate. (1) Aristotle imposed on the proposition the introduction of the terms 'necessary', 'possible', and 'impossible', and al-Ghazali avoided doing that. If someone says: 'Is a perpetual non-necessary conceivable?' We say: Yes. As for individuals, it is manifest, like the dark-skinned person: you might say that he is black-skinned as long as skin exists, but blackness is not necessary for his skin, but it happens to exist in it perpetually. Let us name this proposition 'existential'. As for universals, like our saying: 'Every star is either rising or setting', for it is so at every hour, and that is not necessary in the existence of its essence, since it is not like animal for man. So understand.

The Importance and Definition of Contradiction
Author defines contradictory propositions and emphasizes their role in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of a given categorical proposition in Islamic logical contexts.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the key that unlocks indirect proofs: refute the opposite, and you prove the point.

In this passage, the author explains why understanding contradiction is essential for logical analysis. Often a proof does not directly establish a claim but rather refutes its opposite. By grasping the precise definition of contradiction, you gain a powerful tool for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of contradiction
The author states that sometimes a proof indicates something by refuting its contradictory. Why is this useful? It means if you cannot prove p directly, proving not-p is impossible can suffice.
This is a common strategy in kalam arguments.
2
Understand the definition
Two propositions are contradictory if they differ in quality (affirmation/negation) and are such that they cannot both be true or both false. That is the core logical relationship.
3
Apply the example
The author gives 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created'. Check: if one is true, the other is false. Confirm the conditions hold.
Note that the contradictory must be exact opposite in quality and have the same subject and predicate.
4
Recognize mutual entailment
The author says proving one proves the other indirectly. This is because if you prove not-p is false, p must be true. This is a powerful inferential move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسمة الخامسة للقضية باعتبار نقيضها : اعلم أن فهم النقيض في القضية تمس إليه الحاجة في النظر، فربّما لا يدلّ البرهان على شيء ولكن يدلّ على إبطال نقيضه، فيكون كأنه قد دلّ عليه وربما يوضع في مقدمات القياس شيء فلا يعرف وجه دلالته ما لم يردّ إلى نقيضه، فإذا لم يكن النقيض معلوماً لم تحصل هذه الفوائد، وربما يظنّ أن معرفة ذلك ظاهرة وليس كذلك، فإن التساهل فيه مثار الغلط في أكثر النظريات والقضيتان المتناقضتان هما المختلفتان بالإيجاب والسلب على وجه يقتضي لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة، فإنّا إذا قلنا : العالم حادث وكان صادقاً كان قولنا العالم ليس بحادث كاذباً، وكذا قولنا: قديم إذا عنينا بالقديم نفي الحادث؛ فمهما دلّلنا على أحدهما فقد دلّلنا على الآخر، ومهما قلنا: أحدهما فكأنا قد قلنا الآخر، فهما متلازمان على هذا الوجه ولكن للتناقض شروط ثمانية فإذا لم تراع الشروط لم يحصل التناقض : الأول : أن تكون إحدى القضيتين سالبة والأخرى موجبة كقولنا : العالم حادث، العالم ليس بحادث؛ فإنّا إن قلنا : العالم حادث العالم حادث، فلا يتناقضان . الثاني : : أن يكون موضوع المقدمتين واحداً فإذا تعدّد لم يتناقضا، كقولنا : العالم حادث والباري ليس بحادث فإنهما لا يتناقضان وإنما يشكل هذا لفظ في مشترك، فإنا نقول : العين ،أصفر العين ليس بأصفر، ونريد بإحداهما الدينار وبالآخر العضو الباصر. ونقول في الفقه : الصغيرة مُوَلَّى عليها في بُضْعِها»(١) الصغيرة ليس مُوَلَّى عليها في بضعها، ونريد بإحداهما الثّيِّب وبالأخرى البكر (۲) على منهاج إرادة الخاص بالعام، ويكون الموضوع متعدداً فلا يحصل التناقض . الثالث : أن يكون المحمول واحداً فإن قولنا الإنسان مخلوق، الإنسان ليس بحجر لا يتناقضان، ويشكل ذلك في المحمول المشترك، كقولنا : المُكْرَه (۱) مُولَّى عليها : أي يقوم بتزويجها وليّ مُجبر . البُضْعُ : الزواج، أو العَقْد، أو المهر، أو الفَرْج. (۲) الثَّيِّبُ : التي سبق أن تزوّجت (غير العذراء).
The fifth division of the proposition with respect to its contradictory. Know that understanding the contradictory of a proposition is needed in investigation. For perhaps a demonstration does not indicate something directly but indicates the refutation of its contradictory, so it is as if it indicated it. And perhaps something is placed in the premises of a syllogism and the way of its indication is not known unless it is referred back to its contradictory. So if the contradictory is not known, these benefits are not attained. And perhaps one thinks that knowledge of that is obvious, but it is not so, for leniency in it is a source of error in most theoretical matters. The two contradictory propositions are those that differ in affirmation and negation in such a way that, by their very nature, one of them is true and the other false. For if we say: 'The world is created' and it is true, then our saying 'The world is not created' is false, and similarly our saying '[The world is] eternal' if we mean by 'eternal' the negation of created. So whenever we prove one of them, we have proven the other, and whenever we say one of them, it is as if we have said the other. So they are mutually entailing in this respect. But there are eight conditions for contradiction; if the conditions are not observed, contradiction does not occur. The first: that one of the two propositions be negative and the other affirmative, like our saying: 'The world is created', 'The world is not created'. For if we say 'The world is created, the world is created', they do not contradict. The second: that the subject of the two propositions be one. If it is multiple, they do not contradict, like our saying: 'The world is created' and 'The Creator is not created', for they do not contradict. But this is problematic with equivocal terms, for we say: 'The eye is yellow', 'The eye is not yellow', meaning by one the dinar and by the other the seeing organ. And we say in fiqh: 'The young girl is under guardianship regarding her marriage', 'The young girl is not under guardianship regarding her marriage', meaning by one the previously married woman and by the other the virgin, following the method of intending the specific by the general. So the subject is multiple and contradiction does not occur. The third: that the predicate be one. For our saying 'Man is created', 'Man is not stone' do not contradict. And this is problematic with equivocal predicates, like our saying: 'The coerced...' (1) 'Muwallā ʿalayhā': meaning her guardian who compels her marriage. 'Al-buḍʿ': marriage, or contract, or dowry, or private parts. (2) 'Al-thayyib': one who has previously been married (non-virgin).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth division of propositions, namely with respect to their contradictories (naqīḍ). The author emphasizes the importance of understanding contradiction in logical reasoning: sometimes a proof does not directly establish a proposition but rather refutes its contradictory, which indirectly proves the proposition. Similarly, a premise in a syllogism may require conversion to its contradictory to reveal its logical force. Neglecting to properly understand contradiction leads to errors. The formal definition is then given: two propositions are contradictory if they differ in quality (affirmation vs. negation) such that, by their very nature, one must be true and the other false. The example given is 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created'. The author notes that if both are affirmative, there is no contradiction. After defining the basic principle, the author states that there are eight conditions for valid contradiction. The first three are explained with examples: (1) One must be negative, the other affirmative. (2) The subject must be the same. If the subject is equivocal (like 'al-ʿayn' meaning 'dinar' or 'eye'), the propositions may appear contradictory but are not. A fiqh example is given: 'The young girl is under guardianship regarding her marriage'—if 'young girl' refers to a thayyib (previously married) in one and a bikr (virgin) in the other, the subject is not truly one, so no contradiction. (3) The predicate must be the same. For instance, 'Man is created' and 'Man is not stone' have different predicates, so no contradiction. The passage ends mid-sentence during the third condition, noting the problem of equivocal predicates. The author provides footnotes clarifying the fiqh terms 'muwallā ʿalayhā' and 'al-thayyib'. This lesson demonstrates the precision required for logical contradiction, especially in avoiding fallacies of equivocation.

نقيض
متناقضتان

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a proposition is not just its opposite in truth value, but a proposition differing in quality such that they are mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive of truth possibilities.

The First Three Conditions for Valid Contradiction
Author lists and explains the first three of eight conditions for valid contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first three conditions for valid contradiction to identify whether two propositions are truly contradictory.

Core Idea
For a genuine contradiction, the two propositions must differ in quality and have identical subject and predicate.

After defining contradiction, the author lists eight conditions for a valid contradiction, of which the first three are explained here: quality difference, subject identity, and predicate identity. These conditions prevent fallacies of equivocation, ensuring that logical debates are precise and meaningful.

Guided Path
1
First condition: quality difference
One proposition must be affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative, they could both be true; no contradiction.
Example: 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created' satisfy this condition.
2
Second condition: subject identity
The subject term must refer to exactly the same thing in both propositions. If the subject is equivocal (same word, different meanings), no contradiction.
Author gives the example of 'al-ʿayn' (dinar vs. eye) and a fiqh example about 'young girl' (thayyib vs. bikr).
3
Third condition: predicate identity
The predicate must be the same in both propositions. 'Man is created' and 'Man is not stone' have different predicates, so they are not contradictory.
Author warns about equivocal predicates, like in the example of the coerced person's guardianship (text cut off).
4
Apply conditions to a new example
Take a proposition from Islamic law: 'Selling alcohol is prohibited'. Its contradictory must be 'Selling alcohol is not prohibited' (different quality, same subject and predicate). Check each condition.
If you encounter 'Selling alcohol is allowed', that is not the contradictory because 'allowed' is not simply the negation of 'prohibited' in a strict logical sense; the contradictory must be the direct negation of the predicate.

القسمة الخامسة للقضية باعتبار نقيضها : اعلم أن فهم النقيض في القضية تمس إليه الحاجة في النظر، فربّما لا يدلّ البرهان على شيء ولكن يدلّ على إبطال نقيضه، فيكون كأنه قد دلّ عليه وربما يوضع في مقدمات القياس شيء فلا يعرف وجه دلالته ما لم يردّ إلى نقيضه، فإذا لم يكن النقيض معلوماً لم تحصل هذه الفوائد، وربما يظنّ أن معرفة ذلك ظاهرة وليس كذلك، فإن التساهل فيه مثار الغلط في أكثر النظريات والقضيتان المتناقضتان هما المختلفتان بالإيجاب والسلب على وجه يقتضي لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة، فإنّا إذا قلنا : العالم حادث وكان صادقاً كان قولنا العالم ليس بحادث كاذباً، وكذا قولنا: قديم إذا عنينا بالقديم نفي الحادث؛ فمهما دلّلنا على أحدهما فقد دلّلنا على الآخر، ومهما قلنا: أحدهما فكأنا قد قلنا الآخر، فهما متلازمان على هذا الوجه ولكن للتناقض شروط ثمانية فإذا لم تراع الشروط لم يحصل التناقض : الأول : أن تكون إحدى القضيتين سالبة والأخرى موجبة كقولنا : العالم حادث، العالم ليس بحادث؛ فإنّا إن قلنا : العالم حادث العالم حادث، فلا يتناقضان . الثاني : : أن يكون موضوع المقدمتين واحداً فإذا تعدّد لم يتناقضا، كقولنا : العالم حادث والباري ليس بحادث فإنهما لا يتناقضان وإنما يشكل هذا لفظ في مشترك، فإنا نقول : العين ،أصفر العين ليس بأصفر، ونريد بإحداهما الدينار وبالآخر العضو الباصر. ونقول في الفقه : الصغيرة مُوَلَّى عليها في بُضْعِها»(١) الصغيرة ليس مُوَلَّى عليها في بضعها، ونريد بإحداهما الثّيِّب وبالأخرى البكر (۲) على منهاج إرادة الخاص بالعام، ويكون الموضوع متعدداً فلا يحصل التناقض . الثالث : أن يكون المحمول واحداً فإن قولنا الإنسان مخلوق، الإنسان ليس بحجر لا يتناقضان، ويشكل ذلك في المحمول المشترك، كقولنا : المُكْرَه (۱) مُولَّى عليها : أي يقوم بتزويجها وليّ مُجبر . البُضْعُ : الزواج، أو العَقْد، أو المهر، أو الفَرْج. (۲) الثَّيِّبُ : التي سبق أن تزوّجت (غير العذراء).
The fifth division of the proposition with respect to its contradictory. Know that understanding the contradictory of a proposition is needed in investigation. For perhaps a demonstration does not indicate something directly but indicates the refutation of its contradictory, so it is as if it indicated it. And perhaps something is placed in the premises of a syllogism and the way of its indication is not known unless it is referred back to its contradictory. So if the contradictory is not known, these benefits are not attained. And perhaps one thinks that knowledge of that is obvious, but it is not so, for leniency in it is a source of error in most theoretical matters. The two contradictory propositions are those that differ in affirmation and negation in such a way that, by their very nature, one of them is true and the other false. For if we say: 'The world is created' and it is true, then our saying 'The world is not created' is false, and similarly our saying '[The world is] eternal' if we mean by 'eternal' the negation of created. So whenever we prove one of them, we have proven the other, and whenever we say one of them, it is as if we have said the other. So they are mutually entailing in this respect. But there are eight conditions for contradiction; if the conditions are not observed, contradiction does not occur. The first: that one of the two propositions be negative and the other affirmative, like our saying: 'The world is created', 'The world is not created'. For if we say 'The world is created, the world is created', they do not contradict. The second: that the subject of the two propositions be one. If it is multiple, they do not contradict, like our saying: 'The world is created' and 'The Creator is not created', for they do not contradict. But this is problematic with equivocal terms, for we say: 'The eye is yellow', 'The eye is not yellow', meaning by one the dinar and by the other the seeing organ. And we say in fiqh: 'The young girl is under guardianship regarding her marriage', 'The young girl is not under guardianship regarding her marriage', meaning by one the previously married woman and by the other the virgin, following the method of intending the specific by the general. So the subject is multiple and contradiction does not occur. The third: that the predicate be one. For our saying 'Man is created', 'Man is not stone' do not contradict. And this is problematic with equivocal predicates, like our saying: 'The coerced...' (1) 'Muwallā ʿalayhā': meaning her guardian who compels her marriage. 'Al-buḍʿ': marriage, or contract, or dowry, or private parts. (2) 'Al-thayyib': one who has previously been married (non-virgin).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth division of propositions, namely with respect to their contradictories (naqīḍ). The author emphasizes the importance of understanding contradiction in logical reasoning: sometimes a proof does not directly establish a proposition but rather refutes its contradictory, which indirectly proves the proposition. Similarly, a premise in a syllogism may require conversion to its contradictory to reveal its logical force. Neglecting to properly understand contradiction leads to errors. The formal definition is then given: two propositions are contradictory if they differ in quality (affirmation vs. negation) such that, by their very nature, one must be true and the other false. The example given is 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created'. The author notes that if both are affirmative, there is no contradiction. After defining the basic principle, the author states that there are eight conditions for valid contradiction. The first three are explained with examples: (1) One must be negative, the other affirmative. (2) The subject must be the same. If the subject is equivocal (like 'al-ʿayn' meaning 'dinar' or 'eye'), the propositions may appear contradictory but are not. A fiqh example is given: 'The young girl is under guardianship regarding her marriage'—if 'young girl' refers to a thayyib (previously married) in one and a bikr (virgin) in the other, the subject is not truly one, so no contradiction. (3) The predicate must be the same. For instance, 'Man is created' and 'Man is not stone' have different predicates, so no contradiction. The passage ends mid-sentence during the third condition, noting the problem of equivocal predicates. The author provides footnotes clarifying the fiqh terms 'muwallā ʿalayhā' and 'al-thayyib'. This lesson demonstrates the precision required for logical contradiction, especially in avoiding fallacies of equivocation.

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be truly contradictory, they must differ in quality, have the same subject, and have the same predicate.

?

Six Conditions for Proper Contradiction
Author enumerates first six conditions for valid logical contradiction with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify violations of the six conditions for logical contradiction in propositions, using examples from fiqh and kalam.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than opposites — unity of subject, predicate, relation, and aspect.

Logical contradiction is a powerful tool in argumentation, but it is easily misapplied. The author of this passage stresses that understanding the precise conditions for contradiction is essential for valid reasoning. In this lesson, we will examine the first six of the eight conditions, learning how ambiguity or mismatched elements can create false contradictions. We will see examples from both everyday logic and Islamic jurisprudence to sharpen your ability to spot when two propositions truly contradict each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
In each condition, the author gives a pair of statements. Find them and note their surface form — one affirmative, one negative.
2
Check unity of quality
First condition: one must be affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, they cannot contradict.
3
Check unity of subject and predicate
Second and third conditions: the subject and predicate must be identical in meaning. Watch for ambiguous words (mushtarak) that have multiple meanings, causing a false appearance of contradiction.
Examples: 'eye' as coin vs organ; 'mukhtar' as able to refrain vs acting from desire.
4
Check unity of part and relation
Fourth condition: the predicate must apply to the same part or aspect of the subject. Fifth condition: relative terms (like 'half', 'father') must refer to the same relation.
Example: 'Nubian is white' (teeth) vs 'Nubian is not white' (skin) violates fourth condition.
5
Check unity of aspect
Sixth condition: the mode or aspect (potential vs actual, or different angles) must be the same. If the predicate is affirmed under one aspect and denied under another, there is no contradiction.
Example: 'Water gives drink potentially' vs 'actually'; Quranic verse on throwing.

القسمة الخامسة للقضية باعتبار نقيضها : اعلم أن فهم النقيض في القضية تمس إليه الحاجة في النظر، فربّما لا يدلّ البرهان على شيء ولكن يدلّ على إبطال نقيضه، فيكون كأنه قد دلّ عليه وربما يوضع في مقدمات القياس شيء فلا يعرف وجه دلالته ما لم يردّ إلى نقيضه، فإذا لم يكن النقيض معلوماً لم تحصل هذه الفوائد، وربما يظنّ أن معرفة ذلك ظاهرة وليس كذلك، فإن التساهل فيه مثار الغلط في أكثر النظريات والقضيتان عنينا بالقديم نفي الحادث؛ فمهما دلّلنا على أحدهما فقد دلّلنا على الآخر، ومهما قلنا: أحدهما فكأنا قد قلنا الآخر، فهما متلازمان على هذا الوجه ولكن للتناقض شروط ثمانية فإذا لم تراع الشروط لم يحصل التناقض : الأول : أن تكون إحدى القضيتين سالبة والأخرى موجبة كقولنا : العالم حادث، العالم ليس بحادث؛ فإنّا إن قلنا : العالم حادث العالم حادث، فلا يتناقضان . الثاني : : أن يكون موضوع المقدمتين واحداً فإذا تعدّد لم يتناقضا، كقولنا : العالم حادث والباري ليس بحادث فإنهما لا يتناقضان وإنما يشكل هذا لفظ في مشترك، فإنا نقول : العين أصفر العين ليس بأصفر، ونريد بإحداهما الدينار وبالآخر العضو الباصر. ونقول في الفقه : الصغيرة مُوَلَّى عليها في بُضْعِها الصغيرة ليس مُوَلَّى عليها في بضعها، ونريد بإحداهما الثّيِّب وبالأخرى البكر على منهاج إرادة الخاص بالعام، ويكون الموضوع متعدداً فلا يحصل التناقض . الثالث : أن يكون المحمول واحداً فإن قولنا الإنسان مخلوق، الإنسان ليس بحجر لا يتناقضان، ويشكل ذلك في المحمول المشترك، كقولنا : المُكْرَه على القتل مختار والمُكْرَه على القتل ليس بمختار ولكنه مُضطر، ولا يتناقضان فإن المختار يطلق على معنيين مختلفين فهو مشترك، فقد يُراد به القادر على الترك وقد يراد به الذي يقدم على الشيء لشهوته وانبعاث داعية من ذاته. ومهما كان اللفظ مشتركاً كان الموضوع أو المحمول أكثر من واحد في الحقيقة، وفي الظاهر يظن أنه واحد والعبرة للحقيقة لا لظاهر اللفظ الرابع : ألا يكون المحمول في جزأين مختلفين من الموضوع كقولنا : النوبي أبيض، النوبي ليس بأبيض أي هو أبيض الأسنان وليس بأبيض البشرة. وفي الفقه نقول: «السارق مقطوع السارق ليس بمقطوع أي مقطوع اليد ليس بمقطوع الرجل والأنف . الخامس : ألا يختلف ما إليه الإضافة في المضافات، كقولنا : الأربعة نصف الأربعة ليست نصفاً أي هي نصف الثمانية وليست نصف العشرة ، فلا تناقض . وكذلك قولنا : زيد أب زيد ليس بأب، أي أب لعمرو، وليس بأب لخالد. وفي الفقه نقول : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها المرأة ليس مُولّى عليها أي مُولّى في البضع لا في المال، وقد يضاف إلى البضع كلاهما ولا تناقض من جهة اشتراك لفظ المحمول، فإن أبا حنيفة (١) يقول : مُولّى عليها إذ يتولى الولي نكاحها شرعاً استحباباً أو إيجاباً، وليس مُوَلَّى عليها أي تستقل بنفسها ولا تُجْبَر على العقد. وهذه المعاني يجب مراعاتها لا للنقيض فقط، ولكن لجميع أنواع القياس أيضاً. وعلى ذلك فقول بعض فقهاء الشافعية : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها فلا تلي أمر نفسها، نتيجة غير لازمة فإن أبا حنيفة يقول : قولكم إنها مُوَلَّى عليها إن أردتم به أنها لا تلي أمر نفسها أو الولي يجبرها، فهذا عين المطلوب في محلّ النزاع فجعله مقدمة في القياس مصادرة وإن أريد به أن الولي يتولى عقدها استحباباً أو إيجاباً، فلا يلزم من هذا ألا ينعقد عقدها إذا تعاطته على خلاف الاستحباب . السادس: ألا يكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع على جهتين مختلفتين، كقولنا : الماء في الكوز مُروِ مُطهّر وليس بِمُرْوِ ولا مُطهر، ونريد أنه مُرْوِ بالقوة وليس بِمُرْوِ بالفعل، ولاختلاف جهة الحمل لم يتناقض الحكمان. ومن تعالى: ﴿وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ رَمَى (٢) وهو نفي للرمي وإثبات له، ولكن
The fifth division of the proposition with respect to its contradictory. Know that understanding the contradictory in a proposition is needed in consideration. Sometimes a proof does not indicate something but indicates the refutation of its contradictory, so it is as if it indicated it. And sometimes something is placed in the premises of a syllogism and its manner of indication is not known until it is reduced to its contradictory. So if the contradictory is not known, these benefits are not obtained. And sometimes it is thought that knowledge of that is obvious, but it is not; for leniency in it is a source of error in most theoretical matters. The two contradictory propositions are the two that differ in affirmation and negation in a manner that requires by themselves that one be true and the other false. For if we say: The world is originated, and it is true, then our statement: The world is not originated is false. Similarly our statement: It is eternal, if we mean by eternal the negation of originated. So whenever we prove one, we have proven the other, and whenever we say one, it is as if we have said the other; they are mutually entailing in this manner. But contradiction has eight conditions. If the conditions are not observed, contradiction does not occur. First: One of the two propositions must be negative and the other affirmative, like our statement: The world is originated, the world is not originated; for if we say: The world is originated, the world is originated, they do not contradict. Second: The subject of the two premises must be one; if it is multiple, they do not contradict, like our statement: The world is originated, and the Creator is not originated, for they are not contradictory. This is problematic in a ambiguous word (mushtarak), so we say: The eye is yellow, the eye is not yellow, meaning by one the dinar and by the other the seeing organ. And we say in fiqh: The minor girl is under guardianship regarding her marriage, the minor girl is not under guardianship regarding her marriage, meaning by one the non-virgin and by the other the virgin, following the method of intending the specific by the general, and the subject is multiple, so contradiction is not obtained. Third: The predicate must be one, for our statement: Man is created, man is not a stone, are not contradictory. This is problematic in a ambiguous predicate, like our statement: The person coerced to kill is a free agent (mukhtar), the person coerced to kill is not a free agent but compelled, and they are not contradictory for 'mukhtar' is applied to two different meanings—it is ambiguous: sometimes it means able to refrain, sometimes it means one who does something out of desire and internal motivation. When the word is ambiguous, the subject or predicate is more than one in reality, while in appearance it is thought to be one, and consideration is for reality, not the apparent wording. Fourth: The predicate must not be from two different parts of the subject, like our statement: The Nubian is white, the Nubian is not white, meaning he is white in teeth and not white in skin. And in fiqh: The thief is amputated, the thief is not amputated, meaning amputated in hand not in foot or nose. Fifth: The relation (idafa) in relative terms must not differ, like our statement: Four is half, four is not half, meaning half of eight and not half of ten, so no contradiction. Similarly: Zayd is a father, Zayd is not a father, meaning father of Amr and not father of Khalid. And in fiqh: The woman is under guardianship, the woman is not under guardianship, meaning under guardianship in marriage (al-bud' ) not in property; and it may be added to marriage in both, and there is no contradiction due to the ambiguity of the predicate, for Abu Hanifa says she is under guardianship because the guardian conducts her marriage legally, recommendably or obligatorily, and she is not under guardianship meaning she is independent and not forced into contract. These meanings must be observed not only for contradiction but for all types of syllogism. Accordingly, the statement of some Shafi'i jurists: The woman is under guardianship so she does not manage her own affairs, is a non-necessary conclusion, for Abu Hanifa says: Your statement that she is under guardianship, if you mean that she does not manage her own affairs or the guardian compels her, then that is exactly the disputed issue, so making it a premise in the syllogism is a petition (musadara); and if you mean that the guardian conducts her contract recommendably or obligatorily, then it does not follow that her contract is invalid if she undertakes it contrary to recommendation. Sixth: The proportion of the predicate to the subject must not be from two different aspects (jiha), like our statement: Water in the jug is water-giving and purifying, and it is not water-giving nor purifying, meaning it is water-giving potentially and not water-giving actually; due to difference of aspect, the two judgments do not contradict. And from the Exalted: 'And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' (8:17), which is negation and affirmation of throwing, but...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the logical conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ). The author emphasizes the importance of understanding contradiction for proofs and syllogisms. He defines contradictory propositions as differing in affirmation and negation such that one is necessarily true and the other false. Then he lists six of the eight conditions. Each condition is illustrated with examples from both general logic and Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh). The first condition requires one affirmative and one negative. The second requires the same subject; ambiguity in subject (like 'eye' meaning coin or organ) can cause false contradiction. The third requires the same predicate; ambiguous predicates like 'mukhtar' cause confusion. The fourth requires the predicate not to be from different parts of the subject (e.g., white teeth vs white skin). The fifth requires the same relation in relative terms (e.g., half of a specific number). The sixth requires the same aspect (potential vs actual). The author notes that these conditions are essential not only for contradiction but for all syllogistic reasoning. He uses a fiqh example to show how ignoring conditions leads to a flawed argument by the Shafi'is against Abu Hanifa. The final example from the Quran illustrates how a statement can be both affirmed and negated under different aspects without contradiction.

التناقض
مشترك

C) Fourth condition (predicate not from two parts of subject)

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be genuine contradictories, they must differ in quality (affirmation/negation) and agree in subject, predicate, relation, part, and aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
