Equivocal Terms and Partial Predicates
Author shows how equivocal terms and partial predicates prevent genuine contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two statements are not genuinely contradictory due to equivocal terms or predicates applying to different parts of the subject.

Core Idea
Contradiction fails when words have multiple meanings or predicates attach to different parts.

The author begins by warning us that apparent contradictions often dissolve when we realize that a word (mushtarak) carries two meanings, or that a predicate applies only to part of the subject. In this lesson, we dissect these two pitfalls using the author's own examples from fiqh and everyday language.

Guided Path
1
Recognize equivocal (mushtarak) terms
Examine the word 'free agent' (mukhtār) in the killer example. The author notes it can mean 'able to refrain' or 'acting on desire'. Two statements using 'free agent' in different senses are not contradictory.
Also note: the author says consideration is for reality, not the apparent wording.
2
Analyze the Nubian example
Read 'The Nubian is white, the Nubian is not white'. Identify that 'white' refers to teeth in one and skin in the other. The predicate is attached to different parts of the subject.
This is the fourth condition.
3
Apply to the fiqh thief example
The statements 'The thief is amputated' and 'The thief is not amputated' are both true if the first means 'amputated of hand' and the second means 'not amputated of foot'. Check how this matches the fourth condition.
This illustrates how ignoring part-specific predicates leads to false attributions of contradiction.
4
Conclude: conditions for genuine contradiction
Summarize: for a real contradiction, there must be no equivocation and the predicate must apply to the entire subject identically. Otherwise, both statements can be true.

على القتل مختار والمُكْرَه على القتل ليس بمختار ولكنه مُضطر، ولا يتناقضان فإن المختار يطلق على معنيين مختلفين فهو مشترك، فقد يُراد به القادر على الترك وقد يراد به الذي يقدم على الشيء لشهوته وانبعاث داعية من ذاته. ومهما كان اللفظ مشتركاً كان الموضوع أو المحمول أكثر من واحد في الحقيقة، وفي الظاهر يظن أنه واحد والعبرة للحقيقة لا لظاهر اللفظ.
الرابع : ألا يكون المحمول في جزأين مختلفين من الموضوع كقولنا : النوبي أبيض، النوبي ليس بأبيض أي هو أبيض الأسنان وليس بأبيض البشرة. وفي الفقه نقول: «السارق مقطوع السارق ليس بمقطوع أي مقطوع اليد ليس بمقطوع الرجل والأنف.
الخامس : ألا يختلف ما إليه الإضافة في المضافات، كقولنا : الأربعة نصف الأربعة ليست نصفاً أي هي نصف الثمانية وليست نصف العشرة ، فلا تناقض . وكذلك قولنا : زيد أب زيد ليس بأب، أي أب لعمرو، وليس بأب لخالد. وفي الفقه نقول : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها المرأة ليس مُولّى عليها» أي مُولَّى في البضع لا في المال، وقد يضاف إلى البضع كلاهما ولا تناقض من جهة اشتراك لفظ المحمول، فإن أبا حنيفة (١) يقول : مُولّى عليها إذ يتولى الولي نكاحها شرعاً استحباباً أو إيجاباً، وليس مُوَلَّى عليها أي تستقل بنفسها ولا تُجْبَر على العقد. وهذه المعاني يجب مراعاتها لا للنقيض فقط، ولكن لجميع أنواع القياس أيضاً. وعلى ذلك فقول بعض فقهاء الشافعية : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها فلا تلي أمر نفسها، نتيجة غير لازمة فإن أبا حنيفة يقول : قولكم إنها مُوَلَّى عليها إن أردتم به أنها لا تلي أمر نفسها أو الولي يجبرها، فهذا عين المطلوب في محلّ النزاع فجعله مقدمة في القياس مصادرة وإن أريد به أن الولي يتولى عقدها استحباباً أو إيجاباً، فلا يلزم من هذا ألا ينعقد عقدها إذا تعاطته على خلاف الاستحباب .
السادس: ألا يكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع على جهتين مختلفتين، كقولنا : الماء في الكوز مُروِ مُطهّر وليس بِمُرْوِ ولا مُطهر، ونريد أنه مُرْوِ بالقوة وليس بِمُرْوِ بالفعل، ولاختلاف جهة الحمل لم يتناقض الحكمان. ومن تعالى: ﴿وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ رَمَى﴾ وهو نفي للرمي وإثبات له، ولكن لیست جهة النفي جهة الإثبات فلم يتناقضا، وهذا أيضاً مما يغلط كثيراً في الفقهيات .
السابع : ألا يكون في زمانين مختلفين كقولنا : الصبي له أسنان، ونعني به بعد الفطام والصبي لا أسنان له ونعني به في أول الأمر . ونقول في الفقه : الخمر كانت حراماً، ونعني به في الأعصار السابقة، وكانت خلالاً، ونعني به قبل نزول التحريم. وبالجملة ينبغي ألا تخالف إحدى القضيتين الأخرى إلا في الكيف فقط، فتسلب إحداهما ما أوجبته الأخرى على الوجه الذي أوجبته. وعن الموضوع الذي وضعته بعينه على ذلك النحو وفي ذلك الوقت وبتلك الجهة فإذ ذاك يقتسمان الصدق والكذب، فإن تخلّف شرط جاز أن يشتركا في الصدق أو في الكذب.
الثامن : وهذا في القضية التي موضوعها كلّي على الخصوص، فإنه يزيد في التي موضوعها كلِّي أن تختلف القضيتان بالجزئية والكلية مع الاختلاف في السلب والإيجاب، حتى يلزم التناقض لا محالة، وإلا أمكن أن تصدقا جميعاً كالجزئيتين في مادة الإمكان مثل قولنا بعض الناس كاتب بعض الناس ليس بكاتب، وربما كذبتا جميعاً كالكليتين في مادة الإمكان كقولنا : كلّ إنسان كاتب وليس واحد من الناس كاتباً؛ فالتناقض إنما يتم في المحصورات بعد الشروط التي ذكرناها إن كانت إحدى القضيتين كلّية والأخرى جزئية، ليكون تناقضها ضرورياً، ولنمتحن المواد كلّها ولنضع الموجبة أولاً كلّية فنقول : كلّ إنسان حيوان، ليس بعض الناس بحيوان؛ كلّ إنسان كاتب ليس بعض الناس بكاتب ; كلّ إنسان حجر، ليس بعض الناس بحجر ; فنجد لا محالة إحدى القضيتين صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. ولنمتحن السالبة الكلّية فنقول : ليس واحد من الناس حيواناً بعض الناس حيوان؛ ليس واحد من الناس بحجر بعض الناس حجر ; ليس واحد من الناس بكاتب بعض الناس كاتب ; فبالضرورة تقتسمان الصدق والكذب في جميع المواد. فإن قيل: فالكليتان في مادة الوجوب والامتناع أيضاً تقتسمان الصدق والكذب قلنا: نعم ولكن لا يعرف ذلك إلا بعد معرفة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع أنه ضروري أم لا . وإذا راعيت الشرط الذي ذكرناه علمت التناقض قطعاً، وإن لم تعرف تلك النسبة فإنه كيفما كان الأمر يلزم التناقض .
Concerning the killer: he is a free agent, and the one coerced to kill is not a free agent but is compelled, and they do not contradict each other because 'free agent' is applied to two different meanings, so it is equivocal. It may mean one who is able to refrain, or one who acts upon his desire and an impulse from his own self. Whenever the word is equivocal, the subject or predicate is more than one in reality, while in appearance it is thought to be one; and consideration is for reality, not for the apparent wording.
Fourth: That the predicate not be in two different parts of the subject, as when we say: 'The Nubian is white, the Nubian is not white' meaning he is white of teeth and not white of skin. In fiqh we say: 'The thief is amputated, the thief is not amputated' meaning amputated of hand, not amputated of foot and nose.
Fifth: That the relation differ in relational terms, as when we say: 'Four is half, four is not half' meaning it is half of eight and not half of ten, so there is no contradiction. And similarly our saying: 'Zayd is a father, Zayd is not a father' meaning father of Amr and not father of Khalid. In fiqh we say: 'The woman is under guardianship, the woman is not under guardianship' meaning under guardianship in marriage, not in property; and both may be added to marriage, and there is no contradiction due to the equivocality of the predicate term. For Abu Hanifa says: She is under guardianship in that the guardian contracts her marriage according to law, as a recommendation or obligation; and she is not under guardianship in that she acts independently and is not forced into the contract. These meanings must be observed not only for contradiction but for all types of syllogism as well. Accordingly, the statement of some Shafi'i jurists: 'The woman is under guardianship, so she does not manage her own affairs' is a non-necessary conclusion, for Abu Hanifa says: Your statement that she is under guardianship — if you mean thereby that she does not manage her own affairs or that the guardian compels her, then that is exactly the point at issue, so making it a premise in the syllogism is a petition principii; and if it is meant that the guardian contracts her marriage as a recommendation or obligation, then it does not follow from this that her contract is not valid if she performs it contrary to the recommendation.
Sixth: That the predication not be under two different aspects, as when we say: 'The water in the jug is quenchant and purifying, and it is not quenchant nor purifying' meaning it is quenchant potentially, not actually; and due to the difference in aspect of predication, the two judgments do not contradict. And from the Most High: 'And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' which is a negation of throwing and an affirmation of it, but the aspect of negation is not the aspect of affirmation, so they are not contradictory. This is also something often mistaken in fiqh matters.
Seventh: That it not be in two different times, as when we say: 'The child has teeth' meaning after weaning, and 'The child has no teeth' meaning at the beginning. And we say in fiqh: 'Wine was forbidden' meaning in later periods, and 'It was permissible' meaning before the revelation of prohibition. In summary, one of the two propositions should not differ from the other except in quality only, so that one negates what the other affirms exactly in the manner it affirmed it, and concerning the very subject it posited, in that manner, in that time, and under that aspect; then they share truth and falsity. If any condition is lacking, it is possible for them to share in truth or falsity.
Eighth: This concerns the proposition whose subject is universal specifically; for in the case of a universal subject, it is additionally required that the two propositions differ in particularity and universality along with difference in negation and affirmation, so that contradiction necessarily follows; otherwise it is possible for them both to be true — like two particular propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Some people are writers, some people are not writers' — and perhaps both are false, like two universal propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Every human is a writer, and no human is a writer.' Thus contradiction is only completed in quantified propositions after the conditions we have mentioned, provided that one of the two propositions is universal and the other particular, so that their contradiction is necessary. Let us test all matters: let us first posit the affirmative universal and say: 'Every human is an animal; not some humans are animals'... 'Every human is a writer; not some humans are writers'... 'Every human is a stone; not some humans are stones' — we find invariably that one of the two propositions is true and the other false. And let us test the universal negative and say: 'No human is an animal; some humans are animals'... 'No human is a stone; some humans are stones'... 'No human is a writer; some humans are writers' — of necessity they share truth and falsity in all matters. For if it is said: 'The two universal propositions in the matter of necessity and impossibility also share truth and falsity,' we say: Yes, but that is only known after knowing the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether it is necessary or not. And if you observe the condition we mentioned, you know the contradiction definitely, even if you do not know that relation; for regardless of how it is, contradiction follows.

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions that must be met for two propositions to be genuine contradictories (tanāquḍ). The author lists several pitfalls where people mistakenly think two statements contradict when in fact they do not. Each condition is illustrated with examples from everyday language and from Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh).

The first condition (implied as 'third' in the sequence) concerns equivocal (mushtarak) terms: if a word has multiple meanings, two statements using it may appear contradictory but are not if they refer to different senses. For example, 'the killer is free agent' and 'the coerced killer is not free agent' are not contradictory because 'free agent' can mean able to refrain or acting on desire.

The fourth condition: the predicate must not apply to different parts of the subject. Example: 'The Nubian is white' (in teeth) and 'the Nubian is not white' (in skin) are both true. In fiqh, 'the thief is amputated' (hand) and 'the thief is not amputated' (foot and nose) are not contradictory.

The fifth condition: in relational terms (muḍāfāt), the relation must be the same. 'Four is half' (of eight) and 'four is not half' (of ten) are both true. In fiqh, 'the woman is under guardianship' (in marriage) and 'the woman is not under guardianship' (in property) are not contradictory. The author notes that the Hanafi position distinguishes between types of guardianship, so both statements can be true.

The sixth condition: the predicate must not be attributed under different aspects (jiha). Water in a jug can be 'quenchant potentially' but 'not quenchant actually'. The Quranic verse about throwing (رمي) is given: Allah negates the Prophet's throwing in one aspect but affirms it in another.

The seventh condition: time must be the same. A child has teeth after weaning but not before. Wine was forbidden after the revelation but permissible before.

The eighth condition applies specifically to propositions with universal subjects. For genuine contradiction, one proposition must be universal and the other particular (with opposite quality). The author tests all matter-combinations (possible, necessary, impossible) and shows that universal and particular contradictories always divide truth and falsity. If both are universal or both particular, they can both be true or both false in possible matters.

The key takeaway: for true contradiction, the two propositions must be identical in subject, predicate, time, aspect, and relation, and differ only in quality (affirmation/negation). For universal propositions, they must also differ in quantity (universal/particular).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do 'the Nubian is white' and 'the Nubian is not white' not form a contradiction?
A) Because 'white' is an equivocal term.
B) Because the predicate applies to different parts of the subject.
C) Because the subject is vague.
D) Because the time is different.

Key Takeaway
A genuine contradiction requires that the subject and predicate be identical in all respects except quality; equivocal terms and partial predicates destroy that identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'The milk is pure' and 'The milk is not pure' may appear contradictory. Identify a possible equivocality (e.g., pure in the sense of religiously lawful vs. clean) that could make both true.
?
In a fiqh discussion, someone claims: 'The adulterer is flogged' and 'The adulterer is not flogged' are contradictory. Using the fourth condition, explain how both could be true if the first refers to flogging for unmarried offenders and the second for married offenders (who are stoned).

Relational Terms and Different Aspects
Author explains how different relations and aspects prevent contradiction, with fiqh and Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize when two statements are not contradictory because they involve different relations (muḍāfāt) or different aspects (jiha) of predication.

Core Idea
Contradiction dissolves when the relation changes or the predicate is considered under different aspects.

The author continues with two more conditions: the relation (iḍāfa) must be identical, and the aspect (jiha) of predication must be the same. He illustrates with mathematical, legal, and Quranic examples. This lesson walks through each condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify relational terms (muḍāfāt)
Look at 'four is half, four is not half'. Both are true if 'half' refers to different wholes (half of eight vs. half of ten). Relational terms depend on the complement.
The fiqh example: 'the woman is under guardianship' vs. 'the woman is not under guardianship' depending on whether it is about marriage or property.
2
Examine the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i debate
The author reports that some Shafi'is claim 'the woman is under guardianship, so she does not manage her own affairs'. Abu Hanifa points out that if 'guardianship' means compulsion, it is exactly the disputed point (begging the question); if it means recommendation, the conclusion does not follow.
This shows how relational equivocation can affect syllogistic reasoning.
3
Understand the aspect condition (jiha)
The example: water in a jug is 'quenchant potentially' but not 'actually'. The Quranic verse 'You did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' negates the throw in one aspect (human agency) and affirms it in another (divine agency).
Difference in aspect prevents contradiction.
4
Apply the conditions
Given any pair of statements that seem contradictory, first check if the relation is the same. Then check if the aspect of predication is identical. If either differs, no contradiction.

على القتل مختار والمُكْرَه على القتل ليس بمختار ولكنه مُضطر، ولا يتناقضان فإن المختار يطلق على معنيين مختلفين فهو مشترك، فقد يُراد به القادر على الترك وقد يراد به الذي يقدم على الشيء لشهوته وانبعاث داعية من ذاته. ومهما كان اللفظ مشتركاً كان الموضوع أو المحمول أكثر من واحد في الحقيقة، وفي الظاهر يظن أنه واحد والعبرة للحقيقة لا لظاهر اللفظ.

السادس: ألا يكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع على جهتين مختلفتين، كقولنا : الماء في الكوز مُروِ مُطهّر وليس بِمُرْوِ ولا مُطهر، ونريد أنه مُرْوِ بالقوة وليس بِمُرْوِ بالفعل، ولاختلاف جهة الحمل لم يتناقض الحكمان. ومن تعالى: ﴿وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ رَمَى﴾ وهو نفي للرمي وإثبات له، ولكن لیست جهة النفي جهة الإثبات فلم يتناقضا، وهذا أيضاً مما يغلط كثيراً في الفقهيات .
السابع : ألا يكون في زمانين مختلفين كقولنا : الصبي له أسنان، ونعني به بعد الفطام والصبي لا أسنان له ونعني به في أول الأمر . ونقول في الفقه : الخمر كانت حراماً، ونعني به في الأعصار السابقة، وكانت خلالاً، ونعني به قبل نزول التحريم. وبالجملة ينبغي ألا تخالف إحدى القضيتين الأخرى إلا في الكيف فقط، فتسلب إحداهما ما أوجبته الأخرى على الوجه الذي أوجبته. وعن الموضوع الذي وضعته بعينه على ذلك النحو وفي ذلك الوقت وبتلك الجهة فإذ ذاك يقتسمان الصدق والكذب، فإن تخلّف شرط جاز أن يشتركا في الصدق أو في الكذب.

Concerning the killer: he is a free agent, and the one coerced to kill is not a free agent but is compelled, and they do not contradict each other because 'free agent' is applied to two different meanings, so it is equivocal. It may mean one who is able to refrain, or one who acts upon his desire and an impulse from his own self. Whenever the word is equivocal, the subject or predicate is more than one in reality, while in appearance it is thought to be one; and consideration is for reality, not for the apparent wording.
Fourth: That the predicate not be in two different parts of the subject, as when we say: 'The Nubian is white, the Nubian is not white' meaning he is white of teeth and not white of skin. In fiqh we say: 'The thief is amputated, the thief is not amputated' meaning amputated of hand, not amputated of foot and nose.
Fifth: That the relation differ in relational terms, as when we say: 'Four is half, four is not half' meaning it is half of eight and not half of ten, so there is no contradiction. And similarly our saying: 'Zayd is a father, Zayd is not a father' meaning father of Amr and not father of Khalid. In fiqh we say: 'The woman is under guardianship, the woman is not under guardianship' meaning under guardianship in marriage, not in property; and both may be added to marriage, and there is no contradiction due to the equivocality of the predicate term. For Abu Hanifa says: She is under guardianship in that the guardian contracts her marriage according to law, as a recommendation or obligation; and she is not under guardianship in that she acts independently and is not forced into the contract. These meanings must be observed not only for contradiction but for all types of syllogism as well. Accordingly, the statement of some Shafi'i jurists: 'The woman is under guardianship, so she does not manage her own affairs' is a non-necessary conclusion, for Abu Hanifa says: Your statement that she is under guardianship — if you mean thereby that she does not manage her own affairs or that the guardian compels her, then that is exactly the point at issue, so making it a premise in the syllogism is a petition principii; and if it is meant that the guardian contracts her marriage as a recommendation or obligation, then it does not follow from this that her contract is not valid if she performs it contrary to the recommendation.
Sixth: That the predication not be under two different aspects, as when we say: 'The water in the jug is quenchant and purifying, and it is not quenchant nor purifying' meaning it is quenchant potentially, not actually; and due to the difference in aspect of predication, the two judgments do not contradict. And from the Most High: 'And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' which is a negation of throwing and an affirmation of it, but the aspect of negation is not the aspect of affirmation, so they are not contradictory. This is also something often mistaken in fiqh matters.
Seventh: That it not be in two different times, as when we say: 'The child has teeth' meaning after weaning, and 'The child has no teeth' meaning at the beginning. And we say in fiqh: 'Wine was forbidden' meaning in later periods, and 'It was permissible' meaning before the revelation of prohibition. In summary, one of the two propositions should not differ from the other except in quality only, so that one negates what the other affirms exactly in the manner it affirmed it, and concerning the very subject it posited, in that manner, in that time, and under that aspect; then they share truth and falsity. If any condition is lacking, it is possible for them to share in truth or falsity.
Eighth: This concerns the proposition whose subject is universal specifically; for in the case of a universal subject, it is additionally required that the two propositions differ in particularity and universality along with difference in negation and affirmation, so that contradiction necessarily follows; otherwise it is possible for them both to be true — like two particular propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Some people are writers, some people are not writers' — and perhaps both are false, like two universal propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Every human is a writer, and no human is a writer.' Thus contradiction is only completed in quantified propositions after the conditions we have mentioned, provided that one of the two propositions is universal and the other particular, so that their contradiction is necessary. Let us test all matters: let us first posit the affirmative universal and say: 'Every human is an animal; not some humans are animals'... 'Every human is a writer; not some humans are writers'... 'Every human is a stone; not some humans are stones' — we find invariably that one of the two propositions is true and the other false. And let us test the universal negative and say: 'No human is an animal; some humans are animals'... 'No human is a stone; some humans are stones'... 'No human is a writer; some humans are writers' — of necessity they share truth and falsity in all matters. For if it is said: 'The two universal propositions in the matter of necessity and impossibility also share truth and falsity,' we say: Yes, but that is only known after knowing the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether it is necessary or not. And if you observe the condition we mentioned, you know the contradiction definitely, even if you do not know that relation; for regardless of how it is, contradiction follows.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions that must be met for two propositions to be genuine contradictories (tanāquḍ). The author lists several pitfalls where people mistakenly think two statements contradict when in fact they do not. Each condition is illustrated with examples from everyday language and from Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh).

The first condition (implied as 'third' in the sequence) concerns equivocal (mushtarak) terms: if a word has multiple meanings, two statements using it may appear contradictory but are not if they refer to different senses. For example, 'the killer is free agent' and 'the coerced killer is not free agent' are not contradictory because 'free agent' can mean able to refrain or acting on desire.

The fourth condition: the predicate must not apply to different parts of the subject. Example: 'The Nubian is white' (in teeth) and 'the Nubian is not white' (in skin) are both true. In fiqh, 'the thief is amputated' (hand) and 'the thief is not amputated' (foot and nose) are not contradictory.

The fifth condition: in relational terms (muḍāfāt), the relation must be the same. 'Four is half' (of eight) and 'four is not half' (of ten) are both true. In fiqh, 'the woman is under guardianship' (in marriage) and 'the woman is not under guardianship' (in property) are not contradictory. The author notes that the Hanafi position distinguishes between types of guardianship, so both statements can be true.

The sixth condition: the predicate must not be attributed under different aspects (jiha). Water in a jug can be 'quenchant potentially' but 'not quenchant actually'. The Quranic verse about throwing (رمي) is given: Allah negates the Prophet's throwing in one aspect but affirms it in another.

The seventh condition: time must be the same. A child has teeth after weaning but not before. Wine was forbidden after the revelation but permissible before.

The eighth condition applies specifically to propositions with universal subjects. For genuine contradiction, one proposition must be universal and the other particular (with opposite quality). The author tests all matter-combinations (possible, necessary, impossible) and shows that universal and particular contradictories always divide truth and falsity. If both are universal or both particular, they can both be true or both false in possible matters.

The key takeaway: for true contradiction, the two propositions must be identical in subject, predicate, time, aspect, and relation, and differ only in quality (affirmation/negation). For universal propositions, they must also differ in quantity (universal/particular).

Time and Quantification in Contradiction
Author explains the conditions of time and quantification for genuine contradiction, with thorough testing of logical materials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two propositions are truly contradictory by checking identity of time and, for quantified propositions, the requirement of one universal and one particular.

Core Idea
Same time and, for universals, opposite quantities are essential for contradiction.

The author concludes his conditions with two more: propositions must refer to the same time, and quantified propositions must differ in quantity (universal vs. particular) along with quality. He tests all combinations of matters (possible, necessary, impossible) to show that only universal-particular pairs always divide truth and falsity.

Guided Path
1
Check time identity
Examine the child teeth example: 'The child has teeth' after weaning, 'The child has no teeth' before. Different times allow both true. Similarly, wine was permissible before the prohibition and forbidden after.
This is the seventh condition.
2
Understand the summary condition
The author states that the two propositions should differ only in quality (affirmation/negation) while sharing subject, predicate, time, aspect, and relation. Only then do they share truth and falsity.
If any condition is missing, they could both be true or both false.
3
Examine the eighth condition on quantified propositions
For universal subjects, additionally require one universal and one particular with opposite quality. Test with examples: 'Every human is an animal' vs. 'Not some humans are animals' (impossible), etc. The author shows that these pairs always have opposite truth values.
Test all three matters: possible, necessary, impossible.
4
Distinguish from other quantifier combinations
Note that two universal propositions in possible matter can both be false (e.g., 'Every human is a writer' and 'No human is a writer'), and two particular propositions can both be true (e.g., 'Some people are writers' and 'Some people are not writers'). Only universal-particular pairs guarantee contradiction.
The author notes that two universals in necessary matters also divide truth, but that requires knowing the modal status.

على القتل مختار والمُكْرَه على القتل ليس بمختار ولكنه مُضطر، ولا يتناقضان فإن المختار يطلق على معنيين مختلفين فهو مشترك، فقد يُراد به القادر على الترك وقد يراد به الذي يقدم على الشيء لشهوته وانبعاث داعية من ذاته. ومهما كان اللفظ مشتركاً كان الموضوع أو المحمول أكثر من واحد في الحقيقة، وفي الظاهر يظن أنه واحد والعبرة للحقيقة لا لظاهر اللفظ.
الرابع : ألا يكون المحمول في جزأين مختلفين من الموضوع كقولنا : النوبي أبيض، النوبي ليس بأبيض أي هو أبيض الأسنان وليس بأبيض البشرة. وفي الفقه نقول: «السارق مقطوع السارق ليس بمقطوع أي مقطوع اليد ليس بمقطوع الرجل والأنف.
الخامس : ألا يختلف ما إليه الإضافة في المضافات، كقولنا : الأربعة نصف الأربعة ليست نصفاً أي هي نصف الثمانية وليست نصف العشرة ، فلا تناقض . وكذلك قولنا : زيد أب زيد ليس بأب، أي أب لعمرو، وليس بأب لخالد. وفي الفقه نقول : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها المرأة ليس مُولّى عليها» أي مُولَّى في البضع لا في المال، وقد يضاف إلى البضع كلاهما ولا تناقض من جهة اشتراك لفظ المحمول، فإن أبا حنيفة (١) يقول : مُولّى عليها إذ يتولى الولي نكاحها شرعاً استحباباً أو إيجاباً، وليس مُوَلَّى عليها أي تستقل بنفسها ولا تُجْبَر على العقد. وهذه المعاني يجب مراعاتها لا للنقيض فقط، ولكن لجميع أنواع القياس أيضاً. وعلى ذلك فقول بعض فقهاء الشافعية : المرأة مُوَلَّى عليها فلا تلي أمر نفسها، نتيجة غير لازمة فإن أبا حنيفة يقول : قولكم إنها مُوَلَّى عليها إن أردتم به أنها لا تلي أمر نفسها أو الولي يجبرها، فهذا عين المطلوب في محلّ النزاع فجعله مقدمة في القياس مصادرة وإن أريد به أن الولي يتولى عقدها استحباباً أو إيجاباً، فلا يلزم من هذا ألا ينعقد عقدها إذا تعاطته على خلاف الاستحباب .
السادس: ألا يكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع على جهتين مختلفتين، كقولنا : الماء في الكوز مُروِ مُطهّر وليس بِمُرْوِ ولا مُطهر، ونريد أنه مُرْوِ بالقوة وليس بِمُرْوِ بالفعل، ولاختلاف جهة الحمل لم يتناقض الحكمان. ومن تعالى: ﴿وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ رَمَى﴾ وهو نفي للرمي وإثبات له، ولكن لیست جهة النفي جهة الإثبات فلم يتناقضا، وهذا أيضاً مما يغلط كثيراً في الفقهيات .
السابع : ألا يكون في زمانين مختلفين كقولنا : الصبي له أسنان، ونعني به بعد الفطام والصبي لا أسنان له ونعني به في أول الأمر . ونقول في الفقه : الخمر كانت حراماً، ونعني به في الأعصار السابقة، وكانت خلالاً، ونعني به قبل نزول التحريم. وبالجملة ينبغي ألا تخالف إحدى القضيتين الأخرى إلا في الكيف فقط، فتسلب إحداهما ما أوجبته الأخرى على الوجه الذي أوجبته. وعن الموضوع الذي وضعته بعينه على ذلك النحو وفي ذلك الوقت وبتلك الجهة فإذ ذاك يقتسمان الصدق والكذب، فإن تخلّف شرط جاز أن يشتركا في الصدق أو في الكذب.
الثامن : وهذا في القضية التي موضوعها كلّي على الخصوص، فإنه يزيد في التي موضوعها كلِّي أن تختلف القضيتان بالجزئية والكلية مع الاختلاف في السلب والإيجاب، حتى يلزم التناقض لا محالة، وإلا أمكن أن تصدقا جميعاً كالجزئيتين في مادة الإمكان مثل قولنا بعض الناس كاتب بعض الناس ليس بكاتب، وربما كذبتا جميعاً كالكليتين في مادة الإمكان كقولنا : كلّ إنسان كاتب وليس واحد من الناس كاتباً؛ فالتناقض إنما يتم في المحصورات بعد الشروط التي ذكرناها إن كانت إحدى القضيتين كلّية والأخرى جزئية، ليكون تناقضها ضرورياً، ولنمتحن المواد كلّها ولنضع الموجبة أولاً كلّية فنقول : كلّ إنسان حيوان، ليس بعض الناس بحيوان؛ كلّ إنسان كاتب ليس بعض الناس بكاتب ; كلّ إنسان حجر، ليس بعض الناس بحجر ; فنجد لا محالة إحدى القضيتين صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. ولنمتحن السالبة الكلّية فنقول : ليس واحد من الناس حيواناً بعض الناس حيوان؛ ليس واحد من الناس بحجر بعض الناس حجر ; ليس واحد من الناس بكاتب بعض الناس كاتب ; فبالضرورة تقتسمان الصدق والكذب في جميع المواد. فإن قيل: فالكليتان في مادة الوجوب والامتناع أيضاً تقتسمان الصدق والكذب قلنا: نعم ولكن لا يعرف ذلك إلا بعد معرفة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع أنه ضروري أم لا . وإذا راعيت الشرط الذي ذكرناه علمت التناقض قطعاً، وإن لم تعرف تلك النسبة فإنه كيفما كان الأمر يلزم التناقض .
Concerning the killer: he is a free agent, and the one coerced to kill is not a free agent but is compelled, and they do not contradict each other because 'free agent' is applied to two different meanings, so it is equivocal. It may mean one who is able to refrain, or one who acts upon his desire and an impulse from his own self. Whenever the word is equivocal, the subject or predicate is more than one in reality, while in appearance it is thought to be one; and consideration is for reality, not for the apparent wording.
Fourth: That the predicate not be in two different parts of the subject, as when we say: 'The Nubian is white, the Nubian is not white' meaning he is white of teeth and not white of skin. In fiqh we say: 'The thief is amputated, the thief is not amputated' meaning amputated of hand, not amputated of foot and nose.
Fifth: That the relation differ in relational terms, as when we say: 'Four is half, four is not half' meaning it is half of eight and not half of ten, so there is no contradiction. And similarly our saying: 'Zayd is a father, Zayd is not a father' meaning father of Amr and not father of Khalid. In fiqh we say: 'The woman is under guardianship, the woman is not under guardianship' meaning under guardianship in marriage, not in property; and both may be added to marriage, and there is no contradiction due to the equivocality of the predicate term. For Abu Hanifa says: She is under guardianship in that the guardian contracts her marriage according to law, as a recommendation or obligation; and she is not under guardianship in that she acts independently and is not forced into the contract. These meanings must be observed not only for contradiction but for all types of syllogism as well. Accordingly, the statement of some Shafi'i jurists: 'The woman is under guardianship, so she does not manage her own affairs' is a non-necessary conclusion, for Abu Hanifa says: Your statement that she is under guardianship — if you mean thereby that she does not manage her own affairs or that the guardian compels her, then that is exactly the point at issue, so making it a premise in the syllogism is a petition principii; and if it is meant that the guardian contracts her marriage as a recommendation or obligation, then it does not follow from this that her contract is not valid if she performs it contrary to the recommendation.
Sixth: That the predication not be under two different aspects, as when we say: 'The water in the jug is quenchant and purifying, and it is not quenchant nor purifying' meaning it is quenchant potentially, not actually; and due to the difference in aspect of predication, the two judgments do not contradict. And from the Most High: 'And you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw' which is a negation of throwing and an affirmation of it, but the aspect of negation is not the aspect of affirmation, so they are not contradictory. This is also something often mistaken in fiqh matters.
Seventh: That it not be in two different times, as when we say: 'The child has teeth' meaning after weaning, and 'The child has no teeth' meaning at the beginning. And we say in fiqh: 'Wine was forbidden' meaning in later periods, and 'It was permissible' meaning before the revelation of prohibition. In summary, one of the two propositions should not differ from the other except in quality only, so that one negates what the other affirms exactly in the manner it affirmed it, and concerning the very subject it posited, in that manner, in that time, and under that aspect; then they share truth and falsity. If any condition is lacking, it is possible for them to share in truth or falsity.
Eighth: This concerns the proposition whose subject is universal specifically; for in the case of a universal subject, it is additionally required that the two propositions differ in particularity and universality along with difference in negation and affirmation, so that contradiction necessarily follows; otherwise it is possible for them both to be true — like two particular propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Some people are writers, some people are not writers' — and perhaps both are false, like two universal propositions in the matter of possibility, such as our saying: 'Every human is a writer, and no human is a writer.' Thus contradiction is only completed in quantified propositions after the conditions we have mentioned, provided that one of the two propositions is universal and the other particular, so that their contradiction is necessary. Let us test all matters: let us first posit the affirmative universal and say: 'Every human is an animal; not some humans are animals'... 'Every human is a writer; not some humans are writers'... 'Every human is a stone; not some humans are stones' — we find invariably that one of the two propositions is true and the other false. And let us test the universal negative and say: 'No human is an animal; some humans are animals'... 'No human is a stone; some humans are stones'... 'No human is a writer; some humans are writers' — of necessity they share truth and falsity in all matters. For if it is said: 'The two universal propositions in the matter of necessity and impossibility also share truth and falsity,' we say: Yes, but that is only known after knowing the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether it is necessary or not. And if you observe the condition we mentioned, you know the contradiction definitely, even if you do not know that relation; for regardless of how it is, contradiction follows.

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions that must be met for two propositions to be genuine contradictories (tanāquḍ). The author lists several pitfalls where people mistakenly think two statements contradict when in fact they do not. Each condition is illustrated with examples from everyday language and from Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh).

The author tests all matter-combinations (possible, necessary, impossible) and shows that universal and particular contradictories always divide truth and falsity. The key takeaway: for true contradiction, the two propositions must be identical in subject, predicate, time, aspect, and relation, and differ only in quality (affirmation/negation). For universal propositions, they must also differ in quantity (universal/particular).

Seventh and Eighth Conditions for Contradiction
Author specifies the seventh and eighth conditions for propositional contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seventh and eighth conditions for propositional contradiction and apply them to check whether two propositions are truly contradictory.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity of time and, for universal subjects, a difference in quantity as well as quality.

In this passage, the author expands on the conditions necessary for two propositions to be true logical contradictories. He has already listed six conditions, and now adds the seventh (temporal unity) and the eighth (quantitative difference for universal subjects). Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly identifying contradictions in arguments, especially in theological and legal reasoning where temporal or quantificational nuances might cause apparent contradictions that are not real.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Find the two statements that might be considered contradictory. Note their subjects, predicates, and qualifiers (e.g., universal/particular, affirmative/negative).
2
Check the seventh condition: temporal unity
Ensure both propositions refer to the same time. If they refer to different times (e.g., 'wine was forbidden' vs 'wine was vinegar' at different eras), they are not contradictory.
The example given uses different times to show non-contradiction.
3
Check the eighth condition: quantitative difference
If the subject is universal (e.g., 'every person'), the two propositions must differ in quantity (one universal, one particular) in addition to quality. If both are universal or both particular, contradiction is not guaranteed.
The author tests this with examples in possibility, necessity, and impossibility.
4
Verify other conditions
Confirm they also satisfy the first six conditions: same subject, predicate, place, relation, etc. The passage focuses on the seventh and eighth, but all must hold.
5
Determine if contradiction holds
If all conditions are met, the two propositions are truly contradictory (one true, one false). If any condition fails, they are not contradictory.
The author stresses that with the eighth condition, contradiction is certain without needing modal knowledge.

السابع : ألا يكون في زمانين مختلفين كقولنا : الصبي له أسنان، ونعني به بعد الفطام والصبي لا أسنان له ونعني به في أول الأمر . ونقول في الفقه : الخمر كانت حراماً، ونعني به في الأعصار السابقة، وكانت خلالاً، ونعني به قبل نزول التحريم. وبالجملة ينبغي ألا تخالف إحدى القضيتين الأخرى إلا في الكيف فقط، فتسلب إحداهما ما أوجبته الأخرى على الوجه الذي أوجبته. وعن الموضوع الذي وضعته بعينه على ذلك النحو وفي ذلك الوقت وبتلك الجهة فإذ ذاك يقتسمان الصدق والكذب، فإن تخلّف شرط جاز أن يشتركا في الصدق أو في الكذب. الثامن : وهذا في القضية التي موضوعها كلّي على الخصوص، فإنه يزيد في التي موضوعها كلِّي أن تختلف القضيتان بالجزئية والكلية مع الاختلاف في السلب والإيجاب، حتى يلزم التناقض لا محالة، وإلا أمكن أن تصدقا جميعاً كالجزئيتين في مادة الإمكان مثل قولنا بعض الناس كاتب بعض الناس ليس بكاتب، وربما كذبتا جميعاً كالكليتين في مادة الإمكان كقولنا : كلّ إنسان كاتب وليس واحد من الناس كاتباً؛ فالتناقض إنما يتم في المحصورات بعد الشروط التي ذكرناها إن كانت إحدى القضيتين كلّية والأخرى جزئية، ليكون تناقضها ضرورياً، ولنمتحن المواد كلّها ولنضع الموجبة أولاً كلّية فنقول : كلّ إنسان حيوان، ليس بعض الناس بحيوان؛ كلّ إنسان كاتب ليس بعض الناس بكاتب ؛ كلّ إنسان حجر، ليس بعض الناس بحجر ؛ فنجد لا محالة إحدى القضيتين صادقة والأخرى كاذبة. ولنمتحن السالبة الكلّية فنقول : ليس واحد من الناس حيواناً بعض الناس حيوان؛ ليس واحد من الناس بحجر بعض الناس حجر ؛ ليس واحد من الناس بكاتب بعض الناس كاتب ؛ فبالضرورة تقتسمان الصدق والكذب في جميع المواد. فإن قيل: فالكليتان في مادة الوجوب والامتناع أيضاً تقتسمان الصدق والكذب قلنا: نعم ولكن لا يعرف ذلك إلا بعد معرفة نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع أنه ضروري أم لا . وإذا راعيت الشرط الذي ذكرناه علمت التناقض قطعاً، وإن لم تعرف تلك النسبة فإنه كيفما كان الأمر يلزم التناقض .
Seventh: That it not be in two different times, like our saying: 'The boy has teeth' and we mean after weaning, and 'The boy has no teeth' and we mean in early infancy. And we say in fiqh: 'Wine was forbidden' and we mean in previous eras, and 'It was vinegar' and we mean before the prohibition descended. In general, one of the two propositions should not oppose the other except in quality alone, so that one negates what the other affirmed in the manner it affirmed it, and concerning the subject it posited exactly in that manner, at that time, and with that respect. For then they share truth and falsity. If a condition is violated, it is possible that they both are true or both false. Eighth: This is especially in the proposition whose subject is universal, for it adds that in the one whose subject is universal, the two propositions must differ in particularity and universality along with the difference in negation and affirmation, so that contradiction necessarily occurs. Otherwise, it is possible that both are true, like two particulars in the matter of possibility: our saying 'Some people are writers' and 'Some people are not writers' — and perhaps both are false, like two universals in the matter of possibility: our saying 'Every person is a writer' and 'No person is a writer'. For contradiction is only complete in quantified propositions after the conditions we mentioned, if one of the two propositions is universal and the other particular, so that their contradiction is necessary. Let us test all materials. Let us first posit the universal affirmative: 'Every person is an animal' and 'Not some people are animals'; 'Every person is a writer' and 'Not some people are writers'; 'Every person is a stone' and 'Not some people are stones'. Then we find necessarily that one of the two propositions is true and the other false. And let us test the universal negative: 'No person is an animal' and 'Some people are animals'; 'No person is a stone' and 'Some people are stones'; 'No person is a writer' and 'Some people are writers'. Then necessarily they share truth and falsity in all materials. If it is said: The two universals in the matter of necessity and impossibility also share truth and falsity, we say: Yes, but that is not known except after knowing the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether it is necessary or not. And if you observe the condition we mentioned, you know the contradiction with certainty, even if you do not know that relation. For however the matter is, contradiction is entailed.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh and eighth conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) between propositions. The seventh condition requires that the two propositions refer to the same time. The author gives examples: saying 'the boy has teeth' after weaning vs. 'the boy has no teeth' in early infancy are not contradictory because they refer to different times. Similarly in fiqh, saying 'wine was forbidden' in later times vs. 'it was vinegar' before prohibition are not contradictory. In general, the two propositions must differ only in quality (affirmation vs. negation) while agreeing in subject, predicate, place, time, and relation. The eighth condition applies specifically when the subject is universal: the two propositions must differ in quantity (one universal, one particular) in addition to quality. If both are universal or both particular, they can be both true or both false, as shown with examples in the matter of possibility. The author then tests this with all materials (possibility, necessity, impossibility) and shows that the universal-particular pair always yields one true and one false. He addresses an objection that two universals in necessary/impossible matters also yield true/false, but notes that requires knowing the modal relation, whereas the universal-particular condition guarantees contradiction without modal knowledge.

Key Terms
تناقض
كيف
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the eighth condition, which pair of propositions yields a necessary contradiction?
A) Two universal affirmatives in the matter of possibility
B) A universal negative and a particular negative in the matter of possibility
C) A universal affirmative and a particular negative in the matter of possibility
D) Two particular negatives in the matter of possibility

Key Takeaway
The seventh and eighth conditions ensure that contradiction is not confused with mere difference in time or quantity; a true logical contradictory must differ only in quality while agreeing in all other respects, and for universal subjects must also differ in quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Classification of Premises and Pure Intellectual First Principles
Author divides premises and introduces first type of certain premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify premises into certain and non-certain, and identify the distinguishing features of pure intellectual first principles.

Core Idea
Certain premises are the bedrock of demonstration; first principles are self-evident truths of pure intellect.

In this passage, the author returns to the main discussion of premises after a digression. He divides premises into two broad categories: certain premises (muqaddamat yaqiniyya) and others. Then, focusing on the certain premises, he begins to list their types based on the cognitive faculty (al-mudrak) by which they are known. The first and most fundamental type is 'pure intellectual first principles' (al-awwaliyyat al-ʻaqliyya al-mahda). These are propositions that the intellect accepts immediately without any additional mediating factor. Understanding this classification is essential because it establishes the foundation for all demonstrative knowledge in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The author starts by dividing premises into two categories: certain (yaqiniyya) and non-certain. Find the Arabic terms for these in the passage.
The Arabic phrase 'مقدمات تنقسم إلى يقينيات صادقة واجبة القبول وإلى غيرها' clearly establishes the division.
2
Understand the basis of sub-classification
The certain premises are further divided into four types based on 'al-mudrak' (the faculty of perception). What does 'al-mudrak' refer to here? It is the means by which the premise is known, such as intellect, sense, or testimony.
The passage says 'باعتبار المدرك' – according to the perceiving faculty.
3
Examine the first type: pure intellectual first principles
The text defines them as propositions that occur in a human from his abstract intellect without any additional meaning that compels assent. Note that the simple concepts (dhawat al-basa'it) may require sensory or imaginative aid, but the judgment itself is intellectual.
The phrase 'بغير معنى زائد عليها يوجب التصديق بها' highlights their self-evidence.
4
Distinguish from other types
Although only the first type is mentioned in this passage, anticipate that other types (e.g., sensory data, experience, testimony) will follow. How does the description of first principles set them apart as the most certain?
First principles require no proof; they are immediately known by the intellect alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنرجع إلى الغرض فنقول : المقدمات تنقسم إلى يقينيات صادقة واجبة القبول وإلى غيرها. القسم الأول : المقدمات اليقينية وللقسم الأول باعتبار المدرك أربعة أصناف : الصنف الأول : الأوليات العقلية المحضة وهي قضايا تحدث في الإنسان من جهة قوته العقلية المجرّدة من غير معنى زائد عليها يوجب التصديق بها ولكن ذوات البسائط إذا حصلت في الذهن إما لمعونة الحس أو الخيال أو وجه وجه آخر
And we return to the purpose and say: Premises are divided into certain, truthful and obligatory to accept, and others. The first section: The certain premises. And the first section, according to the faculty of perception, has four types. The first type: The pure intellectual first principles, which are propositions that occur in a human being by virtue of his abstract intellectual power, without any additional meaning that necessitates assent to them; but simple concepts when they occur in the intellect, either with the aid of sense, imagination, or some other path...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins the classification of premises after the digression on poetic syllogism. The author states that premises divide into certain (يقينية) and non-certain. The certain premises are further divided into four types based on the cognitive faculty (المدرك) by which they are known. Only the first type is mentioned here: pure intellectual first principles (الأوليات العقلية المحضة). These are self-evident propositions that arise from the intellect alone, without any additional factor causing assent. The simple concepts (ذوات البسائط) may require the assistance of the senses or imagination to be grasped, but the judgment itself is immediate. This corresponds to the Aristotelian notion of axioms or primary knowledge. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the text continues to describe the other types.

Pure Intellectual Primary Principles (al-Awwaliyyat)
Defines the first type of certain premises: pure intellectual primary principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition qualifies as a pure intellectual primary principle (awwaliyya) and explain its defining characteristics.

Core Idea
Primary principles are self-evident truths the mind accepts without external proof.

In our study of certain premises (al-muqaddimat al-yaqiniyya), we begin with the most fundamental type: pure intellectual primary principles. These are propositions that the mind assents to necessarily, simply by understanding the terms involved, without any need for sensory experience or additional reasoning. This passage from the Mi'yar al-'Ilm defines this category and provides vivid examples to illustrate its nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category being defined
Read the beginning of the passage where the author says 'الصنف الأول : الأوليات العقلية المحضة'. What is the genus? He is dividing certain premises into four types; this is the first. So the category is a type of certain premise.
2
Extract the defining condition
Look for the condition that makes a proposition an awwaliyya. The author says: 'هي قضايا تحدث في الإنسان من جهة قوته العقلية المجردة من غير معنى زائد عليها يوجب التصديق بها'. The key is that the intellect alone, without any extra meaning, necessitates assent.
The condition is 'purely intellectual' – no sense, no authority, no induction.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives three examples: 'Two is greater than one', 'Three plus three is six', 'One thing cannot be both old and new together'. Check each: Do they require anything beyond understanding the terms? The first two are mathematical; the third is a logical principle. They are self-evident once the simple concepts are conceived.
Note that the last example involves compound concepts (old, new) but still immediate after understanding.
4
Understand the role of simple concepts (basa'it)
The author explains that such knowledge requires only the conception of the simple essences (basa'it). Once you conceive 'two' and 'one', the relation 'greater than' is immediately known. No middle term or inference is needed.
This distinguishes awwaliyyat from acquired premises that require a syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنرجع إلى الغرض فنقول : المقدمات تنقسم إلى يقينيات صادقة واجبة القبول وإلى غيرها. القسم الأول : المقدمات اليقينية وللقسم الأول باعتبار المدرك أربعة أصناف : الصنف الأول : الأوليات العقلية المحضة وهي قضايا تحدث في الإنسان من جهة قوته العقلية المجرّدة من غير معنى زائد عليها يوجب التصديق بها ولكن ذوات البسائط إذا حصلت في الذهن إما لمعونة الحس أو الخيال أو وجه وجه آخر وجعلتها القوة المفكرة قضية بأن نسبت أحدها إلى الآخر بسلب أو إيجاب، صدق بها الذهن اضطراراً من غير أن يشعر بأنه من أين استفاد هذا التصديق، بل يقدر كأنه كان عالماً به على الدوام كقولنا: إن الاثنين أكثر من الواحد، والثلاثة مع الثلاثة ستة، وإن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قديماً وحديثاً معاً، وإن السلب والإيجاب معاً لا يصدقان في شيء واحد فقط إلى نظائره. وهذا الجنس من العلوم لا يتوقف الذهن في التصديق به إلا على تصوّر البسائط، أعني الحدود والذوات المفردة، فمهما تصوّر الذوات وتفطن للتركيب لم يتوقف في التصديق، وربما يحتاج إلى توقف حتى يتفطّن لمعنى الحادث والقديم، ولكن بعد معرفتهما لا يتوقف في الحكم بالتصديق.
And let us return to the purpose and say: Premises are divided into certain truthful premises that are obligatory to accept and into others. The first division: Certain premises. And for the first division, in consideration of the means of perception, there are four types: The first type: Pure intellectual primary principles. They are propositions that occur in a human from his purely intellectual faculty without any additional meaning that necessitates assent to them. But simple essences when they are obtained in the mind, either with the help of sense or imagination or some other way, and the thinking faculty makes them into a proposition by relating one to another negatively or affirmatively, the mind assents to them necessarily without feeling from where it acquired this assent. Rather, it is as if it had always known them, like our statement: 'Two is greater than one,' 'Three plus three is six,' 'One thing cannot be both old and new together,' and 'Negation and affirmation together cannot be true of one thing,' and so on. This type of knowledge does not require the mind to pause in assenting to it except upon the conception of the simple concepts, i.e., the definitions and the simple essences. So whenever it conceives the essences and understands the composition, it does not pause in assent. Perhaps it needs to pause until it understands the meaning of 'originated' and 'eternal,' but after knowing them it does not pause in the judgment of assent.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of certain premises: pure intellectual primary principles (al-awwaliyyat al-'aqliyya al-mahda). These are foundational propositions that the mind accepts necessarily once the simple concepts (basa'it) are understood. Examples include mathematical truths ('two is greater than one') and logical principles ('a thing cannot be both old and new'). The author emphasizes that no external sense or experience is needed; the intellect alone suffices. However, for compound concepts like 'originated' and 'eternal', one must first grasp the meanings, after which the judgment becomes immediate. This classification is crucial for understanding how certainty is achieved in demonstrative science.

Key Terms
الأوليات
البسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is derived from sensory experience.
D) It requires a syllogism to prove.

Key Takeaway
Pure intellectual primary principles are the most foundational certain premises, known immediately by the intellect without any external aid.

Identifying Perceptibles (al-Mahsusat) in Islamic Logic
Defines the second type of certain premises: perceptibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify perceptible premises (al-mahsusat) from examples and distinguish them from primary principles and other certain premises.

Core Idea
Perceptibles are certain premises known through senses, not pure intellect.

This passage defines the second category of certain premises: those based on sense perception. Unlike primary principles which are self-evident to the intellect, perceptibles require empirical input. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the epistemic status of premises used in kalam and fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Examine the examples
The author gives clear examples: 'The moon is round', 'Fire is hot', 'Snow is cold'. Notice that all these require observation through the external senses.
These examples show that perceptibles are empirical claims.
2
Identify the role of the senses
The passage states that pure intellect alone cannot judge these propositions; they depend on the senses. If you abstract from sense experience, you cannot know that fire is hot.
3
Distinguish from primary principles
Primary principles (awwaliyyat) are known by intellect alone, e.g., 'The whole is greater than the part'. Perceptibles are a separate class called 'sensory primaries' (awwaliyyat hissiyya) because they are also certain but require sense input.
Both are certain, but their epistemological source differs.
4
Recognize internal perception
The author includes internal states like 'we have fear' or 'we have thought' as perceptibles. These are known through inner faculties, not external senses, but still require a form of perception.
This expands the category to introspection.
5
Consider accidental obstacles
The author warns that perceptibles can fail under weak senses, distance, or dense media. Identify these conditions to avoid false conclusions.
This is a practical check for reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثاني : المحسوسات كقولنا القمر مستدير، والشمس منيرة، والكواكب كثيرة ، والكافور أبيض، والفحم أسود، والنار حارة، والثلج بارد، فإن العقل المجرد إذا لم يقترن بالحواس لم يقض بهذه القضايا، وإنما أدركها بواسطة الحواس، وهذه أوليات حسية. ومن هذا القبيل علمنا بأن لنا فكراً وخوفاً وغضباً وشهوة وإدراكاً وإحساساً، فإن ذلك انكشف للنفس أيضاً بمساعدة قوى باطنة، يقع متأخراً عن القضايا التي صدق بها العقل من غير حاجة إلى قوة أخرى سوى العقل. ولا تشك في صدق المحسوسات إذا استثنيت أموراً عارضة، مثل ضعف الحس وبعد المحسوس وكثافة الوسائط.
The second type: Perceptibles, like our statement: 'The moon is round,' 'The sun is luminous,' 'The stars are numerous,' 'Camphor is white,' 'Charcoal is black,' 'Fire is hot,' 'Snow is cold.' For the pure intellect, if not accompanied by the senses, would not judge these propositions; rather, it perceives them through the senses. These are sensory primaries. Of this kind is our knowledge that we have thought, fear, anger, desire, perception, and sensation, for that is also disclosed to the soul with the help of inner faculties, occurring after the propositions that the mind assents to without need of any other faculty besides the intellect. And do not doubt the truth of perceptibles unless you exclude accidental matters, such as weakness of the sense, distance of the perceived object, and density of the media.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines perceptibles (al-mahsusat), the second type of certain premises. These are propositions known through the external senses (e.g., 'Fire is hot') or internal senses (e.g., 'we have fear'). The author stresses that the intellect alone cannot judge these; they require sense perception. However, they are still certain, provided there are no obstacles like weak senses or distance. This category is distinct from primary principles because it depends on empirical input. The example of internal states (thought, fear) shows that introspective awareness also counts as perceptible, mediated by inner faculties. The author warns that accidental impediments can lead to error, but in normal conditions, perceptibles are reliable.

Key Terms
المحسوسات
أوليات حسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Camphor is white.

Key Takeaway
Perceptibles are certain premises known through sense experience, distinct from purely intellectual primaries, and are reliable under normal conditions.

Experimentals: Certainty Through Repeated Experience
Defines experimentals as certain premises from repeated sense experience with hidden syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why experimentals are considered certain premises and identify the hidden syllogism that underlies them.

Core Idea
Repeated experience with a hidden syllogism yields certainty, not mere probability.

We have seen that certain premises can come from sense perception and tawatur. Now we encounter a third type: experimentals. These are not simply empirical observations; they involve an implicit reasoning that elevates repeated patterns to necessary causal connections. This is crucial in Islamic sciences where experiential knowledge—like the effects of substances—forms the basis for legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'al-mujarribat' (experimentals). Note that they are a type of certain premise, not just empirical generalizations.
2
Extract the defining elements
The definition combines two elements: (1) sense perception and (2) a hidden syllogism (qiyas khafi). The examples (bread satiates, fire burns) show everyday experiences.
The hidden syllogism is key: without it, repeated observation could still be accidental.
3
Understand the hidden syllogism
The author explains the reasoning: if the effect were accidental, it would not occur consistently. Since it does occur consistently, the mind infers a necessary causal connection. This is the hidden logical step.
This is not a formal syllogism but an implicit inference.
4
Distinguish decisive vs. majority judgments
Sometimes experience yields certainty (e.g., fire always burns), and sometimes only a majority judgment (e.g., scammony usually purges). The hidden syllogism still operates, but exceptions are considered rare and requiring explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : المجرّبات وهي أمور وقع التصديق بها من الحس بمعاونة قياس خفي، كحكمنا بأن الضرب مؤلم للحيوان والقطع مؤلم، وحَزّ الرقبة مُهلك والسقمونيا (۱) مسهل ، والخبز مُشبع، والماء مرو، والنار محرقة؛ فإن الحسّ أدرك الموت مع حَزّ الرقبة، وعرف التألم عند القطع بهيئات في المضروب، وتكرّر ذلك على الذكر فتأكد منه عقد قوي لا يشك فيه، وليس علينا ذكر السبب في حصول اليقين بعد أن عرفنا أنه يقيني، وربما أوجبت التجربة قضاء جزمياً وربما أوجبت قضاءً أكثرياً، ولا تخلو عن قوة قياسية خفية تخالط المشاهدات، لو كان هذا الأمر اتفاقياً أو عرضياً غير لازم لما استمرَّ في الأكثر من غير اختلاف، حتى إذا لم يوجد ذلك اللازم استبعدت النفس تأخره عنه وعدته نادراً، وطلبت له سبباً عارضاً مانعاً. وإذا اجتمع هذا الإحساس متكرراً مرة بعد أخرى، ولا ينضبط عدد المرات كما لا ينضبط عدد المخبرين في التواتر، فإن كل واقعة ههنا مثل
The third type: Experimentals. They are matters that are assented to through sense with the aid of a hidden syllogism, like our judgment that beating is painful for animals, cutting is painful, slitting the throat is lethal, scammony is a laxative, bread is satiating, water is quenching, fire is burning. For the sense perceived death with slitting the throat, and recognized pain upon cutting by the states of the beaten [animal], and this repeated on memory, so a strong conviction was established without doubt. It is not necessary for us to mention the cause for obtaining certainty after we have known it is certain. Sometimes experience yields a decisive judgment, and sometimes yields a majority judgment. And it is not devoid of a hidden syllogistic power that mingles with observations, for if this matter were accidental or incidental and not necessary, it would not persist in most cases without variation. Even when that necessary [effect] is not found, the soul considers its absence improbable and counts it rare, and seeks for it an incidental preventing cause. And when this sensation occurs repeatedly, one time after another, and the number of times is not fixed just as the number of reporters in mutawatir [traditions] is not fixed, for each occurrence here is like
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines experimentals (al-mujarribat), the third type of certain premises. These are based on repeated sensory observations combined with a hidden syllogism (qiyas khafi). The author gives examples from daily life: eating bread satiates, scammony purges, fire burns. Through repeated experience, the mind forms a strong conviction that the effect is necessary, not accidental. The hidden syllogism is: if the effect were accidental, it would not occur consistently; since it does occur consistently, there must be a causal connection. This yields either decisive certainty (if always consistent) or a majority judgment (if occasional exceptions). The author compares this to tawatur (mass transmission) where the number of instances compensates for lack of formal proof. The passage is incomplete, but the core definition is clear.

المجرّبات
قياس خفي

B) It infers a necessary causal connection from repeated consistency.
C) It is a formal syllogism that replaces sense experience.
D) It only applies to decisive judgments, not majority ones.

Key Takeaway
Experimentals demonstrate that repeated sense experience, combined with a hidden syllogistic inference, can produce certain knowledge of causal connections.

The Fourth Type of Certain Premises: Ever-Present Middle Term
Author defines the fourth type of certain premises whose middle term is always present in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the fourth type of certain premises from primary premises (awwaliyat) and from ordinary mediated premises, and identify examples of such premises from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Some propositions are certain not because they are self-evident, but because their proof is always in your mind.

In the previous lessons, we encountered premises that are certain on their own (awwaliyat) and those known through sensory experience or testimony. Now the author introduces a subtle category: propositions that are not self-evident, but whose middle term is so familiar that the mind assents to them instantly. This category bridges the gap between pure axiomatic knowledge and knowledge that requires explicit syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the category of propositions being defined in the Arabic. The author calls it 'الصنف الرابع' (the fourth type) and describes it as 'القضايا التي عرفت لا بنفسها بل بوسط ولكن لا يعزب عن الذهن أوساطها'. What exactly is being defined?
2
Extract the defining property
The key property is that the middle term (وسط) never escapes the mind. How does this differ from other non-primary premises? The author contrasts this with cases where the middle term is not immediately present.
3
Analyze the example
Take the example 'الاثنان ثلث الستة' (two is a third of six). Break down the implied syllogism. What is the middle term? How is it ever-present?
The middle term is the division of six into three equal parts.
4
Contrast with cases requiring reflection
Compare with 'هل الاثنان والعشرون ثلث الستة والستين؟' (Is twenty-two a third of sixty-six?). Why does this not evoke immediate assent? This contrast clarifies the condition of ever-presence.
Larger numbers require calculation, so the middle term is not immediately present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الرابع : القضايا التي عرفت لا بنفسها بل بوسط ولكن لا يعزب عن الذهن أوساطها ، بل مهما أحضر جزئي المطلوب حضر التصديق به لحضور الوسط معه كقولنا : الاثنان ثلث الستة، فإن هذا معلوم بوسط وهو أن كل منقسم ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فأحد الأقسام ثلث والستة تنقسم بالاثنينات ثلاثة أقسام متساوية، فالاثنان إذن ثلث الستة ولكن هذا الوسط لا يعزب عن الذهن لقلة هذا العدد وتعود الإنسان التأميل فيه، حتى لو قيل لك : الاثنان والعشرون هل هي ستة وستين؟ لم تبادر إليه مبادرتك إلى الحكم بأن الاثنين ثلث الستة، بل ربما افتقرت إلى أن تقسم الستة والستين على ثلاثة، فإذا انقسمت وحصل أن كل قسم اثنان وعشرون عرفت أن ذلك ثلثه ، وهكذا كلما كثر الحساب؛ فهذا وإن كان معلوماً برأي ثانٍ لا بالرأي الأول ولكنه ليس يحتاج فيه إلى تأمل، فهو جار مجرى الأوليات فيصلح لأن يكون من مواد الأقيسة، بل القضايا التي هي نتائج أقيسة ألفت هي من الأصناف الثلاثة السابقة تصلح أن تكون مواد أقيسة ومقدماتها .
The fourth type: propositions that are known not by themselves but by a middle term, yet their middle terms do not escape the mind; rather, whenever the two parts of the desired [proposition] are present, the assent [to it] occurs due to the presence of the middle term with them. For example, our saying: 'Two is a third of six.' For this is known by a middle term, which is that everything divided into three equal parts, one of the parts is a third. And six is divided by twos into three equal parts. So two is thus a third of six. But this middle term does not escape the mind due to the smallness of this number and man's habituation to contemplating it. Even if it were said to you: 'Is twenty-two a third of sixty-six?', you would not hasten to it like your hastening to the judgment that two is a third of six. Rather, you might need to divide sixty-six by three; then if it divides and it results that each part is twenty-two, you would know that it is its third. And so on as calculations become larger. So this, although known by a second opinion not by the first, yet it does not require reflection; it runs the course of primary premises and is suitable to be among the matters of syllogisms. Rather, propositions that are results of composed syllogisms, from the three preceding types, are suitable to be matters of syllogisms and their premises.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of certain premises within a larger classification. The author describes propositions that are not self-evident but are known through a middle term. However, unlike typical non-primaries, the middle term here is so familiar and immediately present in the mind that the proposition is assented to without conscious reflection. The example given is the mathematical truth that two is a third of six. The middle term—that dividing six into three equal parts yields two—is ever-present due to the small numbers involved. The author contrasts this with larger numbers like twenty-two and sixty-six, which require calculation. He concludes that such premises are akin to primary premises (أوليات) in their immediacy and can serve as materials for syllogisms, even though they are derived from previous reasoning. This illustrates the sophistication of the classification of premises in Islamic logic, going beyond simple self-evident vs. non-self-evident categories.

Key Terms
قضايا
وسط
أوليات
مواد الأقيسة
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are self-evident without any middle term.
B) They are known through a middle term that is always present in the mind.
C) They are only known after lengthy calculation.
D) They are not suitable as premises for syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of certain premises are those known through a middle term that is always present in the mind, making them as immediate as primary premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
