Mashhurat: Widely Known Propositions in Juristic Reasoning
Author classifies non-certain premises and defines mashhurat with first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mashhurat from certain premises and identify the first accidental cause (riqqat al-qalb) that generates them.

Core Idea
Mashhurat are accepted not by reason but by accidental causes like natural compassion.

The author now turns to premises that are not certain. Among these, one type is used in jurisprudence (fiqh) to produce probable judgments. The first kind of such premises is called mashhurat, or widely accepted propositions. In this lesson, you will learn what they are, see examples, and understand the first psychological cause that leads people to accept them – the softness of heart.

Guided Path
1
Locate the classification
Find where the author divides premises into certain and non-certain, and then subdivides non-certain into two types (one suitable for fiqh, one not).
2
Identify mashhurat via examples
Read the list of examples: spreading peace, feeding, family ties, truthfulness, justice, and the opposites. Notice that these are ethical judgments that seem self-evident but are actually due to external causes.
The author contrasts these with judgments based on pure intellect or sense.
3
Analyze the first cause: riqqat al-qalb
Focus on the first cause – natural tenderness of heart. Understand how it leads people to deem slaughter evil. Follow the author's critique of the Mu'tazila who struggled with the problem of animal pain.
The author refutes their reasoning by comparing to a king slapping for a loaf and by appealing to God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : المقدمات التي ليست يقينية ولا تصلح للبراهين وهي نوعان : نوع يصلح للظنّيات الفقهية ونوع لا يصلح لذلك أيضاً. المقدمات الصالحة للفقهيات : النوع الأول : وهو الصالح للفقهيات دون اليقينيات وهي ثلاثة أصناف : مشهورات ومقبولات ومظنونات . الصنف الأول : المشهورات مثل حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام الطعام، وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والأحكام، وحكمنا بقبح إيذاء الإنسان، وقتل الحيوان، ووضع البهتان، ورضاء الأزواج بفجور النسوان ، ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران والطغيان، وهذه قضايا لو خلي الإنسان وعقله المجرّد ووهمه وحسّه لما قضى الذهن به قضاء بمجرد العقل والحس، ولكن إنما قضى بها لأسباب عارضة أكدّت في النفس هذه القضايا وأثبتتها ؛ خمسة : وهي :أولها : رقة القلب بحكم الغريزة وذلك في حق أكثر الناس حتى سبق إلى وهم قوم إن ذبح الحيوان قبيحٌ عقلاً، ولولا أن سياسة الشرع صرفت الناس عن ذلك إلى تحسين الذبح وجعله قرباناً لعم هذا الاعتقاد أكثر الناس. ومن هذا أشكل على المعتزلة وأكثر الفرق وجه العدل في إيلام البهائم بالذبح والمجانين بالمرض، وزعموا بحكم رقة طباعهم أن ذلك قبيح، فمنهم من اعتذر بأنها ستعوض عليها بعد الحشر في الدار الآخرة. ولم ينتبه هؤلاء لقبح صفع الملك ضعيفاً ليعطيه ،رغيفاً مهما قدر على إعطائه دون الصفع، واعتذر فريق بأنها عقوبات على جنايات قارفوها وهم مكلفون، وردّوا بطريق التناسخ بعد الموت إلى هذه القوالب ليعذبوا فيها، ولم يعلموا أن عقوبة من لا يعرف أنه معاقب فينزجر بسببه قبيح. وإن زعموا أنها تعرف كونها معاقبة على جنايات سبقت كان لها قوة مفكرة، ويلزم عليه تجويز معرفة الذبّان والديدان حقائق الأمور وجميع العلوم الهندسية والفلسفية، وهو مناكرة للمحسوس ، ثم مهما لم يكن للمعاقب غرض في انتقام أو تشفٍ أو دفع ضُرّ في المستقبل أو لم يكن للمعاقب مصلحة فهو أيضاً قبيح، والله قادر على إفاضة النعم على الخلق من غير إيلام، ومن غير تكليف وإلزام، فإيذاؤهم بالتكليف أولاً وبالعقوبة آخراً أحرى بأن يكون قبيحاً مما ذكروه، وجعلوه قبيحاً من إيلام البريء عن الجنايات . السبب الثاني : ما جُبِل عليه الإنسان من الحمية والأنفة ، ولأجله يحكم باستقباح الرضا بفجور ،امرأته، ويظنّ أن هذا حكم ضروري للعقل مع أن جماعة من الناس يتعودون إجارة أزواجهم ليألفوا ذلك ولا ينفروا عنه، بل جميع الزناة
The second division: premises that are not certain and not suitable for demonstrations; they are of two types: a type suitable for juristic probabilities and a type not suitable for that as well. The premises suitable for jurisprudence: the first type, which is suitable for jurisprudence but not for certainties; they are three kinds: widely accepted [mashhurat], received [maqbulat], and probable [maznunat]. The first kind: widely accepted, like our judgment that spreading peace and feeding food, maintaining family ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, observing justice in judgments and rulings, and our judgment that harming a human, killing an animal, fabricating slander, husbands' satisfaction with the adultery of women, and requiting favor with ingratitude and tyranny are evil. These are propositions that, if man were left to his bare intellect, imagination, and sense, the mind would not judge them by mere intellect and sense; rather, it judges them only due to accidental causes that have strengthened and established these propositions in the soul, which are five. The first of them: softness of heart by instinct, and that is with respect to most people, so much so that some have come to imagine that slaughtering an animal is intrinsically evil. Were it not that the policy of the Law turned people away from that to deeming slaughter good and making it an offering, this belief would have spread among most people. Due to this, it became problematic for the Mu'tazila and most sects the justice of inflicting pain on animals by slaughter and on the insane by illness, and they claimed, by virtue of their soft nature, that this is evil. Some of them excused it by saying that these animals will be compensated after resurrection in the Hereafter. These people did not notice the evil of a king slapping a weak person to give him a loaf of bread when he is able to give it without slapping. Another group excused it by saying that they are punishments for crimes they committed while they were accountable, and they argued by way of reincarnation after death into these bodies so that they may be punished in them. They did not know that punishing one who does not know he is being punished so as to be deterred by it is evil. If they claim that it knows it is being punished for previous crimes and that it had a thinking faculty, then it would entail allowing the possibility of flies and worms knowing the realities of things and all engineering and philosophical sciences, which contradicts sense-perception. Moreover, whenever the punisher has no purpose of revenge, retaliation, or future prevention of harm, or the punisher has no benefit, it is also evil. God is able to bestow blessings upon creation without inflicting pain and without obligation and coercion. So inflicting pain through obligation first and punishment afterwards is more deserving of being evil than what they mentioned and deemed evil of inflicting pain on the innocent. The second cause: what man is born with of protectiveness and pride; due to it, he deems it evil to tolerate the adultery of his wife, and thinks this is a necessary judgment of reason, even though a group of people habituate themselves to renting out their wives so they get used to that and do not recoil from it, but all fornicators...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage opens the second main division of premises: those that are not certain and not fit for demonstrations. The author splits these into two broad types: one suitable for juristic probable reasoning and one not even suitable for that. He then focuses on the first type suitable for jurisprudence, which itself has three kinds: widely accepted propositions (مشهورات), received propositions (مقبولات, those accepted from a trusted source), and probable propositions (مظنونات). The text begins explaining the first kind, المشهورات, by listing common ethical judgments: spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining family ties, truthfulness, justice, and then the opposite: harming humans, killing animals, slander, tolerating adultery, ingratitude. The author argues that these judgments are not due to pure intellect or sense; rather, they are due to accidental causes that have firmly impressed them in the soul. He then enumerates five such causes, but only fully explains the first cause: natural softness of heart (رقة القلب). This cause leads many to think killing animals is intrinsically evil, but the author refutes this by explaining that if God can provide without pain, then pain for a greater good is not necessarily evil. He critiques the Mu'tazila and others for being inconsistent. The passage then begins the second cause: human pride and protectiveness, giving the example of deeming adultery of one's wife evil. The enumeration is cut off, but the intended five causes likely include other socio-psychological factors.

مقبولات
مظنونات
رقة القلب
السبب

The Five Causes of Mistaking Customary Opinions for Certain Truths
Explains five psychological and social causes that make people consider customary judgments as primary rational truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five causes by which customary opinions come to be mistaken for primary rational truths.

Core Idea
Desire, social cooperation, childhood discipline, and hasty induction all masquerade as self-evident reason.

Al-Ghazali observes that many propositions people consider rationally certain are actually products of their environment and psychology. He enumerates five distinct causes that produce this illusion, ranging from conformity to desire to inductive generalization. Understanding these causes is crucial for anyone who wishes to separate genuine axiomatic truths from merely customary beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first cause: conformity to desires
Notice how people approve of lewdness with another's wife because it aligns with their desires, and they condemn the person who exposes the adultery because that opposes their desires. They claim these are rational judgments, but they are driven by their own moral traits.
This shows how desire can corrupt rational judgment.
2
Identify the second cause: love of peace and social cooperation
Al-Ghazali points out that societies promote actions like greeting with salam and offering food because they foster harmony. In a group that values domination, however, raiding and killing are considered good. This relativity shows that these judgments are not rational but conventional.
3
Identify the third cause: religious discipline from childhood
Practices like bowing, prostrating, and sacrificing animals become ingrained through repeated instruction from parents and teachers since childhood. After a while, people think these actions are intrinsically good, but a person raised without such discipline would not see them as good.
This cause highlights the effect of authority and habituation.
4
Identify the fourth cause: inductive generalization
Because something is usually good (e.g., greeting with salam is good in most cases), people assume it is always good, ignoring exceptions like during relieving oneself. Similarly, truthfulness is usually good, but can be bad when revealing the location of a prophet to his enemies.
Induction leads to absolute judgments that are not universally true.
5
Identify the fifth cause: ignoring conditions of truth
Al-Ghazali sums up that these causes lead the soul to judge as if the propositions are certain, while they are only true conditionally. For example, 'truthfulness is good' is true only with conditions, but people take it absolutely.
This is a meta-cause: the failure to notice the conditional nature of the proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس
يستحسنون الفجور بامرأة الغير ولا يستقبحونه لموافقة شهواتهم، ويستقبحون من ينبه الأزواج عليه ويعرفهم فعل الزناة، ويزعمون أن ذلك غمز وسعاية ونميمة، وهو في غاية القبح. وأهل الصلاح يقولون: هو خيانة وترك الأمانة؛ فتتناقض أحكامهم في الحسن والقبح ويزعمون أنها قضايا العقل، وإنما منشؤها هذه الأخلاق التي جبل الإنسان عليها . السبب الثالث : محبة التسالم والتصالح والتعاون على المعايش، ولذلك يحسن عندهم التودد بإفشاء السلام وإطعام الطعام، ويقبح لديهم السب والتنفير ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران وأمثاله، ولولا ميلهم إلى أمور تنهض هذه الأسباب وسائل إليها أو صوارف عنها، لما قضت العقول بفطرتها في هذه الأمور بحسن ولا قبح، ولذلك نرى جماعة لا يحبون التسالم ويميلون إلى التغالب، فألذ الأشياء وأحسنها عندهم الغارة والنهب والقتل والفتك .
السبب الرابع : التأديبات الشرعية لإصلاح الناس، فإنها لكونها تكررت على الأسماع منذ الصبا بلسان الآباء والمعلمين ووقع النشء عليها، رسخت تلك الاعتقادات رسوخا أدى إلى الظن بأنها عقلية كحسن الركوع والسجود والتقرب بذبح البهائم وإراقة دمائها، وهذه الأمور لو غومض بها العاقل الذي لم يؤدب بقبولها منذ الصبا لكان مجرد عقله لا يقضي فيها بحسن ولا بقبح، ولكن حسنت بتحسين الشرع فأذعن الوهم لقبولها بالتأديب منذ الصبا.
السبب الخامس : الاستقراء للجزئيات الكثيرة، فإن الشيء متى وجد مقرونا بالشيء في أكثر أحواله ظن أنه ملازم له على الإطلاق، كما يحكم على إفشاء السلام بالحسن مطلقا، لأنه يحسن في أكثر الأحوال، ويذهب عن قبحه في وقت قضاء الحاجة، ويحكم على الصدق بالحسن لوجوده موافقا للأغراض مرغوبا في أكثر الأحوال، ويغفل عن قبحه ممن سئل عن مكان نبي أو ولي ليجده السائل فيقتله، بل ربما اعتقد قبح الكذب حينئذ بإخفاء المحل لمصادفة الكذب مقرونا بالقبح في أكثر الأحوال، فهذه الأسباب وأمثالها علل قضاء النفس بهذه القضايا، وليست هذه القضايا صادقة كلها ولا كاذبة كلها ولكن المقصود أن ما هو صادق منها فليس بين الصدق عند العقل بيانا أوليا، بل يفتقر في تحقيق صدقه إلى نظر وإن كان محمودا عند العقل الأول، والصادق غير المحمود، والكاذب غير الشنيع، ورب شنيع حق، ورب محمود كاذب، وقد يكون المحمود صادقا لكن بشرط دقيق لا يتفطن أكثر الناس له، فيؤخذ على الإطلاق مع أنه لا يكون صادقا إلا الشرط كقولنا الصدق حسن وليس كذلك مطلقا بل بشروط، ولفقد بعض تلك الشروط قبح الصدق الذي هو تعريف لموضع النبي المقصود قتله إلى غير ذلك نظائره ومهما أردت أن تعرف الفرق بين هذه القضايا المشهورات وبين الأوليات العقلية فاعرض قولنا : قتل الإنسان قبيح وإنقاذه من الهلاك جميل، على عقلك بعد أن تقدر كأنك حصلت في الدنيا دفعة بالغا عاقلا، ولم تسمع قط تأديبا ولم تعاشر أمة ولم تعهد ترتيبا وسياسة، لكنك شاهدت المحسوسات وأخذت منها الخيالات، فيمكنك التشكيك في هذه المقدمات أو التوقف فيها ولا يمكن التوقف في قولنا : إن السلب والإيجاب لا يصدقان في حال واحدة، وإن الاثنين أكثر من الواحد، فإذن هذه المقدمات لما كانت قريبة من الصدق محتملة الكذب لم تصلح للبراهين التي يطلب منها اليقين وصلحت للفقهيات .
The second investigation: concerning the material of syllogism. They approve of lewdness with another's wife and do not disapprove of it due to conforming to their desires, and they disapprove of one who alerts husbands to it and informs them of the act of adulterers, claiming that is slander and tale-bearing and gossiping, while it is extremely repulsive. The righteous say: it is betrayal and a breach of trust; thus their judgments about good and bad contradict, yet they claim they are rational propositions, whereas their source is these moral traits that man is created with. The third cause: love of peace, reconciliation, and cooperation in livelihoods, therefore among them it is considered good to show affection by greeting with salam and offering food, and considered bad to insult, cause aversion, and meet blessings with ingratitude and the like. Were it not for their inclination toward matters that these causes render as means to or deterrents from them, the intellects would not naturally judge these matters as good or bad. Thus we see a group who do not love peace but incline to domination, so the most pleasurable and good things to them are raiding, plundering, killing, and treachery. The fourth cause: religious disciplines for reforming people, for because they are repeated in hearing since childhood through the tongues of fathers and teachers, and the young grow up with them, those beliefs become firmly rooted to the point of being thought rational, such as the goodness of bowing and prostrating and drawing near by slaughtering animals and spilling their blood. If a rational person who had not been disciplined to accept them since childhood were to ponder these matters, his bare intellect would not judge them as good or bad. But they became good by the Sharia's making them good, so the estimative faculty acquiesced to accept them through discipline since childhood. The fifth cause: induction of many particulars. For when a thing is found conjoined with another in most of its states, it is thought to be inseparable from it absolutely. For example, one judges that greeting with salam is good absolutely because it is good in most cases, and ignores its badness at the time of relieving oneself. One judges truthfulness good because it is found agreeing with objectives and desirable in most cases, and ignores its badness when one is asked about the location of a prophet or saint for the questioner to find and kill him. Perhaps one even believes that lying is bad at that time by hiding the place, because lying happens conjoined with badness in most cases. So these causes and their like are the reasons for the soul's judging these propositions. Not all of these propositions are true nor all false, but the point is that what is true among them is not a primary evident truth to the intellect, rather its truth requires investigation, even if it is praiseworthy according to the primary intellect. The true is not necessarily praiseworthy, nor the false necessarily ugly; often an ugly thing is true, and a praiseworthy thing false. A praiseworthy thing may be true, but with a subtle condition that most people do not notice, so it is taken absolutely while it is true only conditionally. For example, our statement 'truthfulness is good' is not so absolutely but with conditions; when some conditions are absent, truthfulness – which is revealing the location of a prophet intended to be killed – becomes bad, and other similar cases. Whenever you want to know the difference between these well-known propositions (mashhurat) and primary rational truths (awwaliyat), present our statement 'killing a human is bad and saving him from destruction is good' to your mind after you suppose as if you came into the world all at once as an adult rational being, never having heard any discipline, never associated with any nation, and never accustomed to any order or policy, but you witnessed sensible things and derived imaginings from them. You will be able to doubt these premises or withhold judgment, but you cannot withhold judgment on our statement: affirmation and negation cannot both be true at once, and two is more than one. So these premises, being close to truth but liable to falsehood, are not suitable for demonstrations that require certainty, but are suitable for juristic matters.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of common moral and customary judgments. Al-Ghazali, writing on the material of syllogism, aims to distinguish primary rational truths (awwaliyat) from well-known opinions (mashhurat) that people mistake for certain. He lists five causes that produce such mistaken judgments. The first cause is the conformity to desires: people judge actions based on their own whims, calling good what pleases them and bad what opposes them, even if it is hypocritical. The second cause (implied but not numbered) is the love of peace and social cooperation: societies promote behaviors like greeting and hospitality as universally good because they foster harmony, but in a different environment, raiding and killing might be considered good. The third cause is religious discipline: practices like bowing and sacrificing are ingrained from childhood through parental and educational authority, so they appear intuitive, but they are actually conventional. The fourth cause is inductive generalization: because something is usually good, it is assumed to be always good, such as truthfulness; yet in specific cases (like revealing the location of a pursued prophet) truthfulness is bad. After explaining these causes, al-Ghazali offers a test: imagine a person who grew up in isolation with no social or religious training; such a person would doubt the goodness of saving a life but could not doubt logical principles like non-contradiction. Thus, these premises are not certain enough for demonstration (burhan) but suffice for juristic reasoning (fiqhiyat).

Key Takeaway
Customary judgments (mashhurat) are not rational certainties; they are produced by desire, social convention, upbringing, and hasty induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
2. In the Hanafi school, the principle 'certainty is not overridden by doubt' (al-yaqin la yuzalu bi al-shakk) is considered a primary rational truth. Using al-Ghazali's test, would this principle pass as awwaliyya? Why or why not?

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
The Isolation Test: Distinguishing Awwaliyat from Mashhurat
Presents a thought experiment to distinguish primary rational truths from customary opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Ghazali's thought experiment to determine whether a given proposition is a primary rational truth or a well-known opinion.

Core Idea
If you can doubt it after stripping away all social and religious conditioning, it's not a primary truth.

Al-Ghazali provides a powerful test to distinguish truly self-evident truths from merely customary beliefs: imagine yourself as an adult who has never been exposed to any society, culture, or religion, but only to sense perception. If you can still doubt the proposition, it is not a primary rational truth. This thought experiment is a practical tool for evaluating premises in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Set up the isolation scenario
Imagine you suddenly appear in the world as a full-grown rational adult with no prior experience of society, religion, or customs. You have only your senses and your innate reason. You have never heard any moral teachings or norms.
2
Test the proposition 'killing a human is bad'
Would you automatically know that killing is bad? Without any social or religious input, you might question whether killing is always bad—perhaps it could be good in some cases (e.g., self-defense). Al-Ghazali says you can doubt this premise or withhold judgment.
This shows that the premise is not a self-evident axiom (awwaliyya) but a mashhura.
3
Test the proposition 'affirmation and negation cannot both be true at once'
Can you doubt the principle of non-contradiction? Even in the isolation scenario, you cannot rationally doubt that a thing cannot both be and not be at the same time. This is a true awwaliyya.
This is the benchmark for certainty.
4
Apply the test to other propositions
Now you can test any premise you encounter in Islamic discourse. For example, 'the world is originated'—would you know that innately? Al-Ghazali's test would say you can doubt it, so it is not awwaliyya but requires demonstration.
5
Conclude the epistemic status
Propositions that survive the test are suitable for demonstrative arguments (burhan). Those that fail are suitable only for dialectical or juristic reasoning (fiqhiyyat).
This distinction preserves the rigor of Islamic theology and fiqh.

النظر الثاني : في مادة القياس
يستحسنون الفجور بامرأة الغير ولا يستقبحونه لموافقة شهواتهم، ويستقبحون من ينبه الأزواج عليه ويعرفهم فعل الزناة، ويزعمون أن ذلك غمز وسعاية ونميمة، وهو في غاية القبح. وأهل الصلاح يقولون: هو خيانة وترك الأمانة؛ فتتناقض أحكامهم في الحسن والقبح ويزعمون أنها قضايا العقل، وإنما منشؤها هذه الأخلاق التي جبل الإنسان عليها . السبب الثالث : محبة التسالم والتصالح والتعاون على المعايش، ولذلك يحسن عندهم التودد بإفشاء السلام وإطعام الطعام، ويقبح لديهم السب والتنفير ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران وأمثاله، ولولا ميلهم إلى أمور تنهض هذه الأسباب وسائل إليها أو صوارف عنها، لما قضت العقول بفطرتها في هذه الأمور بحسن ولا قبح، ولذلك نرى جماعة لا يحبون التسالم ويميلون إلى التغالب، فألذ الأشياء وأحسنها عندهم الغارة والنهب والقتل والفتك .
السبب الرابع : التأديبات الشرعية لإصلاح الناس، فإنها لكونها تكررت على الأسماع منذ الصبا بلسان الآباء والمعلمين ووقع النشء عليها، رسخت تلك الاعتقادات رسوخا أدى إلى الظن بأنها عقلية كحسن الركوع والسجود والتقرب بذبح البهائم وإراقة دمائها، وهذه الأمور لو غومض بها العاقل الذي لم يؤدب بقبولها منذ الصبا لكان مجرد عقله لا يقضي فيها بحسن ولا بقبح، ولكن حسنت بتحسين الشرع فأذعن الوهم لقبولها بالتأديب منذ الصبا.
السبب الخامس : الاستقراء للجزئيات الكثيرة، فإن الشيء متى وجد مقرونا بالشيء في أكثر أحواله ظن أنه ملازم له على الإطلاق، كما يحكم على إفشاء السلام بالحسن مطلقا، لأنه يحسن في أكثر الأحوال، ويذهب عن قبحه في وقت قضاء الحاجة، ويحكم على الصدق بالحسن لوجوده موافقا للأغراض مرغوبا في أكثر الأحوال، ويغفل عن قبحه ممن سئل عن مكان نبي أو ولي ليجده السائل فيقتله، بل ربما اعتقد قبح الكذب حينئذ بإخفاء المحل لمصادفة الكذب مقرونا بالقبح في أكثر الأحوال، فهذه الأسباب وأمثالها علل قضاء النفس بهذه القضايا، وليست هذه القضايا صادقة كلها ولا كاذبة كلها ولكن المقصود أن ما هو صادق منها فليس بين الصدق عند العقل بيانا أوليا، بل يفتقر في تحقيق صدقه إلى نظر وإن كان محمودا عند العقل الأول، والصادق غير المحمود، والكاذب غير الشنيع، ورب شنيع حق، ورب محمود كاذب، وقد يكون المحمود صادقا لكن بشرط دقيق لا يتفطن أكثر الناس له، فيؤخذ على الإطلاق مع أنه لا يكون صادقا إلا الشرط كقولنا الصدق حسن وليس كذلك مطلقا بل بشروط، ولفقد بعض تلك الشروط قبح الصدق الذي هو تعريف لموضع النبي المقصود قتله إلى غير ذلك نظائره ومهما أردت أن تعرف الفرق بين هذه القضايا المشهورات وبين الأوليات العقلية فاعرض قولنا : قتل الإنسان قبيح وإنقاذه من الهلاك جميل، على عقلك بعد أن تقدر كأنك حصلت في الدنيا دفعة بالغا عاقلا، ولم تسمع قط تأديبا ولم تعاشر أمة ولم تعهد ترتيبا وسياسة، لكنك شاهدت المحسوسات وأخذت منها الخيالات، فيمكنك التشكيك في هذه المقدمات أو التوقف فيها ولا يمكن التوقف في قولنا : إن السلب والإيجاب لا يصدقان في حال واحدة، وإن الاثنين أكثر من الواحد، فإذن هذه المقدمات لما كانت قريبة من الصدق محتملة الكذب لم تصلح للبراهين التي يطلب منها اليقين وصلحت للفقهيات .
The second investigation: concerning the material of syllogism. They approve of lewdness with another's wife and do not disapprove of it due to conforming to their desires, and they disapprove of one who alerts husbands to it and informs them of the act of adulterers, claiming that is slander and tale-bearing and gossiping, while it is extremely repulsive. The righteous say: it is betrayal and a breach of trust; thus their judgments about good and bad contradict, yet they claim they are rational propositions, whereas their source is these moral traits that man is created with. The third cause: love of peace, reconciliation, and cooperation in livelihoods, therefore among them it is considered good to show affection by greeting with salam and offering food, and considered bad to insult, cause aversion, and meet blessings with ingratitude and the like. Were it not for their inclination toward matters that these causes render as means to or deterrents from them, the intellects would not naturally judge these matters as good or bad. Thus we see a group who do not love peace but incline to domination, so the most pleasurable and good things to them are raiding, plundering, killing, and treachery. The fourth cause: religious disciplines for reforming people, for because they are repeated in hearing since childhood through the tongues of fathers and teachers, and the young grow up with them, those beliefs become firmly rooted to the point of being thought rational, such as the goodness of bowing and prostrating and drawing near by slaughtering animals and spilling their blood. If a rational person who had not been disciplined to accept them since childhood were to ponder these matters, his bare intellect would not judge them as good or bad. But they became good by the Sharia's making them good, so the estimative faculty acquiesced to accept them through discipline since childhood. The fifth cause: induction of many particulars. For when a thing is found conjoined with another in most of its states, it is thought to be inseparable from it absolutely. For example, one judges that greeting with salam is good absolutely because it is good in most cases, and ignores its badness at the time of relieving oneself. One judges truthfulness good because it is found agreeing with objectives and desirable in most cases, and ignores its badness when one is asked about the location of a prophet or saint for the questioner to find and kill him. Perhaps one even believes that lying is bad at that time by hiding the place, because lying happens conjoined with badness in most cases. So these causes and their like are the reasons for the soul's judging these propositions. Not all of these propositions are true nor all false, but the point is that what is true among them is not a primary evident truth to the intellect, rather its truth requires investigation, even if it is praiseworthy according to the primary intellect. The true is not necessarily praiseworthy, nor the false necessarily ugly; often an ugly thing is true, and a praiseworthy thing false. A praiseworthy thing may be true, but with a subtle condition that most people do not notice, so it is taken absolutely while it is true only conditionally. For example, our statement 'truthfulness is good' is not so absolutely but with conditions; when some conditions are absent, truthfulness – which is revealing the location of a prophet intended to be killed – becomes bad, and other similar cases. Whenever you want to know the difference between these well-known propositions (mashhurat) and primary rational truths (awwaliyat), present our statement 'killing a human is bad and saving him from destruction is good' to your mind after you suppose as if you came into the world all at once as an adult rational being, never having heard any discipline, never associated with any nation, and never accustomed to any order or policy, but you witnessed sensible things and derived imaginings from them. You will be able to doubt these premises or withhold judgment, but you cannot withhold judgment on our statement: affirmation and negation cannot both be true at once, and two is more than one. So these premises, being close to truth but liable to falsehood, are not suitable for demonstrations that require certainty, but are suitable for juristic matters.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of common moral and customary judgments. Accepted and Probable Premises: Maqbulat and Maznunat
Defines maqbulat and maznunat and their role in juristic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish maqbulat from maznunat and identify their role in juristic reasoning.

Core Idea
Accepted and probable premises are the backbone of juristic inference, not certain proofs.

Not every premise in Islamic reasoning is certain. Al-Ghazali here distinguishes between premises that we accept from trustworthy sources (maqbulat) and those that are merely probable (maznunat). Understanding their definitions and degrees of probability helps you evaluate the strength of arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage and list the two types of premises: maqbulat (المقبولات) and maznunat (المظنونات). Note where each definition begins.
The passage clearly marks them as 'الصنف الثاني' and 'الصنف الثالث'.
2
Extract the definition of maqbulat
Maqbulat are beliefs we hold because of an informant—either a group less than tawatur or a single person with clear rectitude or abundant knowledge. Examples include fathers, teachers, imams, and isolated hadith reports (akhbar al-ahad).
Contrast this with tawatur, which yields certainty.
3
Extract the definition of maznunat
Maznunat are beliefs where the soul inclines toward one side but acknowledges the possibility of the opposite. The example: 'So-and-so only goes out at night for suspicion.'
Maznunat are not stable; they can change upon reflection.
4
Compare their certainty levels
Note that both types are probabilistic (zann), not certain. Maqbulat have degrees of probability depending on the source's reliability. Maznunat are inherently less stable and can be revised.
Al-Ghazali says the degrees of probability in maqbulat are 'innumerable'.
5
Understand their usage in fiqh
Maqbulat are explicitly said to be suitable for juristic syllogisms (maqayis fiqhiyya), not for rational demonstrations (barahin 'aqliyya). Maznunat are also used in practical inference, like planning actions.
This shows that Islamic law often operates on probable premises, not just certain ones.

الصنف الثاني : المقبولات وهي أمور اعتقدناها بتصديق من أخبرنا بها من جماعة ينقص عددهم عن عدد التواتر، أو شخص واحد تميز عن غيره بعدالة ظاهرة أو علم وافر كالذي قبلناه من آبائنا وأساتيذنا وأئمتنا واستمررنا على اعتقاده، وكأخبار الآحاد في الشرع، فهي تصلح للمقاييس الفقهية دون البراهين العقلية، ولها في إثارة الظن مراتب لا تكاد تخفى؛ فليس المستفيض في الكتب الصحاح من الأحاديث كالذي ينقله الواحد، ولا ما ينقله أحد الخلفاء الراشدين كما ينقله غيره، ودرجات الظن فيه لا تحصى.
الصنف الثالث : المظنونات وهي أمور يقع التصديق بها لا على الثبات بل مع خطور إمكان نقيضها بالبال ولكن النفس إليها أميل كقولنا : إن فلانا إنما يخرج بالليل لريبة، فإن النفس تميل إليه ميلا يبنى عليه التدبير للأفعال، وهي مع تشعر بإمكان نقيضه والمشهورات والمقبولات إذا أعتبرت من حيث يشعر بنقيضها في بعض الأحوال، فيجوز أن تسمى مظنونة، وكم من مشهور في بادئ الرأي يورث اعتقادا، فإن تأملته وتعقبته عاد ذلك الإذعان لقبوله ظنا أو تكذيبا كقول
The second type: accepted opinions (maqbulat), which are matters we believe due to the affirmation of one who informed us, from a group whose number is less than the number of tawatur, or from a single person who is distinguished from others by manifest rectitude or abundant knowledge, like what we accepted from our fathers, teachers, and imams and continued to believe, and like isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) in the Sharia. They are suitable for juristic syllogisms, not for rational demonstrations. They have degrees in producing probability that are hardly hidden: a widely transmitted hadith in authentic books is not like one transmitted by a single narrator, nor is what one of the Rightly Guided Caliphs narrates like what another narrates; the degrees of probability in it are innumerable. The third type: probable opinions (maznunat), which are matters that are assented to not with stability but with the possibility of their opposite occurring in the mind, yet the soul is more inclined to them. For example, our saying: 'So-and-so only goes out at night for suspicion.' The soul inclines to it with an inclination upon which planning for actions is built, even though it is aware of the possibility of its opposite. The well-known (mashhurat) and accepted opinions, if considered from the aspect that their opposite is conceivable in some cases, may be called probable. How many a common opinion initially produces belief, but if you reflect on it and examine it, that acceptance reverts to probability or denial, as in the saying...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage classifies two types of premises that fall short of certainty. The second type is maqbulat (accepted opinions): beliefs derived from authority of individuals or groups less than tawatur (mass transmission). Examples include reports from a single reliable narrator (akhbar al-ahad) or teachings from parents and scholars. These are not certain but can produce varying degrees of probability (zann). For instance, a hadith found in Sahih collections transmitted by multiple chains is stronger than a single transmission; similarly, a report from a caliph carries more weight than from an unknown person. The third type is maznunat (probable opinions): beliefs held with a leaning toward their truth but with awareness of a possible opposite. They are often used in daily inference, like judging someone's nightly outings as suspicious. Al-Ghazali notes that both mashhurat and maqbulat can also be considered maznunat when their opposites remain possible. Many initial convictions, upon scrutiny, reduce to mere probability or become rejected. Both types serve in juristic reasoning but not in demonstrations seeking certain knowledge.

Key Terms
المقبولات
المظنونات
الظن

D) Maznunat (probable opinions)

Key Takeaway
Maqbulat and maznunat are probabilistic premises that form the basis of juristic reasoning, demonstrating that fiqh often relies on probable knowledge rather than certainty.

Muqabbulat: Accepted Premises and Their Degrees of Probability
Author defines muqabbulat and explains their degrees of probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of muqabbulat and rank their degrees of probability based on the authority of the source.

Core Idea
Not all accepted premises are equal; degrees of probability matter in fiqh.

In this passage, al-Ghazali defines the second type of premises in logic: the accepted premises (muqabbulat). These are propositions we accept on authority, but unlike tawatur, they yield only probability. He emphasizes that there are varying degrees of probability, which is crucial for juristic reasoning. Understanding these distinctions will help you evaluate the strength of evidence in Islamic legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of muqabbulat
Look for the defining characteristics: accepted due to testimony of a group below tawatur or a single notable person distinguished by justice or knowledge.
The definition excludes mass-transmitted reports and self-evident truths.
2
Recognize the source categories
Note the examples: fathers, teachers, imams, and solitary hadith reports (akhbar al-ahad). These are all forms of authoritative testimony.
The category includes any report from non-tawatur sources, not only religious ones.
3
Understand the epistemic status
Muqabbulat produce zann (probability), not yaqin (certainty). They are suitable for juristic syllogisms (maqayis fiqhiyya) but not for intellectual demonstrations (barahin 'aqliyya).
This distinguishes them from certain premises like primary intelligibles or sense perceptions.
4
Distinguish degrees of probability
Al-Ghazali gives a hierarchy: a hadith widely transmitted in authentic books is stronger than one from a single narrator; a report from a Rightly Guided Caliph is stronger than from others. Degrees are numerous and not fully enumerable.
This hierarchy is based on the reliability and number of transmitters.
5
Apply the ranking to a juristic example
Consider two hadiths: one from Sahih al-Bukhari (mustafid) and one from a single unknown narrator. The former has higher probability and should be given more weight in legal inference.
Practice ranking helps internalize the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثاني : المقبولات وهي أمور اعتقدناها بتصديق من أخبرنا بها من جماعة ينقص عددهم عن عدد التواتر، أو شخص واحد تميز عن غيره بعدالة ظاهرة أو علم وافر كالذي قبلناه من آبائنا وأساتيذنا وأئمتنا واستمررنا على اعتقاده، وكأخبار الآحاد في الشرع، فهي تصلح للمقاييس الفقهية دون البراهين العقلية، ولها في إثارة الظنّ مراتب لا تكاد تخفى ؛ فليس المستفيض في الكتب الصحاح من الأحاديث كالذي ينقله الواحد، ولا ما ينقله أحد الخلفاء الراشدين كما ينقله غيره، ودرجات الظن فيه لا تُحصى.
The second type: the accepted premises (muqabbulat). These are matters we believe due to the affirmation of someone who informed us, from a group whose number is less than tawatur, or a single person distinguished from others by apparent justice or abundant knowledge, like what we accepted from our fathers, teachers, and imams and continue to believe, and like solitary reports in the Sharia. They are suitable for juristic syllogisms but not for intellectual demonstrations. They have degrees of probability that are hardly hidden: the wide-spread [hadith] in the authentic books is not like that transmitted by a single person, nor what one of the Rightly Guided Caliphs transmits is like what others transmit, and the degrees of probability therein cannot be enumerated.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of premises: al-muqabbulat (accepted premises). These are propositions we accept based on the testimony of others who are less than tawatur (mass transmission) in number, or a single person known for justice or knowledge. Examples include teachings from parents, teachers, and imams, as well as solitary hadith reports in religious law. Al-Ghazali states that such premises are suitable for juristic syllogisms (maqayis fiqhiyya) but not for intellectual demonstrations (barahin 'aqliyya) because they produce only probability, not certainty. Importantly, he notes that not all muqabbulat are equal: there are degrees of probability. A hadith widely transmitted in authentic collections is stronger than one from a single narrator, and a report from a Rightly Guided Caliph is stronger than from others. This reflects the nuanced approach of Islamic legal theory to the strength of evidence.

التواتر
أخبار الآحاد

Maznunat: Conjectural Premises and Their Shifting Nature
Author defines maznunat and shows how mashhurat can become maznunat upon reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-maznunat (conjectural premises) and explain how commonly accepted propositions (mashhurat) can become conjectural upon reflection.

Core Idea
Conjectural premises are stable enough for action, yet shift with reflection.

This passage from al-Ghazali's Mi'yar al-Ilm introduces the third type of premises: al-maznunat. These are propositions that gain assent not with certainty but with awareness of their opposite, yet the soul inclines to them. More importantly, al-Ghazali shows that even widely accepted statements (mashhurat) can become maznuna when examined closely, revealing their probabilistic nature. Understanding this shifting nature is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-maznunat (المظنونات). Note that it is the third type of premises in al-Ghazali's classification.
The term is plural; singular is maznuna.
2
Extract the definition
The author states that maznunat are matters assented to 'not with stability' but with the possibility of the opposite occurring to the mind, yet the soul inclines to them.
Key phrase: 'yattabi'u al-tasdiq biha la 'ala al-thabat'
3
Analyze the example
The example 'So-and-so only goes out at night out of suspicion' illustrates a conjecture. The soul inclines to it, but one is aware of the opposite possibility.
This shows how maznunat are used in practical reasoning (tadbir li-l-af'al).
4
Observe the shift from mashhurat to maznuna
Al-Ghazali explains that mashhurat and muqabbulat can be called maznuna if one reflects on their opposite. The saying 'Support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed' is initially accepted, but reflection reveals its opposite: the oppressor should not be supported unconditionally.
This shift demonstrates that the epistemic status of a premise can change with deeper analysis.
5
Connect to prophetic hadith
Al-Ghazali ties the reflection to the hadith where the Prophet interprets 'supporting the oppressor' as preventing his oppression. This confirms that the apparent meaning was not certain.
The hadith grounds the logical point in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصنف الثالث : المظنونات وهي أمور يقع التصديق بها لا على الثبات بل مع خطور إمكان نقيضها بالبال ولكن النفس إليها أميل كقولنا : إن فلاناً إنما يخرج بالليل لريبة، فإن النفس تميل إليه ميلاً يبنى عليه التدبير للأفعال، وهي مع تشعر بإمكان نقيضه والمشهورات والمقبولات إذا أعتبرت من حيث يشعر بنقيضها في بعض الأحوال، فيجوز أن تُسمّى مظنونة، وكم من مشهور في بادئ الرأي يورث اعتقاداً، فإن تأمّلته وتعقبته عاد ذلك الإذعان لقبوله ظنّاً أو تكذيباً كقول القائل : ينبغي أن تنصر أخاك ظالماً أو مظلوماً ؛ فهذا محمود مشهور يتسارع الذهن إلى قبوله ثم يتأمل فيتبين خلافه، وهو أن الظالم ينبغي ألا يُنصر بل ينبغي أن يُمنع ظلمه ويُنصر المظلوم عليه، وهو المراد بالحديث المعقول فيه، فإنه سُئل عن ذلك فقيل : كيف يُنصر الظالم؟ فقال : نصرته أن تمنعه من ظلمه.
The third type: the conjectural premises (al-maznunat). These are matters that are assented to not with stability but with the possibility of their opposite occurring to the mind, yet the soul inclines towards them, like our statement: 'So-and-so only goes out at night out of suspicion,' for the soul inclines to it in a way that builds action upon it, while being aware of the possibility of its opposite. Mashhurat and muqabbulat, if considered from the aspect that one is aware of their opposite in some cases, may be called maznuna. How many a mashhura initially conveys conviction, but if you ponder and reflect, that assent turns into probability or denial, like the statement of that speaker: 'You should support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed.' This is a praised, commonly-accepted statement; the mind hastens to accept it, then upon reflection its opposite becomes clear: that the oppressor should not be supported, but rather his oppression should be prevented and the oppressed supported. That is what is meant by the well-known hadith on this, for the Prophet was asked about it: 'How does one support the oppressor?' He said: 'His support is to prevent him from his oppression.'
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of premises: al-maznunat (conjectural premises). These are propositions that are believed but not with stability; one is aware of the possibility of their opposite, yet the soul leans towards them. The example given is a suspicion about someone's motives: 'So-and-so only goes out at night for a suspicious reason.' This is a conjecture because the opposite is possible. The author then notes that mashhurat and muqabbulat can also be considered maznuna if one reflects on the possibility of their opposite. He illustrates with the saying 'Support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed.' Initially, it seems praiseworthy and is commonly accepted, but upon reflection, one realizes that supporting the oppressor actually means preventing his oppression, not aiding his injustice. This shows how a commonly accepted statement can shift from seeming certain to merely probable or even false. The passage ties this to a prophetic hadith that clarifies the correct meaning. Thus, maznunat are not fixed; they can change upon examination, and they are suitable for practical reasoning.

Key Terms
المظنونات (al-maznunat)
الظن (al-zann)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Distinguishing Rational and Jurisprudential Premises
Author distinguishes rational from jurisprudential premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the types of premises suitable for rational and jurisprudential reasoning and explain how particular premises become universal in fiqh.

Core Idea
Rational and jurisprudential reasoning share the same logical form but differ in the material and criteria for universality.

The author has just enumerated ten types of premises underlying valid arguments. Now he addresses a key question: How do rational ('aqliyyat) and jurisprudential (fiqhiyyat) reasoning differ? While their logical structure is identical, they rely on different materials and different methods for ensuring universality. This distinction is essential for anyone working in Islamic legal theory or theology.

Guided Path
1
Map the ten premise types
From the passage, note that the ten premises are grouped into three sections: four from the first, three from the second (materials of fiqhiyyat), and three from the last. The first section likely contains certain premises, the second conjectural ones suitable for fiqh, and the third ambiguous/fallacious ones unsuitable for both.
The exact categories are not repeated here; they were given earlier in the text.
2
Identify the difference in material
The author states that rational premises are a subset suitable for both fields. Jurisprudential premises additionally include conjectural (zanniyyat) premises. Some premises (ambiguous, fallacious) are unsuitable for both.
3
Understand universalization in fiqh
In rational matters, a premise must be self-evident or mutawatir to be universal. In fiqh, particular premises (like a single Companion's statement) can be treated as universal by relying on the authority of the Lawgiver, consensus, or other evidence detailed in usul al-fiqh.
The Quranic example 'three days in the Hajj' is clear in wording and mutawatir in transmission, giving it certainty.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. This is known from explicit Quran and mutawatir hadith, so it works in both rational and jurisprudential reasoning. But 'A single hadith from a trustworthy narrator is binding' is a premise that works in fiqh but not in rational demonstration because it is merely probable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن مجموع ما ذكرناه من أصناف هذه المقدمات التي سميناها عشرة: أربعة من القسم الأول، وثلاثة من القسم الثاني، وهي مواد الفقهيات، وثلاثة من القسم الأخير، وقد ذكرنا حكمها. فإن قال قائل: فبماذا تخالف العقليات الفقهيات؟ قلنا: لا مخالفة بينهما في صورة القياس وإنما يتخالفان في المادة، ولا في كل مادة، بل ما يصلح أن يكون مقدمة في العقليات يصلح للفقهيات، ولكن قد يصلح للفقهيات ما لا يصلح للعقليات كالظنيات، وقد يؤخذ ما لا يصلح لهما جميعا كالمشبهات والمغلطات. كما يتخالفان في كيفية ما به تصير المقدمة كلية، فإن المقدمات الجزئية في الفقه يتسامح بجعلها كلية، وإنما يدرك ذلك أقوال صاحب الشرع وأفعاله وأقوال أهل الإجماع، وأقوال آحاد الصحابة، إن رؤي ذلك حجة على ما يستقصى في أصول الفقه، والجاري منها مجرى الأوليات من العقليات ما هو صريح في لفظه بين في طريقه، كاللفظ الصريح المسموع من الشارع أو المنقول بطريق التواتر، فإن المتواتر كالمسموع. فقوله: ﴿ ثلاثة أيام في الحج ﴾ صريح في لفظه، أعني: كونه عشرة بين في طريقه، أعني: التواتر.
Thus the total of what we have mentioned from the types of these premises that we called ten: four from the first section, three from the second section, and these are the materials of jurisprudential matters, and three from the last section, and we have mentioned their ruling. So if someone says: 'How do rational matters differ from jurisprudential matters?' We say: There is no difference between them in the form of the syllogism; rather they differ in the material, and not in every material, but what is suitable to be a premise in rational matters is suitable for jurisprudential matters, but what is suitable for jurisprudential matters may not be suitable for rational matters, like conjectural premises. And sometimes what is not suitable for both is taken, like ambiguous and fallacious premises. Also they differ in the manner by which a premise becomes universal, for particular premises in fiqh are tolerated to be made universal, and that is understood from the statements of the Lawgiver and His actions and the statements of those of consensus, and the statements of individual Companions, if that is seen as a proof—as will be investigated in usul al-fiqh. And that which among them runs in the course of self-evident rational premises is what is clear in its wording and evident in its transmission, like an explicit wording heard from the Lawgiver or transmitted by tawatur, for the mutawatir is like heard. So His saying: 'Three days in the Hajj' is clear in its wording, meaning: it is ten, evident in its transmission, meaning: tawatur.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage summarizes the classification of premises into ten types (four from first section, three from second, three from last) and then addresses the difference between rational and jurisprudential reasoning. The author emphasizes that they share the same syllogistic form; the difference lies in the material (the content of premises). Some premises (e.g., certain ones) can be used in both, but jurisprudential matters allow conjectural premises that would not suffice for rational proofs. Also, the method of universalization differs: in fiqh, particular premises (like specific reports) can be treated as universal through authoritative sources (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, etc.), whereas in rational matters, only self-evident or mutawatir premises are universally valid. The example given is the Quranic phrase 'three days in the Hajj' which is clear in meaning and transmitted by tawatur, giving it certainty akin to hearing it directly.

Key Terms
العقليات
الفقهيات
المتواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Rational and jurisprudential reasoning share the same logical form but differ in the material of premises and the criteria for universalization: fiqh allows conjectural premises and treats particular reports as universal through textual authority.

Material Difference: Rational vs Juristic Premises
Author distinguishes rational and juristic premises in material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which kinds of premises are acceptable in rational sciences versus juristic sciences based on their material (madda).

Core Idea
Rational premises reject probability; juristic premises embrace it – but both use the same syllogistic engine.

The author begins by addressing a common question: do rational and juristic arguments differ in their logical form? He answers with a clear 'no'. The difference is solely in the material – the content and certainty level of the premises. In this lesson, we examine what types of premises are shared and which are exclusive to each domain.

Guided Path
1
Recognize premise categories
Identify the three groups mentioned: first division (certainties), second division with three juristic materials, and third division (ambiguous/fallacious).
The author references earlier categorizations without naming them here.
2
Understand shared premises
Some premises are suitable for both rational and juristic syllogisms. The author says 'what is suitable to be a premise in rational matters is suitable for juristic matters'.
This shows overlap in the material domain.
3
Identify exclusive juristic premises
Presumptions (zanniyyat) are accepted in fiqh but not in rational demonstration. Why? Because rational matters demand certainty.
This is the key difference in material.
4
Identify excluded premises
Ambiguous (mutashabihat) and fallacious (mughalitat) premises are not suitable for either domain. They corrupt reasoning.
These are mentioned but not elaborated.
5
Summarize the material distinction
The material determines the domain. Some materials are common, some are fiqh-specific, some are useless for both.

فإذن مجموع ما ذكرناه من من القسم الأول، وثلاثة من القسم الثاني، وهي مواد الفقهيات، وثلاثة من القسم الأخير، وقد ذكرنا حكمها فإن قال قائل : فبماذا تخالف العقليات الفقهيات؟ قلنا : لا مخالفة بينهما في صورة القياس وإنما يتخالفان في المادة ولا في كل مادة، بل ما يصلح أن يكون مقدمة في العقليات يصلح للفقهيات، ولكن قد يصلح للفقهيات ما لا يصلح للعقليات كالظنيات، وقد يؤخذ ما لا يصلح لهما جميعاً كالمشبهات والمغلطات. كما يتخالفان في كيفية ما به تصير المقدمة كلية، فإن المقدمات الجزئية في الفقه يتسامح بجعلها كلية، وإنما يدرك ذلك أقوال من صاحب الشرع وأفعاله وأقوال أهل الإجماع، وأقوال آحاد الصحابة، إن رؤي ذلك حجّة على ما يستقصى في أصول الفقه والجاري منها مجرى الأوليات من العقليات ما هو صريح في لفظه بيّن في طريقه كاللفظ الصريح المسموع من الشارع أو المنقول بطريق التواتر، فإن المتواتر كالمسموع. فقوله: ﴿ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ فِي الْحَجِّ وَسَبْعَةٍ إِذَا رَجَعْتُمْ ﴾ صريح في لفظه، أعني : كونه عشرة بين في طريقه، أعني القرآن متواتر، وقد يكون بيناً في طريقه ظاهراً في لفظه كالمراد من قوله : إذا رجعتم . وقد يكون صريحاً في لفظه غير بين في طريقه كالنص الذي ينقله الآحاد من لفظ صاحب الشرع، وقد يكون عادماً للقوتين كالظاهر الذي ينقله الآحاد .
Thus the total of what we mentioned: [some] from the first section, and three from the second section, which are the materials of the juristic matters, and three from the last section, and we have mentioned their ruling. If someone says: 'How do rational matters differ from juristic matters?' We say: There is no difference between them in the form of the syllogism; they only differ in material, and not in every material. Rather, what is suitable to be a premise in rational matters is suitable for juristic matters, but what is suitable for juristic matters may not be suitable for rational matters, such as presumptions. And sometimes what is not suitable for both is taken, such as ambiguous and fallacious things. They also differ in how the premise becomes universal, because particular premises in jurisprudence are tolerantly made universal. That is understood from the sayings of the Lawgiver, His actions, the sayings of the people of consensus, and the sayings of individual Companions, if that is seen as proof, as detailed in Usul al-Fiqh. What runs in the course of primary rational matters is that which is explicit in its wording and clear in its transmission, like the explicit statement heard from the Lawgiver or transmitted by tawatur, for the mutawatir is like the heard. His saying: 'Three days during Hajj and seven when you return' is explicit in its wording—I mean: it being ten is clear in its transmission, I mean: the Qur'an is mutawatir. And it may be clear in its transmission and apparent in its wording, like what is intended by His saying 'when you return'. And it may be explicit in its wording but not clear in its transmission, like a text transmitted by individuals from the Lawgiver's wording. And it may lack both qualities, like the apparent [text] transmitted by individuals.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses the distinction between rational ('aqliyyat) and juristic (fiqhiyyat) premises. The author first notes that the total number of premise types mentioned earlier (in the book) are ten: some from the first division (likely certainties), three from the second (juristic materials), and three from the last (fallacious and ambiguous). He then raises a question: How do rational and juristic matters differ? The answer: They do not differ in the form of the syllogism (sura al-qiyas)—both use the same logical structure—but they differ in their matter (madda), and not in every respect; some premises are common. However, juristic premises can include weaker types like presumptions (zanniyyat), which are not acceptable in rational sciences. Also, both may use premises that are unsuitable for either, like ambiguous or fallacious ones. A key difference is how a premise becomes universal (kulliyya). In jurisprudence, particular premises are tolerantly treated as universal; this is derived from the sources: the Lawgiver's statements and actions, consensus, and sayings of Companions. In rational matters, universality requires explicitness in wording and clarity in transmission—like a clear statement from the Lawgiver or a mutawatir report (which is equivalent to hearing it directly). The author then analyzes a Qur'anic verse (2:196) as an example: 'Three days during Hajj and seven when you return'—the wording explicitly says 'three' and 'seven', and the transmission (the Qur'an) is mutawatir, so it is both clear in wording and transmission. He contrasts this with cases where the wording is explicit but transmission is not (e.g., an ahad hadith) or where transmission is clear but wording is not, or where both are lacking (a 'zahir' text transmitted by individuals). This classification is important for understanding how premises are treated in fiqh vs. pure logic.

Universality of Premises: Rational vs Juristic Standards
Author explains how premises become universal differently in rational and juristic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how particular premises become universal in jurisprudence and contrast this with the rational requirement of explicit wording and clear transmission.

Core Idea
In fiqh, a single companion's saying can stand as a universal premise; in logic, only the mutawatir or the explicit is universal.

Having established that the difference is in material, the author now turns to a deeper issue: how a particular premise can be treated as universal. In rational sciences, universality demands clarity in wording and certainty in transmission. In jurisprudence, there is tolerance: particular statements from the Lawgiver, consensus, or even a single companion are elevated to universal status. This lesson explores the four-fold classification of texts based on wording and transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria for universality
The author mentions two criteria: explicitness in wording (sarahat al-lafz) and clarity in transmission (bayān al-tarīq). Both are needed for a premise to be fully universal in rational sciences.
2
Understand the rational standard
In rational matters, only statements that are explicit in wording and transmitted by tawatur qualify as universal. The mutawatir is treated like direct hearing.
This is the strictest standard.
3
Understand the juristic tolerance
In fiqh, particular premises from authoritative sources (Lawgiver's sayings and actions, consensus, companion sayings) are treated as universal even if they do not meet both criteria. The author says 'they are tolerantly made universal'.
This is a pragmatic concession for deriving rulings.
4
Analyse the example
The Quranic verse 2:196 is given: 'Three days during Hajj and seven when you return'. It is explicit in wording (explicit numbers) and clear in transmission (Qur'an is mutawatir). So it meets both criteria.
The verse is an example of a premise that works for both rational and juristic matters.
5
Apply the four-fold classification
The author classifies texts into: (i) explicit wording + clear transmission (Qur'an), (ii) clear transmission + apparent wording (verse's 'when you return' part), (iii) explicit wording + unclear transmission (khabar wahid), (iv) neither (zahir transmitted by individuals).
This classification helps determine which premises can be used in rational vs juristic arguments.

Four Types of Shari'ah Terms in Universal and Particular Propositions
Author classifies shari'ah terms into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any shari'ah term into one of the four types based on its wording (universal/particular) and its intended scope (universal/particular).

Core Idea
A shari'ah term's wording may differ from its intended scope, creating four logical types.

When interpreting Islamic legal texts, it is crucial to distinguish between the literal wording of a term and its intended application. The author presents a classification of shari'ah terms into four types based on whether the wording is universal or particular and whether the intended meaning is universal or particular. This classification helps you avoid errors when applying general and specific expressions in fiqh arguments. By mastering these types, you will be able to analyze legal texts more precisely and construct sound juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording type
Determine if the term's wording is universal (e.g., 'every', 'all') or particular (e.g., 'these two', 'a dinar'). Look for explicit quantifiers or context that indicates generality or specificity.
Universal wording includes words like كُلّ ('every') or general nouns without restriction. Particular wording may be a proper noun, a limited phrase, or a pronoun referring to a specific group.
2
Identify the intended scope
Ask: Does the legislator intend the term to apply to all individuals of its kind, or only to a subset? Consider textual evidence (e.g., qualifying conditions, other verses/hadith) that indicate restriction or expansion.
For example, the phrase 'pastured sheep' in the zakat hadith is universal in wording but intended only for sheep that reach the nisab.
3
Map to one of the four types
Combine the two dimensions: (1) universal wording intended as universal, (2) particular wording intended as particular, (3) universal wording intended as particular, (4) particular wording intended as universal. Match your findings to the correct type.
Use the author's examples as reference: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is type 1; 'These two are forbidden for males' is type 2; 'On pastured sheep is zakat' is type 3; 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good' is type 4.
4
Apply the classification to fiqh reasoning
Once classified, use the type to determine how the term functions in legal argument. For type 3 (universal intended as particular), the author advises against converting it into a syllogism with added conditions; instead, stick to the general wording and let the opponent bring evidence for specification.
This step connects the logical classification to the practical rules of juristic dialectic (jadal al-fiqh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الألفاظ الشرعية في القضية الكلية والجزئية أربعة أقسام : الأول : كلية أريد بها كلّية كقوله : كل مسكر حرام . الثاني : جزئية بقيت جزئية كقوله في الذهب والإبريسم : «هذان حرامان على ذكور أمتي» فإنه بقي مختصاً بالذكور ولم يتعد إلى الإناث . والثالث : كلّية أريد بها جزئية كقوله : «في سائمة الغنم زكاة»، أريد بها ما بلغ نصاباً. وقوله: ﴿ وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا ﴾ المراد به بعض السارقين، فإذا أردنا أن نجعل هذه كلية ضممنا إليها الأوصاف التي بان اعتبارها فيه وقلنا مثلاً كلّ من سرق نصاباً كاملاً من حرز مثله لا شبهة له فيه قطع . والنباش أو الذي يسرق الأشياء الرطبة مثلاً بهذه الصفة فيقطع ؛ هذه العادة . والصواب عندنا في مراسم جدل الفقه أن لا يفعل ذلك مهما وجد عموم لفظ، بل اللفظ ويتعلق الخصم بالمخصص، وما يدعى من يتطرّق إلى العموم فليس مانعاً من التمسك بالعموم على اصطلاح الفقهاء، وإذا اصطلحوا على هذا فالتمسك به أولى من إيراده في شكل قياس، لأنهم ليسوا يقبلون تخصيص العلة. ومهما قلت كل من سرق نصاباً كاملاً من حرز مثله قطع، منع الخصم وقال : أهملت وصفاً وهو أن لا يكون المسروق رطباً فما الذي عرّفك أن هذا غير معتبر، فلا يبقى لك إلا أن تعود إلى العموم وتقول: هو الأصل. ومن زاد وصفاً فعليه الدليل إذن التمسك بالعموم أولى إذا وجد. والرابع : هو الجزئي الذي أريد به الكلّي، فإنَّا كما نعبر بالعام عن الخاص فنقول : ليس في الأصدقاء خير، ونرید به بعضهم كذلك قد يطلق الخاص ونريد به العام كقوله تعالى : وَمِنْهُم مَّنْ إِن تَأْمَنْهُ بِدِينَارٍ لَا يُؤَدِّهِ إِلَيْكَ فإنه يراد به سائر أنواع أمواله. وكقوله : فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَمُ فيعبر بالقليل عن الكثير.
The totality of shari'ah terms in universal and particular propositions are four divisions: First: a universal intended as universal, like his saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Second: a particular remaining particular, like his saying regarding gold and silk: 'These two are forbidden for the males of my ummah', for it remained specific to males and did not extend to females. Third: a universal intended as particular, like his saying: 'On pastured sheep is zakat'—it was intended for what reaches the nisab. And His saying: 'The male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands'—what is intended is some thieves. So if we want to make these universal, we add to them the descriptions that validate their consideration, and we say, for example: 'Every one who steals a complete nisab from a secure place like it, with no doubt for him, is cut.' The grave robber or one who steals moist things, for example, with this description, is cut; this is the custom. The correct view according to us in the conventions of juristic dialectic is not to do that as long as a general wording is found, but rather [one adheres to] the wording, and the opponent is required to produce a specific restriction. And what is claimed of [a possibility] that could apply to the general is not a preventive from adhering to the general according to the convention of jurists. And if they have agreed on this, then adhering to the general is better than presenting it in the form of a syllogism, because they do not accept specification of the cause. And whenever you say 'Every one who steals a complete nisab from a secure place like it is cut', the opponent objects and says: 'You omitted a description, namely that the stolen item should not be moist; so what informs you that this is not considered?' Then you have no recourse but to return to the general and say: 'It is the principle.' And whoever adds a description must provide evidence. Therefore, adhering to the general is better if it exists. Fourth: the particular intended as universal, for just as we express the general intending the specific, we say: 'There is no good among friends', intending some of them; likewise, the specific may be uttered intending the general, as His saying: 'Among them is one who, if you entrust him with a dinar, he will not repay it to you'—what is intended is all types of his wealth. And as His saying: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it'—he expresses a little [to mean] much.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of shari'ah (Islamic legal) expressions in terms of whether they are universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i) in wording and in intended meaning. The author lists four divisions. 

First: A universal wording intended as universal, e.g., 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden'—this applies to all intoxicants without exception. 

Second: A particular wording intended as particular, e.g., the hadith 'These two (gold and silk) are forbidden for the males of my ummah'—it remains specific to males and does not extend to females. 

Third: A universal wording intended as particular, e.g., 'On pastured sheep is zakat'—the term 'sheep' is general, but the intended meaning is only those sheep that reach the nisab (minimum threshold). Similarly, the Quranic verse 'The male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands'—the word 'thief' is general, but it is understood to apply only to those who meet specific conditions (e.g., stealing a nisab, from a secure place, etc.). The author explains that if one wants to make such a universal into a universal premise in a syllogism, one must add the restricting conditions. However, he states the preferred method in juristic dialectic (jadal al-fiqh) is not to convert such expressions into syllogistic form as long as the general wording exists. Instead, one adheres to the general wording and requires the opponent to produce evidence for specification. The possibility of specification does not prevent adherence to the general according to juristic convention. He gives a hypothetical debate: if someone says 'Every thief who steals a nisab from a secure place is cut,' the opponent might object that he omitted a condition (e.g., that the item not be moist or something). Then the only recourse is to return to the general wording and assert that the general is the principle; whoever adds a condition bears the burden of proof. Thus, adhering to the general is better. 

Fourth: A particular wording intended as universal. Just as we may use a general expression when we mean a specific instance (e.g., 'There is no good among friends' intending some friends), we may also use a specific expression when we mean the whole class. Examples: The Quranic verse 'Among them is one who, if you entrust him with a dinar, he will not repay it'—the mention of 'dinar' is specific, but it refers to all types of wealth. Similarly, 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good shall see it'—the atom's weight is specific but indicates any amount, emphasizing that even the smallest good deed is seen. This classification is essential for understanding how to interpret legal texts and construct valid arguments in fiqh.

القضية الجزئية
النصاب
التخصيص

Key Takeaway
A shari'ah term's literal wording may be universal or particular, but its intended legal scope may differ; identifying the correct type is foundational for accurate legal interpretation and argumentation.
